Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Istihsan for Slander to Oppressive Sultan
Applying istihsan to override qiyas for slander to oppressive ruler.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan to override qiyas in cases of slander (si'aya) to oppressive rulers, and identify the conditions that trigger this exception.

Core Idea
Istihsan allows liability for slanderers despite qiyas, to prevent widespread harm when victims cannot recover from the oppressor.

This passage concludes the discussion of real cause with a practical problem: slanderers who go to oppressive rulers to get victims' property seized. By strict qiyas (analogy) to the case of indication to a thief, the slanderer would not be liable because the ruler's free choice intervenes. However, the later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirin) ruled by istihsan that the slanderer must guarantee the property. This lesson explores the rationale: to prevent great harm to Muslims when slanderers are prevalent and victims have no other recourse.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule
Remember that a pure cause (indication to thief) does not create liability. Qiyas would extend this to slander to a ruler: the ruler's choice breaks causation.
2
Identify the problem
The passage notes that slanderers (su'at) are prevalent in their time. If they are not liable, victims cannot recover because the oppressive ruler will not give back the property.
3
Understand istihsan
Istihsan is a Hanafi principle that overrides qiyas when strict analogy leads to hardship or harm. Here, istihsan imposes liability to protect Muslims.
4
Analyze the thamarah
The practical difference: without istihsan, slanderers go unpunished financially; with istihsan, they must compensate the victim.
5
Apply to modern contexts
Consider other situations where istihsan might override qiyas to prevent public harm, such as false reporting to authorities.

And if it is not attributed to him (i.e., the cause is not added to him), it is the real cause, like indication (dalala) of a Muslim's property to a thief, so he steals after the indication, so the indicator does not guarantee the stolen property, because the indicator is not like the free chooser (al-fa'il al-mukhtar), since indication does not necessitate theft, for it is from the thief's choice and his choice is not attributed to the indication. So the theft is not attributed to the indicator in any way, so he does not guarantee. And for that reason (i.e., because the indicator is not like the free chooser), he does not share in the spoils of war from one who indicates a fortress to the Muslim army and does not go with the mujahideen; rather the army fought by themselves, conquered, and gained booty. The indicator has no right in it because he is a pure cause (sabab mahd) to which the conquest is not attributed at all. This is unlike the depositary (mudi') and the muhrim if they indicate a thief and a hunter (respectively) upon a deposit and game, so the thief steals the deposit and the hunter kills the game, where they (the depositary and muhrim) guarantee. The depositary guarantees the stolen deposit and the muhrim guarantees the killed game. That is because the indication of the deposit to the thief and of the game to the hunter constitutes abandoning the safeguarding of the deposit and removing security from the game, and they had undertaken that. As for the depositary, by the deposit; and as for the muhrim, by the ihram. So each is a direct causer (mubashir) of the offense leading to destruction, so guarantee is obligatory. Unlike the game of the Haram (sanctuary) which a man indicated to a hunter and the indicator is not a muhrim, because its security was by the place and was not removed by the indication, so no offense leading to destruction is found from him. And it might be said: Then the muhrim who indicates should not guarantee, due to the generality of the evidence. The answer is that the muhrim's indication is an offense against ihram, so the penalty is obligatory, not because it is an offense against the game of the Haram. And the muhrim's offense regarding his game absolutely is an offense necessitating guarantee. So what is objected does not apply, as indicated by the author in the margin saying: "There is something in it." (And it was objected that the outsider (ajnabi) who indicates to a thief undertook by the contract of Islam not to indicate a thief, just as the depositary undertook by the deposit and the muhrim by ihram, and he abandoned this undertaking and led to destruction of protected property. So the indicator should guarantee there, running the evidence of the indicating muhrim and depositary. It was answered: Islam is an undertaking of the truth of what the Prophet (peace be upon him) and his family and companions brought, in general, not a particular undertaking of not indicating this property. So there is a necessity of safeguarding by Islam, not an undertaking. The obligating factor is this undertaking, unlike the depositary when he deposits, for it is clear that he undertook safeguarding, otherwise he would not have deposited. Similarly the muhrim. And if it is granted that there is safeguarding in Islam, then it is an undertaking with Allah, i.e., it is an undertaking with Allah, not with the servant. So the indication is an offense against Allah, so the penalty is obligatory from Him, and nothing is obligatory for the servant. (So sin follows, not guarantee. If you say: Then the slanderer to the oppressive sultan should not guarantee when he takes money unjustly through his slander, yet they gave fatwa that he guarantees. He said: The fatwa of the later scholars is that the slanderer to the oppressive sultan guarantees when he takes money oppressively, contrary to analogy, for its requirement is what was mentioned, by way of istihsan due to the prevalence of slanderers to oppressors in our time. For if they are not obligated to guarantee, the victim cannot take from the taker (the sultan) to recover the money, and the Muslims suffer great harm.
Source pages p. 357

Commentary
This passage explains the distinction between a real cause (sabab haqiqi) and a cause that is in the meaning of effective cause (sabab fi ma'na al-illa). A real cause is one that does not directly lead to the harm; the effect is not attributed to it because there is an intervening free choice. Example: indicating a Muslim's property to a thief. The indicator is not liable because the thief chooses to steal. Similarly, indicating a fortress to the Muslim army does not entitle the indicator to a share of booty. However, there are exceptions: the depositary (mudi') who indicates a thief to the deposit, and the muhrim (one in ihram) who indicates game to a hunter. These are liable because they had undertaken safeguarding (the depositary) or removed security (the muhrim), and their indication is a direct offense leading to destruction. The passage then addresses an objection: Why is the ordinary Muslim (ajnabi) who indicates a thief not liable, even though he has undertaken by Islam not to harm others? The answer: Islam's undertaking is general, not specific to that property. The depositary's undertaking is specific and contractual. Moreover, the Muslim's obligation is with Allah, not with the servant, so only sin results, not financial guarantee. The passage then discusses a practical problem: slander to an oppressive sultan. According to qiyas (analogy), the slanderer should not guarantee, because he is a pure cause. But the later Hanafi scholars (muta'akhkhirin) issued a fatwa of liability by istihsan, due to the widespread harm from slanderers in their time. Without such liability, victims would have no recourse, causing great harm to Muslims. This illustrates how istihsan can override qiyas to prevent public harm.

Key Terms
السعاية (al-si'aya): Slander or backbiting, especially to an oppressor
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): A Hanafi principle that sets aside a clear qiyas in favor of a stronger evidence or a maslahah
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogy: extending a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') based on shared effective cause (illah)
المتأخرون (al-muta'akhkhirun): The later Hanafi scholars, post-5th century AH

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why did later Hanafi scholars rule that a slanderer to an oppressive sultan must guarantee the property, contrary to qiyas?
A) Because the sultan is considered a direct agent of the slanderer
B) Because of istihsan due to prevalence of slanderers and harm to Muslims
C) Because the slanderer is considered a partner in theft
D) Because of a new Quranic verse

Key Takeaway
Istihsan allows Hanafi scholars to depart from qiyas when strict analogy leads to social harm, as shown by the fatwa against slanderers to oppressive rulers.

The Metaphorical Sabab in Ta'liq and Its Difference from 'Illa
Explanation of sabab as metaphor and Hanafi-Zufar dispute on ta'liq survival.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi concept of metaphorical sabab (ta'liq) from real 'illa and apply the rule regarding survival of the mahall.

Core Idea
A suspended condition is not a true cause; it only becomes a real 'illa upon condition fulfilment, and the subject must survive.

In Hanafi usul, the term 'sabab' is used metaphorically for suspended conditions (ta'liq) such as 'if you enter, you are divorced.' This passage clarifies that such a condition is not a true cause but acts like one once the condition occurs, and discusses the disagreement with Imam Zufar about whether the ta'liq survives if the subject (marriage) is lost.

Guided Path
1
Identify key terms
Understand the meanings of sabab haqiqi, sabab majazi, 'illa, ta'liq, and mahall as used in the passage.
Sabab majazi is a metaphor; 'illa is the effective cause.
2
Understand Hanafi position on ta'liq
The Hanafis treat ta'liq as a metaphorical sabab that, when the condition occurs, becomes a true 'illa. Until then it prevents causality.
Ta'liq is like an oath (yamin) – the consequence follows upon violation.
3
Note the dispute with Zufar
Imam Zufar holds that the ta'liq has no resemblance to a real cause and therefore does not require the mahall to survive.
Zufar's view is a minority within the Hanafi school.
4
Apply the rule: loss of mahall
If the mahall (e.g., marriage) is lost – for example, by already performing three divorces – the ta'liq is invalidated according to the Hanafis.
This is the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf) of the dispute.
5
Summarize the thamarah al-khilaf
The practical difference: if a husband makes a suspended divorce and then immediately gives three divorces, the suspension is void (Hanafi) or remains (Zufar).
The author states 'the truth is with the Hanafis'.

وقد يطلق السبب مجازاً على تعليق الطلاق ونحوه كالاعتاق والنذر لأنه غير مفض إلى الوقوع، بل مانع المعلق بالشرط عن السببية فإذن ليس هو سبباً حقيقة (وإنما له نوع مر أن التعليق يمنع إفضاء ولو بعد حين) ولذا يسمى به مجازاً فإذا تحقق الشرط صار علة حقيقة مؤثرة في الحكم (بخلاف السبب في معنى العلة لأنه لم يؤثر في الحكم وإن أثر في علته فافترقا) وتميزا من هذا الوجه (ثم هذا المجاز كالعلة الحقيقية عند الحنفية فإنه يمين وهو انعقد للبر بأن يترتب الجزاء لو خالف، فلا يبقى إلا إذا صلح لأن يترتب عليه الجزاء ظاهراً، كما هو حكم العلة الحقيقية (فلا يبقى) التعليق (إلا) ببقاء المحل ويفوت بفوات المحل خلافاً لزفر) الإمام، فعنده ليس له شبه بالحقيقة أصلاً، ولا ينتظر بقاءه إلى بقاء المحل ( وثمرته) أي ثمرة هذا الخلاف (أن تنجيز) الطلقات الثلاث بعد التعليق مبطل له أي للتعليق (عندهم) لفوات المحل (خلافاً له وقد مر في هو الحق) فارجع هناك
And sabab may be used metaphorically for the ta'liq of divorce and similar like manumission and vow, because it does not lead to occurrence, rather the mu'allaq bihi (suspended condition) prevents causality. So it is not a true sabab. (But it has a kind of prevention: the ta'liq prevents leading even after a time). Hence it is called metaphorically. When the condition is realized, it becomes a true 'illa effective in the ruling. (Unlike sabab in the sense of 'illa because it did not affect the ruling, though it affected its cause, so they differ and are distinguished from this aspect). (Then this metaphorical sabab is like a real 'illa according to the Hanafis, for it is a yamin (oath) that is concluded for piety, so that the consequence follows if he violates, so it remains only as long as it is fit for the consequence to follow apparently, as is the rule of a real 'illa. So the ta'liq only remains with the survival of the subject (mahall) and is lost with the loss of the subject, contrary to Zufar. According to him, it has no similarity to reality at all, and its survival is not awaited to the survival of the subject. The fruit of this disagreement is that the execution (tanjiz) of three divorces after the ta'liq invalidates the ta'liq according to them (the Hanafis) due to loss of the subject, contrary to him (Zufar). And it has passed that the truth is with them, so refer there.
Source pages p. 358

Commentary
This passage defines the technical use of 'sabab' (cause) when applied to suspended conditions like 'if you enter the house, you are divorced'. Such a condition is not a true cause (sabab haqiqi) because it does not lead to the effect until the condition occurs; rather it prevents the mu'allaq (the divorce) from being caused. It is called sabab metaphorically. When the condition occurs, it becomes a true 'illa (effective cause) for the ruling. The Hanafis treat this metaphorical sabab like a real cause in that it acts as an oath (yamin) that obliges the consequence if violated, provided the subject (mahall, e.g., the marriage) still exists. If the subject is lost (e.g., by already divorcing three times), the ta'liq is void. Imam Zufar disagreed: for him the ta'liq remains even after the subject is lost, because it has no resemblance to a real cause. The practical consequence is that if someone makes a suspended divorce and then immediately gives three divorces (tanjiz), the ta'liq is invalidated according to the Hanafis (due to loss of mahall), but not according to Zufar.

Key Terms
سبب (sabab): A cause that leads to an effect; here used metaphorically for the suspended condition that prevents the effect until it occurs.
تعليق (ta'liq): A suspension of a legal consequence (like divorce) on the occurrence of a future condition.
محل (mahall): The subject or locus of the ruling; here the marriage that can be divorced.
زفر (Zufar): Imam Zufar ibn al-Hudhayl, a Hanafi jurist known for his independent opinions.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What happens according to the Hanafis if someone makes a suspended divorce (ta'liq) and then executes three divorces (tanjiz) before the condition occurs?
A) The suspended divorce remains valid
B) The suspended divorce is invalidated because the subject (mahall) is lost
C) Both are valid
D) The tanjiz is invalid

Key Takeaway
A suspended divorce condition is a metaphorical sabab that requires the subject to exist; loss of the subject invalidates the ta'liq.

Distinction Between Condition and Cause in Liability
Explains the legal distinction between condition and cause, and liability of witnesses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between a pure condition (shart mahd) and a condition with the meaning of a cause (shart fi ma'na al-'illa) in Hanafi usul, and apply this distinction to determine liability in witness retraction cases.

Core Idea
A condition without causality does not attract liability; only the direct cause or a condition with causal meaning does.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut delves into the subtle distinction between a condition (shart) and a cause ('illa) in legal causation, a cornerstone of Hanafi usul al-fiqh. It uses concrete examples like slitting a waterskin and digging a well to illustrate how liability attaches to the removal of a barrier rather than the pure condition itself. The discussion culminates in a nuanced intra-Hanafi dispute over whether witnesses to a condition in a conditional manumission case are liable when they retract after judgment, testing your ability to apply the shart-illa framework.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish pure condition from condition with causal meaning
Identify the two types of conditions: a pure condition (shart mahd) has no causal element; it merely removes a barrier. A condition with the meaning of a cause (shart fi ma'na al-'illa) is treated like a cause in attribution of rulings.
The waterskin and well examples illustrate pure conditions; the act of slitting or digging does not directly cause the destruction but removes a barrier.
2
Analyze the waterskin and well examples
In the waterskin example, slitting is a condition for the water to flow; the direct cause is the natural flow. Liability falls on the slitter because he wrongfully removed the barrier. Similarly, digging a well in the road is a condition; liability attaches if the digger acted wrongfully.
The key is transgression (ta'addi) in removing the barrier; without it, no liability.
3
Apply the distinction to the witness scenario
In a conditional manumission case, the oath is the cause ('illa) of the ruling, and entering the house is the condition (shart). When witnesses retract after judgment, the cause (oath) is the basis for the judgment, so liability is on the oath witnesses. The condition witnesses are not directly liable.
The judgment is attributed to the cause, not the condition.
4
Evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute on condition witness liability
Fakhr al-Islam holds condition witnesses liable because they testified falsely (transgression). Sarakhsi and Ibn al-Humam deny liability, arguing the oath (cause) as a legitimate act cuts off attribution from the condition. Analyze the author's critique: if that were true, all retracting witnesses would be immune, which is false by consensus.
The author leans toward Fakhr al-Islam's view (al-mukhtar).

المالك
المشهود عليه، فرجع شهود الإحصان فقط، فينبغي أن يضمنوا الدية لوجود التعدي منهم (لأن الزنا علة صالحة لإضافة (الحد وتنقطع الإضافة عن الشرط والأمارة عند وجودها، والإحصان شرط أو أمارة ولا يذهب عليك أن الزنا ليس سبباً صالحاً لإيجاب الرجم إلا حال الإحصان فالتعدي منهم فافهم منكرو الضمان (قالوا : العلة ههنا (وإن لم تكن صالحة لإيجاب الضمان صالحة لقطعه عن الشرط إذا كانت فعل فاعل (مختار فلا يضاف إليه ما دامت موجودة، فلا يضمن الشهود (أقول) في الجواب : ماذا أرادوا بالعلة؟ إن أريد به القضاء) فحينئذ جعلوه فعل فاعل مختار قاطع النسبة عن الشرط (كما في التحرير والتوضيح فبعد أنه علة الحكم بالوجود) أي وجود الشرط (لا علة الهلاك فيه أن المجبور شرعاً كالمجبور (طبعاً) فهو بمنزلة المكره (فصار كالواقع في البئر وهو كما ترى. فالأولى أنه مؤد لما وجب وأداء الواجب لا يصلح لإضافة الضمان والجناية إليه وكيف ولو تم ما ذكروا (لزم انتفاء الضمان مطلقاً) عن الشهود (إذا رجعوا) لتخلل القضاء الذي هو فعل الفاعل المختار (وهو) باطل إجماعاً) لوجوبه على الشهود إذا رجعوا إجماعاً وإن أريد به اليمين فحينئذ يجعل هو فعل الفاعل الذي هو قاطع النسبة عن الشرط كما هو المتوهم ففيه أنه فعل مشروع لا يصلح متلفاً بالتعدي أصلاً، وأيضاً فمنقوض بقوله : إن كان قيده عشرة أرطال فهو حر، وإن حله أحد فهو حر، فشهدوا بعشرة أي بأنه عشرة أرطال فقضى بعتقه ثم وزن (فثمانية أي فإذا هي ثمانية أرطال (ضمنوا الله عنه ( لأن القضاء) بعتقه على موجب شرعي لقيام الحجة ظاهره (بلا تقصير) منه في تعريف الحق لأنه أي تعريف الحق إنما هو (بعد الحل و الحال أن ذلك معتق) قبل ذلك بالقضاء، لأن القضاء في العقود والفسوخ ينفذ ظاهراً أو باطناً، فهو حر بالقضاء في الواقع، وفيما بينه وبين الله تعالى أيضاً (فعتق باليمين الأول إذ ليس غيره (وهي غير صالحة لإضافة الضمان لأن تصرف المالك ليس بتعد والضمان لا يكون إلا بالتعدي فتعين الشرط) أي الشهادة لكونه تعدياً فيضمنون فلو جعل اليمين قاطعاً للنسبة والضمان عن الشرط فاليمين ههنا ،موجود فيجب أن يقطع عن صاحب الشرط ، فلا يجب الضمان (وعندهما) العبد رقيق بعد القضاء والقضاء بالعتق باطل عندهما باطناً لأن القضاء بخلاف الواقع لا ينفذ عندهما باطناً (والعتق) ينزل عليه ( بالحل) باليمين الثاني (فلا تعدي من شهود الشرط فلا ضمان فتدبر، وكل حكم تعلق بشرطين كأن دخلت هذه الدار ( وهذه الدار (وكالطهارة للصلاة) فإن الطهارة أمور متعددة من غسل الوجه واليد والرجل ومسح الرأس مثلاً فسموا أولهما شرطاً اسماً لا حكماً) أما كونه شرطاً اسماً فلتوقف الحكم عليه، وأما عدم كونه شرطاً حكماً فلانفكاك الحكم عنه (وقول) الإمام فخر الإسلام إنه شرط مجازاً محل نظر) فإن الشرط لم يؤخذ في مفهومه الوجود عند الوجود، اللهم إلا أن يجدد اصطلاح (نعم) في التعليقي وغيره فرق لوجوب الاتصال) في التعليق (وعدمه) أي عدم وجوب الاتصال في غيره، ولا يلزم منه اعتبار الاتصال في مفهوم الشرط فتدبر (وكل) شرط اعترض عليه فعل فاعل مختار وهو) والحال أن الفعل (غير منسوب إليه) أي إلى الشرط فهو في معنى السبب المحض لا يوجب شيئاً (فلا يضمن
The cause. The fourth principle: analogy. So it is as if it is established in the time of sleep, so its occurrence in the house where he can learn is considered as knowledge legally, like travel and hardship. Or it is made conditional for the obligated person by suspension (ta'liq) literally, as when it begins with a word of suspension, like 'If I marry a woman or this one, she is divorced', or in meaning, where there is no single word indicating the condition, but the conditional meaning is understood from the construction, like 'the woman I marry' or 'every woman I marry', because the indeterminate thing described by a relative clause or the indefinite described by a sentence both customarily and linguistically convey the conditional meaning that sentences beginning with words of suspension convey. Hence the particle of consequence enters the report. Unlike 'this one' or 'Zaynab whom I marry', because description with indication or naming is meaningless. And suspension is only understood from it. This section is called a pure condition due to the absence of causality in it entirely; rather the effect of suspension is the removal of the cause, i.e., the elimination of causality. Since it has been stated that what is suspended on a condition is not a cause before its existence. Then the ruling may be attributed to it, i.e., to the condition, in the absence of a cause or a suitable basis for attribution, so that what is consequent upon the cause follows it, and they call it a condition with the meaning of a cause. The reason for the naming is obvious, like slitting a waterskin, from which its contents flowed, and digging a well in the road, so a person walked and fell into it unaware. So slitting and digging are conditions, but in the sense of a cause, because the flowing and the falling of something heavy, which are the cause and reason for destruction, and the waterskin was a barrier, and by slitting the barrier was removed. A natural act is not suitable for attributing destruction as a tort to it. And similarly walking, which is the fall, which is the cause of destruction, is permissible, and a tort is only attributed to transgression, so it is not suitable for attribution, unless he intentionally passes there, for it is prohibited and suitable for attributing the tort to him, so there is no transgression here except in removing the barrier to falling, so liability is attributed to him, so the slitter and digger guarantee what was destroyed by them. And regarding witnesses of the oath and the condition, i.e., when two witnesses testify to his swearing upon manumission, for example, on entering the house, and two others testify to his entering the house, which is the condition, if they all retract after the judgment of manumission, liability is on the witnesses of the oath, because the judicial decision is final and cannot be overturned, for it was based on apparent evidence, so manumission is established, so destruction occurred, and the judicial decision is not suitable as a basis for liability, so it is upon the witnesses because they caused destruction, but upon the witnesses of the oath, not the condition, because the ruling is attributed to the cause when it exists, not to the condition, and the cause is the oath, so the destruction is from the witnesses of the oath, so they guarantee. Regarding imposing liability on the witnesses of the condition if they retract alone without the witnesses of the oath in the supposed case, they differed. A group, including Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may God have mercy on him), said yes, they guarantee, and that is the chosen view. Another group, including Imam Shams al-Sarakhsi (may God have mercy on him), said no, they do not guarantee, and Sheikh Kamal al-Din ibn al-Humam (may God have mercy on him) chose this. For us (the latter group): that his property is destroyed without right, so there must be liability on the transgressor, and the oath is not suitable as a cause for liability, so it is not obligatory on its witnesses, for it is an act of disposal in his property, and the judicial decision is obligatory on the judge when evidence becomes apparent to him, so it is not suitable as a cause for liability, because there is no transgression in performing the obligatory. So there is no transgression except from the witnesses of the condition, because they testified with false testimony and committed a major sin, leading to what it led to, so they guarantee. It becomes like witnesses of retaliation when they retract, because they are transgressors and destruction is attributed to them. And this does not necessitate the objection regarding witnesses of iḥṣān (marital status). For if witnesses testify to fornication, then others testify to iḥṣān, and the judge rules for stoning, and the stoned person dies, then the witnesses of iḥṣān alone retract, they should guarantee the blood money due to their transgression, because fornication is a cause suitable for attributing the penalty, and attribution is cut off from the condition and indication when it exists, and iḥṣān is a condition or indication. And do not be unaware that fornication is not a suitable cause for imposing stoning except in the case of iḥṣān, so the transgression is from them. Understand. Those who deny liability said: The cause here, even if it is not suitable for imposing liability, is suitable for cutting it off from the condition if it is the act of a free agent, so it is not attributed to it as long as it exists, so the witnesses do not guarantee. I say in response: What did they mean by 'cause'? If they mean the judicial decision, then they made it the act of a free agent cutting off the relationship from the condition, as in al-Tahrir and al-Tawdih. Then after it is a cause of the ruling by existence, i.e., the existence of the condition, it is not a cause of destruction. Indeed, one who is compelled legally is like one compelled naturally, so he is like one coerced. So it becomes like falling into a well, and it is as you see. The better view is that he performed what was obligatory, and performing the obligatory is not suitable for attributing liability and tort to him. How so? If what they mentioned were valid, liability would be negated absolutely from the witnesses if they retract, due to the intermediacy of the judicial decision which is the act of the free agent, but that is false by consensus, because liability is obligatory on the witnesses if they retract by consensus. And if they mean the oath, then they make it the act of the free agent that cuts off the relationship from the condition, as is supposed, then there is that it is a legitimate act not suitable as a cause of destruction by transgression at all. Also, it is refuted by his statement: 'If his shackle is ten ratls, he is free, and if someone unlocks it, he is free.' So they testified to ten, i.e., that it is ten ratls, and the judge ruled for his freedom, then weighed and found eight, i.e., it turned out to be eight ratls, they guarantee him, because the judicial decision of freedom was on a legally sound basis due to apparent evidence without shortcoming on his part in establishing the right, because establishing the right is after the unlocking, while the situation is that he was freed before that by the judicial decision, because the judicial decision in contracts and dissolutions is effective outwardly and inwardly, so he is free by the judicial decision in reality and also between him and God. So he is freed by the first oath, as there is no other. And it is not suitable for attributing liability because the owner's disposal is not transgression, and liability is only for transgression. So the condition is determined, i.e., the testimony, because it is a transgression, so they guarantee. If the oath were made a cutter of the relationship and liability from the condition, then the oath exists here, so it must cut off from the owner of the condition, so liability would not be obligatory. And according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad), the slave remains a slave after the judicial decision, and the judicial decision of freedom is invalid inwardly according to them, because a judicial decision contrary to reality is not effective inwardly, and freedom is based on the unlocking by the second oath, so there is no transgression from the witnesses of the condition, so no liability. So ponder. And every ruling connected to two conditions, like 'If you enter this house and this house', and like purification for prayer, for purification consists of multiple acts: washing the face, hands, feet, and wiping the head, for example, so they call the first of them a condition in name, not as a legal condition. As for it being a condition in name, because the ruling depends on it; as for it not being a condition legally, because the ruling can be separated from it. The statement of Imam Fakhr al-Islam that it is a condition metaphorically is questionable, for the condition does not require existence upon existence in its concept, unless one invents a new terminology. Yes, there is a difference between conditional and other constructions regarding the requirement of connection in the conditional and its absence in other constructions, but this does not necessitate considering connection in the concept of the condition. So ponder. And every condition that is intervened by an act of a free agent, while the act is not attributed to it, i.e., to the condition, it is in the sense of a pure cause that does not necessitate anything, so he does not guarantee.
Source pages p. 359

Commentary
This passage is an advanced usul al-fiqh discussion from the sharh of Musallam al-Thubut, focusing on the distinction between a 'condition' (shart) and a 'cause' ('illa) in legal causation, specifically regarding liability (daman). The author begins by defining a 'pure condition' (shart mahd) as one that has no causal element; its effect is merely to remove a barrier or create a context, not to directly cause a ruling. The example of slitting a waterskin or digging a well illustrates this: the act of slitting or digging is a condition for the water to flow or the person to fall, but the direct cause of destruction is the natural flow or fall, which are not acts of a free agent. Thus, liability attaches to the one who removed the barrier (the slitter or digger) only if they acted wrongfully. The discussion then moves to a nuanced case involving witnesses: one set testifies to an oath (the cause) and another to the condition (entry into a house) in a conditional manumission. After judgment, if both retract, liability falls on the oath witnesses because the judgment was based on the oath (cause). However, if only the condition witnesses retract, a dispute arises. Fakhr al-Islam holds them liable because they testified falsely, committing a transgression. Sarakhsi and Ibn al-Humam deny liability, arguing that the oath (cause) as a free and legitimate act cuts off attribution from the condition. The author analyzes these positions, presenting arguments and counterarguments, including analogies to witnesses of retaliation and the case of weighing a shackle. The passage concludes with a general principle: if a condition is intervened by the act of a free agent not attributed to the condition, then that condition is a 'pure cause' (sabab mahd) that does not entail liability. This is a highly technical issue highlighting the Hanafi school's rigorous analysis of causal chains and the conditions for tort liability.

Key Terms
العلة (al-'illa): The cause that directly produces a legal ruling; the effective cause in legal causation.
الشرط (al-shart): A condition that must exist for a ruling to apply but does not directly cause it; its effect is often removal of a barrier.
الضمان (al-daman): Legal liability or guarantee to compensate for loss or damage.
التعدي (al-ta'addi): Transgression or wrongful act that is the basis for tort liability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of witnesses of an oath and a condition for manumission, if both retract after judgment, which witnesses are liable according to the preferred view mentioned?
A) Witnesses of the condition only
B) Witnesses of the oath only
C) Both sets of witnesses
D) Neither set of witnesses

Key Takeaway
A condition without causality does not attract liability; only the direct cause or a condition with causal meaning does.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Liability for Opening a Cage: Qiyas vs Istihsan
Dispute on liability for freeing a bird or animal from a cage via qiyas and istihsan.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on liability for opening a cage and distinguish between qiyas and istihsan in this usul dispute.

Core Idea
Opening a cage does not make you liable for the animal's escape because its choice severs causal attribution in Hanafi law.

This passage presents a classic usul al-fiqh dispute between the Hanafi school and Imams Muhammad and al-Shafi'i on whether the person who opens a cage or stable is liable for the resulting flight or escape. The disagreement hinges on whether the animal's action is attributed to its own choice or to the opener's removal of a barrier. The Hanafis argue by qiyas (analogy) that the animal's volition cuts causation; the opponents employ istihsan (juristic preference) by likening the escape to natural flow. Understanding this dispute sharpens your ability to evaluate how different usul principles generate conflicting rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two parties and their rulings
The Hanafi school (Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, Muhammad al-Shaybani) holds that the opener is not liable. Imams Muhammad and al-Shafi'i hold the opener liable. Note that within the Hanafi school, the position is unanimous.
The text says 'خلافاً لمحمد' — Imam Muhammad here refers to al-Shaybani, who agrees with the Hanafi position; the opposing view is attributed to Muhammad al-Shaybani? Actually the text says 'خلافاً لمحمد الإمام والشافعي' — probably meaning Muhammad ibn al-Hasan al-Shaybani and al-Shafi'i. But in the Hanafi school, Muhammad agrees with Abu Hanifa, so the dispute is between the Hanafi view (Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn) and al-Shafi'i. Rereading: 'خلافاً لمحمد الإمام والشافعي' — this might be a copyist error; typically the opponent is al-Shafi'i, and 'Muhammad' might refer to Muhammad ibn al-Hasan? But that is unlikely. For accuracy, follow the text: the opponent is 'Muhammad [al-Shaybani?] and al-Shafi'i'. However, since the Hanafi school considers Muhammad al-Shaybani as part of the school, the more plausible reading is that 'Muhammad' means a different scholar? The translation says 'Imam Muhammad and al-Shafi'i'. We'll present it as stated.
2
State the Hanafi proof from the text
The Hanafi proof is that the bird's flight and the animal's escape are by their own choice ('باختيارهما'). The opening is not a legal cause because the animal's volition intervenes. The text also cites analogies: a hunting dog that veers off does not attribute the catch to the hunter; a mount that swerves does not make its owner liable for damages.
The analogies show that any intervening volitional act, even if legally negligible, cuts attribution.
3
State the opponent's proof and its basis
The opponent argues that the animal's flight/escape is natural (طبيعي) when the barrier is removed, like the flow of liquid from a pierced waterskin. Thus the opener is the direct cause and liable. They also argue that the animal's act is legally negligible (هدر), so it should be attributed to the opener, not the animal.
This reasoning resembles istihsan because it prioritizes protection of property over strict causal analysis.
4
Analyze the Hanafi response and refutation
The Hanafi response insists that even if the tendency is natural, there is still an element of choice that cuts attribution. The refutation uses the dog and mount examples to show that volitional acts, even if minimal, break the causal chain. The text also questions: if the animal's choice is similar to nature, why is it not like a judge forced by false testimony? The point is that the animal's wildness does not make its action purely natural without any choice.
The response underscores that legal causation requires a direct and exclusive link; the animal's choice, however limited, is sufficient to sever it.
5
Distinguish qiyas from istihsan in this context
According to Qadi Imam Abu Zaid (as cited in al-Kashshaf), the Hanafi view is the answer of qiyas, while the opponent's view is close to istihsan. The opponent attaches the customary response (fleeing when barrier removed) to nature, disregarding the animal's choice to protect property. This illustrates how istihsan can override strict qiyas for public interest.
Remember: Hanafis do not reject istihsan; they use it selectively. Here, the Hanafi view is the standard qiyas; the opponent's is an istihsan-based departure.

الأصل الرابع/ القياس الحال العبد المقيد (قيمة العبد إن أبق بعد الحل لأن (الإباق وجد باختياره، والحل غير موجب له بل حق المولى يسده عن الإباق لكونه مكلفاً بالإطاعة (بخلاف شق الزق) فإنه موجب لسيلان ما طبعه ذلك وكذا في فتح القفص والاصطبل فطار الطير أوفر الدابة (لا يضمنهما الفاتح) لكون الطيران والفرار باختيارهما، وليس موجباً لهما (خلافاً لمحمد) الإمام والشافعي رضي الله عنه لأن في طبعهما الفرار عند عدم المانع، فكان) طبيعياً (كسيلان المائع) بالشق فيضمن الفاتح ولأن فعلهما) هذا هدر شرعاً فلا اعتبار له فيضاف إلى الفاتح دون اختيارهما بخلاف العبد) المحلول قيده لصحة الذمة شرعاً بوجوب الاجتناب عن الفرار (ورد) قوله : ( بأن للاختيار مدخلاً (البتة في الطيران والفرار (وهو) أي الفرار (وإن كان طبيعياً) لهما للتوحش (ليس طبعياً بأن لا يكون للاختيار مدخل، وإذا لم يكن طبعياً قطع النسبة عن الفاتح (وكونه هدراً شرعاً (لا يمنع) قطع الحكم عن الشرط كمن أرسل كلباً إلى صيد فمال عنه إلى جهة أخرى ثم مال إليه فأخذه لا يحل لأن بالميل عنه (قطع النسبة) نسبة الإرسال إلى المرسل، وكمن أرسل دابته على الطريق فجالت يمنة ويسرة فأتلفت شيئاً لا ضمان على المالك) لأنها بالتوجه إلى الجهة الأخرى قطعت نسبتها عن المرسل، فعلم أن تخلل فعل مختار، ولو كان هدراً مما يقطع النسبة إلى غيره، وإذا انقطع نسبة طيران الطير أو ند الدابة لم يضمن وفيه ما فيه إذ مداخلة هذا الاختيار الشبيه بالطبع لا يقطع النسبة البتة، كيف وليس توحشه الطبيعي أدون من انجبار القاضي في الحكم بالقصاص بشاهد الزور. وأما مسألة إرسال الكلب فلأن الشرط في حل الذبيحة الذهاب من عند المرسل لطلب الصيد، وبالميل إلى جهة أخرى علم أنه ما كان ذهب لطلب الصيد ومسألة إرسال الدابة على الطريق فيه الكلام ولو سلمت فالاجتناب عن اتلافه كان ممكناً فالقصور من المتلف نفسه أو ماله فافهم. وفي «الكشف : قال القاضي الإمام أبو زيد رحمه الله ما ذكرنا جواب القياس، وما ذكره الخصم قريب من الاستحسان، فقد ألحق العادة وإن كانت عن اختيار بالطبيعة التي لا اختيار فيها صيانة لأموال الناس وأهدر اختيار ما لا عقل له لأنه جبار انتهى.
The fourth principle / Qiyas: The case of a tied slave: (The value of the slave if he runs away after being untied is due because (the running away occurred by his choice, and untying is not a cause for it, rather the right of the master restrains him from running away because he is obligated to obedience (unlike piercing a waterskin) for that causes the flow of what is in its nature, and likewise opening a cage or stable, if a bird flies or an animal escapes, (the opener is not liable) because flying and escaping are by their own choice, and not caused by them (contrary to Muhammad) the Imam and al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with them, because in their nature is fleeing when there is no obstacle, thus it is natural (like the flow of liquid) by piercing, so the opener is liable, and because their action) is negligible in law, so it is not considered, so it is attributed to the opener without their choice, unlike the slave) whose tie is removed because of the validity of legal capacity by the obligation to avoid fleeing. (He refuted) his statement: ( that choice has a role (at all in flying and escaping, (and it) i.e. fleeing (even if natural) for them due to wildness (is not purely natural by having no role for choice, and if not purely natural, attribution is cut from the opener. (And its being negligible in law (does not prevent) cutting the ruling from the condition, like one who sent a dog to hunt, then it turned away to another direction, then turned again and caught it, it is not permissible because by turning away (it cut the attribution) the attribution of sending to the sender; and like one who let his mount loose on the road, then it swerved right and left and damaged something, there is no liability on the owner) because by heading to the other direction it cut its attribution from the sender. So it is known that an intervening volitional act, even if negligible, cuts attribution to another. And if the attribution of the bird's flight or the animal's escape is cut, he is not liable. There is a point here because the intervention of this choice similar to nature does not cut attribution at all, for how is its natural wildness less than the judge's being forced to impose retaliatory punishment on the testimony of a false witness? As for the case of sending the dog, the condition for the game being lawful is that it goes from the sender seeking game, and by turning to another direction it is known that it did not go seeking game. And the case of letting the mount loose on the road has discussion; if conceded, avoiding its destruction was possible, so the shortcoming is from the one who caused destruction himself or his property, so understand. In al-Kashshaf: Qadi Imam Abu Zaid, may Allah have mercy on him, said: What we mentioned is the answer of qiyas, and what the opponent mentioned is close to istihsan, for he attached the custom, even if by choice, to nature which has no choice, to protect people's property, and he disregarded the choice of the irrational because it is invalid (jubbār). End.
Source pages p. 361

Commentary
This passage is a madhhab dispute on the liability of a person who opens a cage or stable, causing a bird to fly away or an animal to escape. The Hanafi position, attributed to Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad al-Shaybani, is that the opener is not liable. The reasoning is that the flight and escape are acts of the animal's own choice; the opening is merely the removal of a barrier, not a direct cause. The animal is considered a rational agent in this context, though its choice is limited, but it is enough to cut the causal attribution to the opener. Imam Muhammad and Imam al-Shafi'i hold the opener liable, arguing that the animal's action is natural (tabi'i) like the flow of liquid when a container is pierced; thus the opener is the direct cause. The text refutes this by pointing out that even if natural, the choice still has a role, and gives analogies: a hunting dog that veers off then catches prey is not considered the hunter's catch because the dog's deviation cut the attribution; similarly, a mount that swerves and causes damage does not make the owner liable. The text then cites al-Kashshaf, where Qadi Imam Abu Zaid distinguishes between the Hanafi view as qiyas and the opponent's view as close to istihsan, which is used to protect people's property by disregarding the animal's choice.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning by which a ruling from an original case is extended to a new case due to shared effective cause (ʿilla).
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): A method of juristic preference that deviates from strict qiyas for a stronger reason, such as custom or necessity.
هدر (hadar): Legally negligible; an action that does not give rise to liability in terms of the doer's responsibility.
قطع النسبة (qaṭʿ al-nisba): Cutting the attribution or causal link between an action and a person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, why is the person who opens a cage not liable for a bird that flies away?
A) Because the bird's action is legally negligible (hadar).
B) Because the bird's flight is by its own choice, and not caused by the opening.
C) Because the opener had no intention to release the bird.
D) Because the bird is considered property that the owner has abandoned.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the existence of choice in the agent — even if legally negligible — severs causal attribution, justifying the non-liability of the cage-opener.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
10 min
Permissibility of Qiyas: Rational and Debate
Arguments on permissibility of qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the rational status of qiyas and evaluate the arguments for and against its permissibility.

Core Idea
Qiyas is rationally permissible, neither obligatory nor impossible.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut discusses the rational permissibility of qiyas (analogical reasoning) as a source of Islamic law. It presents the Hanafi position that qiyas is ja'iz 'aqlan, refuting both the claim that it is obligatory (held by some Mu'tazila) and the claim that it is impossible (held by some Shi'a and Mu'tazila). The author uses rational arguments and responds to objections, ultimately affirming that reason itself judges that similar cases should have similar rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the thesis
The passage opens with the Hanafi thesis: qiyas is rationally permissible. Note the three positions: permissible (majority), obligatory (al-Qaffal, Abu al-Husayn), impossible (some Shi'a, al-Nazzam).
The Hanafi school occupies the middle ground.
2
Examine the proof for permissibility
The proof is rational: if qiyas were impossible, its actual occurrence (which is observed) would entail the impossible, which is not the case. Moreover, reason itself judges that similar things should have similar rulings; denying this is obstinate (mukabara).
This proof targets the impossibility claim.
3
Analyze the opposing view of obligation
The obligers argue: without qiyas, most incidents would lack rulings. The Hanafi response: original permissibility (ibaha asliyya) and general texts ('umumat) can cover many cases, so there is no emptiness.
This shows qiyas is not the only source for new cases.
4
Respond to the mercy argument
Some claim difference among mujtahids is a mercy, so uniform qiyas would remove it. Hanafi response: ijtihad extends beyond qiyas to interpretations of apparent, hidden, and ambiguous texts, so difference remains.
Qiyas is not the sole cause of ikhtilaf.
5
Assess the final reinforcement
The passage concludes that divine laws are based on welfares varying with time and place, so delegation to rational opinion (qiyas) is necessary to avoid lack of rulings. This strengthens the case for qiyas.
This addresses the insufficiency of general texts alone.

Chapter: The religious obligation to perform qiyas and act according to it is rationally permissible. (Reason does not deem it impossible) according to the majority of the people of Islam. (It is) not obligatory as held by al-Qaffal and Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili, nor impossible rationally as held by some Shi'a and some Mu'tazila, among them al-Nazzam. For us, if it were impossible, its occurrence would entail the impossible, and causing something impossible does not entail impossibility at all. (How can it be? For comparing like things is a matter of reason) and it judges that similar things have one ruling, and denying this is obstinate. Then this proof is only to refute the saying of the Rawafid and their like. As for the saying of Abu al-Husayn, its refutation does not concern us, so they ignored it and suffice with uncovering Abu al-Husayn's doubt. Those who claim obligation said: If qiyas were not obligatory, most incidents would be devoid of rulings. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise false. (We say) We do not accept the falsity of the consequent: rather it is permissible to act on original permissibility (ibaha asliyya) and the like. Even if the falsity of the consequent is conceded, we do not accept the implication because it is possible to specify (each incident by general texts, so there would be no emptiness. And if it is said: general texts are not available in such a manner, I say: then the rational obligation does not remain. (I say: If it is said: the difference among mujtahids is a mercy, so not every incident is covered by rulings, otherwise there would be no difference and the great mercy would go away. We say: Difference is not restricted to qiyas, because ijtihad is possible in other things from apparent meanings, hidden meanings, and ambiguous texts, so opinions differ in understanding their meanings and deriving legal rulings from them. (Then indeed) i.e., the implication is not without strength, because divine rulings are based on the welfare of the servants as a favor, and they vary according to difference of time and place, so they cannot be confined except by delegation to opinion, otherwise incidents would be devoid due to insufficiency of general texts, so ponder). And you should not miss that the variation of welfare in every...
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage is an usul rule establishing the permissibility of qiyas (analogical reasoning) as a source of Islamic law. It states that qiyas is rationally permissible (ja'iz 'aqlan) and not impossible, according to the majority of Muslims. It is not rationally obligatory as some argued, nor impossible as some Shi'a and Mu'tazila claimed. The proof: if qiyas were impossible, then when it occurs (as it does), it would entail the impossible, which is not the case. Moreover, reason itself judges that similar cases should have similar rulings; denying this is obstinate. Those who claim qiyas is obligatory argue that without it, most new incidents would lack rulings. The Hanafi response is that original permissibility (ibaha asliyya) can be applied, and general texts can cover many cases. The passage also addresses the argument that difference among mujtahids is a mercy, so qiyas is not needed; but the response is that ijtihad extends beyond qiyas to other interpretive methods, so difference still exists. Finally, the passage emphasizes that divine laws are based on varying welfares, so delegation to rational opinion (like qiyas) is necessary to avoid lack of rulings.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning to derive rulings for new cases based on similarity to an original case with a shared effective cause.
الإباحة الأصلية (al-ibāḥa al-aṣliyya): The original ruling that all things are permissible unless there is evidence to prohibit them.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi position on the rational status of qiyas according to this passage?
A) It is rationally obligatory.
B) It is rationally permissible.
C) It is rationally impossible.
D) It is prohibited in matters of worship.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that qiyas is rationally permissible, necessary for deriving rulings in new matters, but not obligatory given original permissibility and general texts.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Rational Permissibility of Qiyas: Hanafi Position and Proof
Rational proof for permissibility of qiyas and response to wajib argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on the rational permissibility of qiyas and explain the primary rational argument that similar cases should have similar rulings.

Core Idea
Qiyas is rationally permissible because reason itself judges that similar cases have similar rulings.

This lesson addresses the foundational debate in usul al-fiqh: is analogical reasoning (qiyas) rationally permissible? The Hanafi school, representing the majority, holds that it is ja'iz 'aqlan, not wajib nor mumtani'. We will examine the rational proof and the response to those who claim qiyas is obligatory. Understanding this debate is essential for appreciating the role of ijtihad in deriving legal rulings.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three positions on qiyas
The text presents three views: permissible (majority of Islam), obligatory (al-Qaffal and Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili), and impossible (some Shi'a and Mu'tazila, including al-Nazzam).
The author immediately sets aside the wajib view as not needing refutation.
2
State the Hanafi rational proof
If qiyas were rationally impossible, its actual occurrence would entail a logical contradiction. But no contradiction follows from its occurrence. Moreover, reason itself judges that similar things should have similar rulings; denying this is stubbornness.
This proof directly refutes the deniers who claim impossibility.
3
Explain why the wajib view is not refuted
The author says the wajib view (Abu al-Husayn) does not concern them; they only need to expose its weak point. The focus is on refuting the deniers.
This shows the Hanafi approach: not every opposing view needs detailed refutation.
4
Respond to the wajib argument
The obligators argue that if qiyas were not wajib, many incidents would lack rulings. The Hanafi response: rulings can come from original permissibility (ibaha asliyya) or general texts, and disagreement is not confined to qiyas.
This demonstrates the sufficiency of other sources of law.

والعقل يمنع من طريق غير مأمون فالقياس ممنوع عقلاً (قلنا) منع العقل ممنوع، بل إذا كان الصواب راجحاً لا يمنع العقل فإنّ المظان الأكثرية) النافعة (لا تترك بالاحتمالات (الأقلية النادرة والقياس لما كان الصواب فيه راجحاً ينبغي أن لا يترك (كيف) يترك راجح الصواب وأكثر تصرفات العقلاء لفوائد غير متيقنة بالاستقراء) ثم دليلهم منقوض بظواهر النصوص، فإنه غير مأمون لوجود الاحتمال، فلا يتبع (و) قالوا (ثانياً : وهو للنظام) حاصل القياس تماثل المتماثلات بين الأحكام والشارع لم يعتبر الأحكام كذلك، فلا يكون القياس معتبراً عنده تعالى ووجه عدم اعتبار الأحكام كذلك قوله (ثبت الفرق بين المتماثلات، كإيجاب الغسل من المني دون البول) مع كونهما نجسين خارجين من سبيل واحد وقطع سارق القليل دون غاصب الكثير مع أن جناية الأول أصغر من جناية الثاني (وكثير) من الأحكام كذلك (و) ثبت الجمع بين المختلفات كالتسوية بين القتل عمداً وخطأ في الإحرام) كون العمد جناية كاملة دون الخطأ ( وكالزنا والردة كلاهما يوجبان القتل مع كون الثاني أكبر كبيرة من الأول إلى غير ذلك . والقياس) كان يقضي (بالعكس) أي بثبوت الجمع بين المتماثلات والفرق بين المختلفات (قلنا) ليس المتماثلات متماثلة من كل وجه، ولا المختلفات مختلفة من كل وجه بل يجوز اختلاف المتماثلات في المناط، واتفاق المختلفات فيه. ويجوز الفرق لفارق فلا مماثلة باعتبار ذلك الفارق (والجمع) بجامع فلا مخالفة بالنظر إليه (مطلقاً، ألا ترى النظام مع اعتزاله ومخالفته إيانا (معنا في الإسلام) فتتفق الأحكام التي بحسبه على أن الاتفاق لعلل مختلفة (جائز) يعني أنه يجوز أن يكون لعلل شتى معلول واحد، فيجوز اتحاد أحكام المختلفات فافهم . (و) قالوا : (ثالثاً : القياس يوجد فيه اختلاف كثير (كما هو الواقع المشاهد وكل ما يوجد فيه اختلاف لا يكون من عند الله، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو مردود إجماعاً) إذ لا حكم إلا لله تعالى (أما) المقدمة (الثانية فلقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ عِندِ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلَافًا كَثِيرًا ﴾ [النساء : ۸۲] فإنه دل على أن ما عند الله لا يوجد فيه اختلاف لأنّ لو لانتفاء الثاني لأجل انتفاء الأول، فانتفاء الأول سبب يلزمه انتفاء الثاني وينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى تلك المقدمة وهي قولنا ما يوجد فيه اختلاف ليس من عند الله تعالى (و) قال (في) شرح المختصر أن في الآية إشارة إلى المقدمة الأولى أيضاً، وقرره التفتازاني بأنها دلت على أن ما ليس من عند الله يوجد فيه الاختلاف ومعلوم من الخارج (أن القياس ليس من عند الله بل بإخراج المجتهد برأيه، فهو مما يوجد فيه اختلاف (ثم أورد) هو نفسه بأنه لو كان هذا أي كون القياس لا من الله معلوماً لما احتيج إلى الآية) المذكورة (بل نضمه إلى المقدمة (الثالثة : ويتم الدليل (أقول) ليس تقريره ما ذكر بل تقريره أنها دلت على أن ما من عند غير الله ففيه اختلاف بصريحها. ومعلوم أن القياس من عند غير الله) وهو المجتهد
Chapter: The devotion to acquiring analogical reasoning and acting upon its requirement is rationally permissible. Reason does not deem it impossible according to the majority of the people of Islam. It is not obligatory as al-Qaffal and Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili held, nor rationally impossible as some Shi'a and some Mu'tazila, including al-Nazzam, held. For us, if it were impossible, its occurrence would entail a contradiction, and no contradiction necessarily follows from its occurrence at all, necessarily. How? For the consideration of similarities is a dictate of reason, and reason judges that similar things have a single ruling; denying this is stubbornness. Then this proof is only for invalidating the saying of the Rafidah and their ilk. As for the saying of Abu al-Husayn, its invalidation does not concern us, so they turned away from it and sufficed with exposing the doubt of Abu al-Husayn. Those who make it obligatory said: If devotion to analogical reasoning were not obligatory, most incidents would be devoid of rulings. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. We say: We do not concede the falsity of the consequent; rather, acting upon original permissibility and the like is permissible. And even if the falsity of the consequent were conceded, we do not concede the entailment due to the possibility of specification upon every incident by general texts, so there is no void. And if it is said: General texts do not exist likewise, I say: Then rational obligation does not remain. I say: If it is said: Disagreement among mujtahids is a mercy, so do not make rulings universal for every incident, otherwise no disagreement would occur and much mercy would be lost. We say: Disagreement is not confined to analogical reasoning, because ijtihad in other things like apparent texts, subtle texts, and ambiguous texts is permissible, so opinions differ in understanding their meanings and deriving legal rulings from them. Then, that entailment is not devoid of strength, because divine rulings are based on benefits for the servants as a favor, and they vary according to variation of time and place, so they cannot be controlled except by delegation to opinion, otherwise incidents would be devoid due to insufficiency of general texts, so reflect. And it does not escape you that the variation of benefits in every time such that it does not fall under fixed controls from the Lawgiver is a point of reflection; it needs a proof to clarify that. How? The controls set by the people of ijtihad have not left any incident up to this point, so what do you think of His knowledge encompassing what is from eternity to eternity? So reflect. The deniers said: First, analogical reasoning is a path not safe from error, and reason forbids a path not safe from error, so analogical reasoning is forbidden rationally. We say: The forbiddance of reason is denied. Rather, when correctness is preponderant, reason does not forbid it, because beneficial preponderant probabilities are not abandoned due to rare minority probabilities. And since correctness in analogical reasoning is preponderant, it should not be abandoned. How can preponderant correctness be abandoned while most rational actions are for benefits not certain by induction? Then their proof is refuted by the apparent texts of the scriptures, for they are not safe due to the existence of probability, so they are not followed. And they said: Second, which is for al-Nazzam: The essence of analogical reasoning is the similarity of similar things in rulings, but the Lawgiver does not consider rulings that way, so analogical reasoning is not considered with Him, may He be exalted. The evidence that rulings are not considered that way is his statement: Difference between similar things is established, like requiring ghusl from semen but not urine, even though both are impurities exiting from one passage; and cutting the hand of a thief for a little but not a usurper for much, even though the crime of the former is lesser than the crime of the latter; and many rulings are like that. And union between different things is established, like equality between intentional and unintentional killing in ihram, even though intentional is a complete crime unlike unintentional; and like zinā and apostasy both requiring killing, even though the latter is a greater major sin than the former; and so on. And analogical reasoning would dictate the opposite, i.e., establishing union between similars and difference between differents. We say: Similars are not similar in every respect, nor are differents different in every respect; rather, it is permissible for similars to differ in the ratio (manāṭ) and for differents to agree in it. And difference is permissible due to a differentiator, so there is no similarity considering that differentiator, and union is due to a unifier, so there is no opposition considering it, absolutely. Do you not see that al-Nazzam, despite his Mu'tazilism and opposition to us, is with us in Islam? So rulings that are according to it agree that agreement is due to different causes is permissible. That is, it is permissible for one effect to be from various causes, so it is permissible for rulings of differents to be unified, so understand. And they said: Thirdly, analogical reasoning involves much disagreement, as is observed in reality, and everything that involves disagreement is not from Allah, and everything that is like that is rejected by consensus, for there is no ruling except from Allah, may He be exalted. As for the second premise, it is due to His statement, exalted be He: {And if it had been from other than Allah, they would have found in it much disagreement} [al-Nisa' 4:82]. For it indicates that what is with Allah has no disagreement, because 'law' (if) for the non-existence of the second due to the non-existence of the first, so the non-existence of the first is a cause necessitating the non-existence of the second, and it reverses by contrapositive to that premise, which is our saying: What involves disagreement is not from Allah, may He be exalted. And he said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that in the verse there is an indication of the first premise as well, and al-Taftazani explained it that it indicates that what is not from Allah involves disagreement, and it is known from external evidence that analogical reasoning is not from Allah but rather by the derivation of the mujtahid with his opinion, so it is something that involves disagreement. Then he himself objected that if this, i.e., that analogical reasoning is not from Allah, were known, there would be no need for the mentioned verse. Rather, we would append it to the third premise and the proof would be complete. I say: His explanation is not what was mentioned; rather, his explanation is that it indicates that what is from other than Allah involves disagreement explicitly, and it is known that analogical reasoning is from other than Allah, i.e., the mujtahid.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut (a sharh of Musallam al-Thubut) addresses the rational permissibility of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in usul al-fiqh. The author first states the Hanafi position: qiyas is rationally permissible (ja'iz 'aqlan), contrary to those who deem it obligatory (wajib) or impossible (mumtani'). The rational proof is that if qiyas were impossible, its actual occurrence (as practiced by scholars) would entail a logical contradiction, but it does not. Moreover, reason itself judges that similar cases should have similar rulings; denying this is stubbornness. The author then dismisses the objection of Abu al-Husayn (who held it wajib) as irrelevant, focusing on refuting the deniers (Shi'a and Mu'tazila). The deniers' first argument: qiyas is not safe from error, so reason forbids it. The Hanafi response: reason does not forbid probabilistic reasoning when the correct outcome is more likely; people routinely act on probabilities. Moreover, even textual evidence (zawahir al-nusus) is probabilistic, yet it is followed. Second argument (al-Nazzam): the Lawgiver does not treat similar cases alike (e.g., ghusl from semen vs. urine) and treats different cases alike (e.g., intentional and unintentional killing in ihram), thus qiyas is invalid. The response: similarities are not absolute; differences exist in the illah (effective cause). What appears as similarity may have a distinguishing factor, and what appears as difference may share a unifier. Third argument: qiyas leads to much disagreement, and anything with disagreement cannot be from Allah (citing Quran 4:82). The author discusses the logical structure of the verse: by contrapositive ('aks al-naqid), whatever has much disagreement is not from Allah. The deniers claim qiyas has disagreement, so it is not from Allah. The Hanafi response clarifies that the verse refers to the Quran itself (a text), not to human reasoning. Moreover, the premise that qiyas is 'from other than Allah' is not automatically conceded; the verse is used to prove that, but if it were already known, the argument would be circular. Ultimately, the Hanafi position is that qiyas is a valid rational method, and the objections do not hold.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared illah.
التعبد (al-ta'abbud): Being bound to practice an act of devotion; here meaning legitimacy of practicing qiyas.
المتماثلات (al-mutamathilat): Things that are similar; reason dictates similar cases have similar rulings.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position in this passage, qiyas is:
A) Rationally obligatory (wajib 'aqlan).
B) Rationally permissible (ja'iz 'aqlan).
C) Rationally impossible (mumtani' 'aqlan).
D) Rationally recommended (mandub 'aqlan).

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school affirms qiyas as rationally permissible, grounding it in reason's judgment of similarity and dismissing the wajib claim as irrelevant to their proof.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
10 min
Refuting the Deniers: Three Objections to Qiyas and Hanafi Responses
Three objections to qiyas and Hanafi rebuttals

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the three objections of those who deny qiyas and state the Hanafi responses to each.

Core Idea
Deniers' objections—safety, inconsistency, and disagreement—are refuted by showing probability suffices, similarity is not absolute, and disagreement arises from ijtihad.

Opponents of qiyas raise three substantive objections: that it is not safe from error, that the Lawgiver's rulings contradict the logic of similarity, and that qiyas leads to disagreement contrary to the Quran. In this lesson, we analyze each objection and the Hanafi rebuttal, clarifying the rational and textual foundations of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
List the three objections
The deniers argue: (1) Qiyas is not safe from error, so reason forbids it. (2) The Lawgiver does not treat similar cases alike (e.g., ghusl from semen vs. urine; thief vs. usurper) and treats different cases alike (e.g., intentional and unintentional killing in ihram; zina and apostasy). (3) Qiyas leads to much disagreement, and anything with disagreement is not from Allah (Quran 4:82).
Objection 2 is attributed to al-Nazzam.
2
For each, state the Hanafi response
(1) Reason does not forbid probabilistic reasoning when correctness is preponderant; even textual evidence is probabilistic. (2) Similars are not similar in all respects; differences in illah (manāṭ) exist. (3) The verse refers to the Quran, not qiyas; disagreement is due to ijtihad, not inherent in qiyas.
The response to objection 3 includes a discussion of the logical structure of the verse.
3
Evaluate the strength of the Hanafi responses
The responses are logically sound: they acknowledge the legitimate concerns but show that the objections do not undermine the validity of qiyas. The Hanafi position accommodates the variability of rulings and the role of ijtihad.
Consider how these responses apply to modern objections to analogical reasoning.
