النظام لنا لو كان ممتنعاً للزم من وقوعه محال و(لا يلزم من إلزامه محال أصلا ضرورة (كيف والاعتبار بالأمثال من قضية العقل) وهو يحكم أن المتماثلات حكمها ،واحد، وإنكار هذا مكابرة؛ ثم هذا الدليل إنما هو لإبطال قول الروافض ونحوهم، وأما قول أبي الحسين فلا يهمنا إبطاله ولذا أعرضوا عنه واكتفوا بكشف شبهة أبي الحسين. الموجبون (قالوا : لولا التعبد بالقياس واجب الخلت الوقائع أكثرها عن الأحكام) والتالي باطل، فالمقدم مثله (قلنا) لا نسلم بطلان التالي : بل يجوز العمل بالإباحة الأصلية ونحوها ولو سلم بطلان التالي فلا نسلم الملازمة لجواز التنصيص) على كل واقعة (بالعمومات فلا خلو وإن قيل : لم توجد العمومات كذلك قلت: لم يبق الوجوب العقلي (أقول : إن قيل: (الاختلاف بين المجتهدين (رحمة فلا تعمم الأحكام كل واقعة، وإلا لم يقع اختلاف فتذهب الرحمة الكثيرة قلنا : الاختلاف لا ينحصر في القياس لجواز الاجتهاد في غيره من الظواهر والخفي والمتشابه فتختلف الآراء في فهم معانيها وأخذ الحكم الشرعي منها (ثم إنه) أي اللزوم لا يخلو عن قوة، لأن الأحكام الإلهية مبنية على المصالح للعباد تفضلاً (وهي متفاوته حسب تفاوت الزمان والمكان، فلا يمكن ضبطها إلا بالتفويض إلى الرأي وإلاّ خلت الوقائع لعدم كفاية العمومات فتدبر) وأنت لا يذهب عليك أن تفاوت المصالح في كل زمان بحيث لا يدخل تحت ضوابط موضوعة من قبل الشارع محل تأمل لا بد في إبانة ذلك من دليل كيف والضوابط الموضوعة من أهل الاجتهاد لم تخرج واقعة إلى هذه الغاية، فما ظنك علمه محيط بما يكون من الأزل إلى الأبد، فتأمل. المنكرون (قالوا : أولاً) القياس طريق غير مأمون من الخطأ والعقل يمنع من طريق غير مأمون فالقياس ممنوع عقلاً (قلنا) منع العقل ممنوع، بل إذا كان الصواب راجحاً لا يمنع العقل فإنّ المظان الأكثرية) النافعة (لا تترك بالاحتمالات (الأقلية النادرة والقياس لما كان الصواب فيه راجحاً ينبغي أن لا يترك (كيف) يترك راجح الصواب وأكثر تصرفات العقلاء لفوائد غير متيقنة بالاستقراء) ثم دليلهم منقوض بظواهر النصوص، فإنه غير مأمون لوجود الاحتمال، فلا يتبع (و) قالوا (ثانياً : وهو للنظام) حاصل القياس تماثل المتماثلات بين الأحكام والشارع لم يعتبر الأحكام كذلك، فلا يكون القياس معتبراً عنده تعالى ووجه عدم اعتبار الأحكام كذلك قوله (ثبت الفرق بين المتماثلات، كإيجاب الغسل من المني دون البول) مع كونهما نجسين خارجين من سبيل واحد وقطع سارق القليل دون غاصب الكثير مع أن جناية الأول أصغر من جناية الثاني (وكثير) من الأحكام كذلك (و) ثبت الجمع بين المختلفات كالتسوية بين القتل عمداً وخطأ في الإحرام) كون العمد جناية كاملة دون الخطأ ( وكالزنا والردة كلاهما يوجبان القتل مع كون الثاني أكبر كبيرة من الأول إلى غير ذلك . والقياس) كان يقضي (بالعكس) أي بثبوت الجمع بين المتماثلات والفرق بين المختلفات (قلنا) ليس المتماثلات متماثلة من كل وجه، ولا المختلفات مختلفة من كل وجه بل يجوز اختلاف المتماثلات في المناط، واتفاق المختلفات فيه. ويجوز الفرق لفارق فلا مماثلة باعتبار ذلك الفارق (والجمع) بجامع فلا مخالفة بالنظر إليه (مطلقاً، ألا ترى النظام مع اعتزاله ومخالفته إيانا (معنا في الإسلام) فتتفق الأحكام التي بحسبه على أن الاتفاق لعلل مختلفة (جائز) يعني أنه يجوز أن يكون لعلل شتى معلول واحد، فيجوز اتحاد أحكام المختلفات فافهم . (و) قالوا : (ثالثاً : القياس يوجد فيه اختلاف كثير (كما هو الواقع المشاهد وكل ما يوجد فيه اختلاف لا يكون من عند الله، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو مردود إجماعاً) إذ لا حكم إلا لله تعالى (أما) المقدمة (الثانية فلقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ عِندِ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلَافًا كَثِيرًا ﴾ [النساء : ۸۲] فإنه دل على أن ما عند الله لا يوجد فيه اختلاف لأنّ لو لانتفاء الثاني لأجل انتفاء الأول، فانتفاء الأول سبب يلزمه انتفاء الثاني وينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى تلك المقدمة وهي قولنا ما يوجد فيه اختلاف ليس من عند الله تعالى (و) قال (في) شرح المختصر أن في الآية إشارة إلى المقدمة الأولى أيضاً، وقرره التفتازاني بأنها دلت على أن ما ليس من عند الله يوجد فيه الاختلاف ومعلوم من الخارج (أن القياس ليس من عند الله بل بإخراج المجتهد برأيه، فهو مما يوجد فيه اختلاف (ثم أورد) هو نفسه بأنه لو كان هذا أي كون القياس لا من الله معلوماً لما احتيج إلى الآية) المذكورة (بل نضمه إلى المقدمة (الثالثة : ويتم الدليل (أقول) ليس تقريره ما ذكر بل تقريره أنها دلت على أن ما من عند غير الله ففيه اختلاف بصريحها. ومعلوم أن القياس من عند غير الله) وهو المجتهد
Chapter: The devotion to acquiring analogical reasoning and acting upon its requirement is rationally permissible. Reason does not deem it impossible according to the majority of the people of Islam. It is not obligatory as al-Qaffal and Abu al-Husayn al-Mu'tazili held, nor rationally impossible as some Shi'a and some Mu'tazila, including al-Nazzam, held. For us, if it were impossible, its occurrence would entail a contradiction, and no contradiction necessarily follows from its occurrence at all, necessarily. How? For the consideration of similarities is a dictate of reason, and reason judges that similar things have a single ruling; denying this is stubbornness. Then this proof is only for invalidating the saying of the Rafidah and their ilk. As for the saying of Abu al-Husayn, its invalidation does not concern us, so they turned away from it and sufficed with exposing the doubt of Abu al-Husayn. Those who make it obligatory said: If devotion to analogical reasoning were not obligatory, most incidents would be devoid of rulings. The consequent is false, so the antecedent is likewise. We say: We do not concede the falsity of the consequent; rather, acting upon original permissibility and the like is permissible. And even if the falsity of the consequent were conceded, we do not concede the entailment due to the possibility of specification upon every incident by general texts, so there is no void. And if it is said: General texts do not exist likewise, I say: Then rational obligation does not remain. I say: If it is said: Disagreement among mujtahids is a mercy, so do not make rulings universal for every incident, otherwise no disagreement would occur and much mercy would be lost. We say: Disagreement is not confined to analogical reasoning, because ijtihad in other things like apparent texts, subtle texts, and ambiguous texts is permissible, so opinions differ in understanding their meanings and deriving legal rulings from them. Then, that entailment is not devoid of strength, because divine rulings are based on benefits for the servants as a favor, and they vary according to variation of time and place, so they cannot be controlled except by delegation to opinion, otherwise incidents would be devoid due to insufficiency of general texts, so reflect. And it does not escape you that the variation of benefits in every time such that it does not fall under fixed controls from the Lawgiver is a point of reflection; it needs a proof to clarify that. How? The controls set by the people of ijtihad have not left any incident up to this point, so what do you think of His knowledge encompassing what is from eternity to eternity? So reflect. The deniers said: First, analogical reasoning is a path not safe from error, and reason forbids a path not safe from error, so analogical reasoning is forbidden rationally. We say: The forbiddance of reason is denied. Rather, when correctness is preponderant, reason does not forbid it, because beneficial preponderant probabilities are not abandoned due to rare minority probabilities. And since correctness in analogical reasoning is preponderant, it should not be abandoned. How can preponderant correctness be abandoned while most rational actions are for benefits not certain by induction? Then their proof is refuted by the apparent texts of the scriptures, for they are not safe due to the existence of probability, so they are not followed. And they said: Second, which is for al-Nazzam: The essence of analogical reasoning is the similarity of similar things in rulings, but the Lawgiver does not consider rulings that way, so analogical reasoning is not considered with Him, may He be exalted. The evidence that rulings are not considered that way is his statement: Difference between similar things is established, like requiring ghusl from semen but not urine, even though both are impurities exiting from one passage; and cutting the hand of a thief for a little but not a usurper for much, even though the crime of the former is lesser than the crime of the latter; and many rulings are like that. And union between different things is established, like equality between intentional and unintentional killing in ihram, even though intentional is a complete crime unlike unintentional; and like zinā and apostasy both requiring killing, even though the latter is a greater major sin than the former; and so on. And analogical reasoning would dictate the opposite, i.e., establishing union between similars and difference between differents. We say: Similars are not similar in every respect, nor are differents different in every respect; rather, it is permissible for similars to differ in the ratio (manāṭ) and for differents to agree in it. And difference is permissible due to a differentiator, so there is no similarity considering that differentiator, and union is due to a unifier, so there is no opposition considering it, absolutely. Do you not see that al-Nazzam, despite his Mu'tazilism and opposition to us, is with us in Islam? So rulings that are according to it agree that agreement is due to different causes is permissible. That is, it is permissible for one effect to be from various causes, so it is permissible for rulings of differents to be unified, so understand. And they said: Thirdly, analogical reasoning involves much disagreement, as is observed in reality, and everything that involves disagreement is not from Allah, and everything that is like that is rejected by consensus, for there is no ruling except from Allah, may He be exalted. As for the second premise, it is due to His statement, exalted be He: {And if it had been from other than Allah, they would have found in it much disagreement} [al-Nisa' 4:82]. For it indicates that what is with Allah has no disagreement, because 'law' (if) for the non-existence of the second due to the non-existence of the first, so the non-existence of the first is a cause necessitating the non-existence of the second, and it reverses by contrapositive to that premise, which is our saying: What involves disagreement is not from Allah, may He be exalted. And he said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that in the verse there is an indication of the first premise as well, and al-Taftazani explained it that it indicates that what is not from Allah involves disagreement, and it is known from external evidence that analogical reasoning is not from Allah but rather by the derivation of the mujtahid with his opinion, so it is something that involves disagreement. Then he himself objected that if this, i.e., that analogical reasoning is not from Allah, were known, there would be no need for the mentioned verse. Rather, we would append it to the third premise and the proof would be complete. I say: His explanation is not what was mentioned; rather, his explanation is that it indicates that what is from other than Allah involves disagreement explicitly, and it is known that analogical reasoning is from other than Allah, i.e., the mujtahid.
Source pages p. 362

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut (a sharh of Musallam al-Thubut) addresses the rational permissibility of qiyas (analogical reasoning) in usul al-fiqh. The author first states the Hanafi position: qiyas is rationally permissible (ja'iz 'aqlan), contrary to those who deem it obligatory (wajib) or impossible (mumtani'). The rational proof is that if qiyas were impossible, its actual occurrence (as practiced by scholars) would entail a logical contradiction, but it does not. Moreover, reason itself judges that similar cases should have similar rulings; denying this is stubbornness. The author then dismisses the objection of Abu al-Husayn (who held it wajib) as irrelevant, focusing on refuting the deniers (Shi'a and Mu'tazila). The deniers' first argument: qiyas is not safe from error, so reason forbids it. The Hanafi response: reason does not forbid probabilistic reasoning when the correct outcome is more likely; people routinely act on probabilities. Moreover, even textual evidence (zawahir al-nusus) is probabilistic, yet it is followed. Second argument (al-Nazzam): the Lawgiver does not treat similar cases alike (e.g., ghusl from semen vs. urine) and treats different cases alike (e.g., intentional and unintentional killing in ihram), thus qiyas is invalid. The response: similarities are not absolute; differences exist in the illah (effective cause). What appears as similarity may have a distinguishing factor, and what appears as difference may share a unifier. Third argument: qiyas leads to much disagreement, and anything with disagreement cannot be from Allah (citing Quran 4:82). The author discusses the logical structure of the verse: by contrapositive ('aks al-naqid), whatever has much disagreement is not from Allah. The deniers claim qiyas has disagreement, so it is not from Allah. The Hanafi response clarifies that the verse refers to the Quran itself (a text), not to human reasoning. Moreover, the premise that qiyas is 'from other than Allah' is not automatically conceded; the verse is used to prove that, but if it were already known, the argument would be circular. Ultimately, the Hanafi position is that qiyas is a valid rational method, and the objections do not hold.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning: applying a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared illah.
التعبد (al-ta'abbud): Being bound to practice an act of devotion; here meaning legitimacy of practicing qiyas.
المتماثلات (al-mutamathilat): Things that are similar; reason dictates similar cases have similar rulings.
المناط (al-manāṭ): The ratio or effective cause; the shared attribute that justifies analogical reasoning.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the deniers' arguments against qiyas as presented in the passage?
A) Qiyas is not safe from error.
B) The Lawgiver treats similar cases differently.
C) Qiyas leads to consensus among scholars.
D) Qiyas causes much disagreement.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school refutes the three main objections to qiyas by affirming that rational probability is acceptable, similarity is based on effective cause, and disagreement does not negate divine origin.

Responding to the Rational Objection to Qiyas
Response to the rational objection to qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the first objection against qiyas and apply the Hanafi usul principle that probabilistic proofs are valid in reason and shari'ah.

Core Idea
Reason does not forbid a method that yields preponderant probability; qiyas is valid despite fallibility.

This passage addresses a fundamental challenge to qiyas: since it is not certain, why should we rely on it? The Hanafi response defends qiyas by showing that both reason and revelation accept probabilistic evidence. This lesson breaks down the argument and its implications for usul al-fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Read the first part of the passage: al-munkirun claim that qiyas is 'ghayr ma'mun min al-khata' (not safe from error) and that reason forbids following such a path. This is the core of their rationalist objection.
This objection assumes that legal proofs must be certain (qat'i).
2
Articulate the Hanafi principle
The Hanafi response states that reason does not forbid a method when 'al-sawab rajihan' (correctness is preponderant). The key principle: probability (ghalabat al-zann) suffices for action in both reason and shari'ah.
The text says 'idha kana al-sawabu rajihan la yamna' al-aql'.
3
Recognize the inductive proof
The author uses induction (istiqra') to support the principle: 'Most actions of rational beings are for benefits that are not certain.' This demonstrates that uncertainty does not prevent action.
The quote 'akthar tasarrufat al-'uqala' li-fawa'id ghayr mutayaqqana' is a rational proof.
4
Refutation by analogy to texts
The passage then argues that even the apparent meanings of revealed texts (zuwar al-nusus) are not free from probabilistic interpretation, yet they are valid proofs. Similarly, qiyas should not be rejected.
This shows internal consistency: the same standard applies to Quran and Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المنكرون (قالوا : أولاً) القياس طريق غير مأمون من الخطأ والعقل يمنع من طريق غير مأمون فالقياس ممنوع عقلاً (قلنا) منع العقل ممنوع، بل إذا كان الصواب راجحاً لا يمنع العقل فإنّ المظان الأكثرية) النافعة (لا تترك بالاحتمالات (الأقلية النادرة والقياس لما كان الصواب فيه راجحاً ينبغي أن لا يترك (كيف) يترك راجح الصواب وأكثر تصرفات العقلاء لفوائد غير متيقنة بالاستقراء) ثم دليلهم منقوض بظواهر النصوص، فإنه غير مأمون لوجود الاحتمال، فلا يتبع
The rejecters said: Firstly, qiyas is a path not safe from error, and reason forbids a path not safe from error, so qiyas is rationally prohibited. We said: The prohibition of reason is itself prohibited. Rather, when correctness is preponderant, reason does not forbid it. Indeed, preponderant probable indications that are beneficial are not abandoned due to rare improbable possibilities. And since correctness in qiyas is preponderant, it should not be abandoned. How can preponderantly correct be abandoned while most rational actions are for benefits that are not certain through induction? Then their proof is invalidated by the apparent meanings of texts, for they are not safe from probability, yet they are followed. So qiyas is not to be abandoned.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the first objection of those who reject qiyas (analogical reasoning) as a source of Islamic law. Their argument is that qiyas is not immune from error, and human reason cannot accept an uncertain means of deriving law. The Hanafi response refutes this by stating that reason does not reject a method if it is predominantly correct. In fact, most human actions are based on probabilities, not certainties. Moreover, even the apparent meanings of the Qur'an and Sunnah (zuwar al-nusus) are not absolutely certain, yet they are valid proofs. Therefore, the objection fails. This is a rational argument (dalil aqli) establishing the validity of probabilistic reasoning in usul al-fiqh, a key Hanafi principle.

Key Terms
القياس (al-qiyas): Analogical reasoning in Islamic law: extending a ruling from an original case to a new case due to shared effective cause (illa).
المظان (al-mazann): Places of preponderant probability; indicators that are mostly correct.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the first objection against qiyas, what is the primary reason for accepting qiyas despite its fallibility?
A) Qiyas is always correct and never leads to error.
B) Reason does not forbid a method when its results are preponderantly correct, and most human actions are based on probabilities.
C) The Quran explicitly commands the use of qiyas.
D) The Prophet (peace be upon him) personally used qiyas in all his rulings.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts probabilistic proofs (zann) as valid in law, provided they are preponderantly probable.

Al-Nazzam's Objection to Qiyas and the Hanafi Response
Response to the objection that shari'ah does not observe similarity in rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state Al-Nazzam's objection to qiyas and explain the Hanafi response that similarity in effective cause (manat) is sufficient for analogical reasoning.

Core Idea
Qiyas does not require absolute similarity; it only requires shared effective cause (manat).

This lesson examines a classical objection to qiyas raised by the Mu'tazili scholar Al-Nazzam. He argued that the Lawgiver often differentiates between apparently similar cases and unifies apparently different ones, thus undermining the basis of analogical deduction. The Hanafi response clarifies that qiyas operates on the effective cause (manat), not on total similarity. By analyzing this dispute, we strengthen our understanding of the proper scope and method of qiyas.

Guided Path
1
Identify Al-Nazzam's objection
Al-Nazzam claims that shari'ah rulings show differences between similar cases and similarities between different cases, which contradicts the principle of qiyas.
2
Review the examples given
Examples of difference: ghusl from semen but not urine; cutting off hand of a thief of little but not usurper of much. Examples of combination: equal penalty for intentional and unintentional killing in ihram; equal death penalty for zina and apostasy.
3
Understand the Hanafi rebuttal
Similar things are not similar in every respect; they may differ in the effective cause (manat). Different things may share a manat. Thus qiyas can still hold when the manat is correctly identified.
Key phrase: 'ليس المتماثلات متماثلة من كل وجه'
4
Note the closing argument
Al-Nazzam himself agrees with Muslims on Islam despite differences, showing that different causes can yield same effects. So unity of ruling does not require unity of cause.
The text says 'جائز أن يكون لعلل شتى معلول واحد'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) قالوا (ثانياً : وهو للنظام) حاصل القياس تماثل المتماثلات بين الأحكام والشارع لم يعتبر الأحكام كذلك، فلا يكون القياس معتبراً عنده تعالى ووجه عدم اعتبار الأحكام كذلك قوله (ثبت الفرق بين المتماثلات، كإيجاب الغسل من المني دون البول) مع كونهما نجسين خارجين من سبيل واحد وقطع سارق القليل دون غاصب الكثير مع أن جناية الأول أصغر من جناية الثاني (وكثير) من الأحكام كذلك (و) ثبت الجمع بين المختلفات كالتسوية بين القتل عمداً وخطأ في الإحرام) كون العمد جناية كاملة دون الخطأ ( وكالزنا والردة كلاهما يوجبان القتل مع كون الثاني أكبر كبيرة من الأول إلى غير ذلك . والقياس) كان يقضي (بالعكس) أي بثبوت الجمع بين المتماثلات والفرق بين المختلفات (قلنا) ليس المتماثلات متماثلة من كل وجه، ولا المختلفات مختلفة من كل وجه بل يجوز اختلاف المتماثلات في المناط، واتفاق المختلفات فيه. ويجوز الفرق لفارق فلا مماثلة باعتبار ذلك الفارق (والجمع) بجامع فلا مخالفة بالنظر إليه (مطلقاً، ألا ترى النظام مع اعتزاله ومخالفته إيانا (معنا في الإسلام) فتتفق الأحكام التي بحسبه على أن الاتفاق لعلل مختلفة (جائز) يعني أنه يجوز أن يكون لعلل شتى معلول واحد، فيجوز اتحاد أحكام المختلفات فافهم .
And they said, secondly, and it is attributed to al-Nazzam: The gist of qiyas is the similarity of similar things between rulings, but the Lawgiver did not consider rulings in that way, so qiyas is not considered valid with Him. The proof that rulings are not so considered is His statement: 'Difference between similar things is established, such as requiring ghusl from semen but not from urine,' even though both are impure and exit from the same passage; and cutting off the hand of a thief of little [value] but not the usurper of much, even though the crime of the first is less than that of the second; and many rulings similarly. And combination between different things is established, such as the equality between intentional and unintentional killing in ihram, even though intentional is a complete crime while unintentional is not; and [such as] zina and apostasy both requiring the death penalty even though the second is a greater major sin than the first, and so forth. And qiyas would require the opposite, i.e., establishing combination between similar things and difference between different things. We said: Similar things are not similar in every respect, nor are different things different in every respect. Rather, it is possible for similar things to differ in the effective cause (manat), and for different things to agree in it. And difference is possible due to a distinguishing factor, so there is no similarity with respect to that factor; and combination is due to a uniting factor, so there is no difference with respect to it. Moreover, do you not see that al-Nazzam, despite his Mu'tazilism and his opposition to us, agrees with us in Islam? So rulings that [follow from] that agreement occur. And it is possible for a single effect to have different causes, so it is permissible for the rulings of different things to be the same. So understand.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage presents the second objection, attributed to the Mu'tazili scholar al-Nazzam. He argues that qiyas assumes that similar cases should have similar rulings, but the Lawgiver often differentiates between seemingly similar cases (e.g., ghusl required from semen but not from urine) and unifies seemingly different cases (e.g., intentional and unintentional killing in ihram given the same penalty). Therefore, qiyas does not reflect the Lawgiver's method. The Hanafi response is that similarity or difference is not absolute; two cases can be similar in some aspects and different in others. The effective cause (manat) is what matters. Furthermore, al-Nazzam himself agrees with Muslims on many rulings despite differing in creed, showing that different causes can produce the same effect. Hence, qiyas remains valid when the effective cause is properly identified.

Key Terms
المناط (al-manat): The effective cause or ratio legis for a ruling; the basis for analogical extension.
المتماثلات (al-mutamathilat): Things that are similar or analogous in certain respects.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response, why does the requirement of ghusl from semen but not from urine not invalidate qiyas?
A) Because the two substances are not actually similar.
B) Because the effective cause (manat) differs.
C) Because the Lawgiver can do as He wills.
D) Because the urine example is not authentic.

Key Takeaway
Qiyas is not undermined by superficial differences; it is the similarity in the effective cause that matters.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

6 min
Rebuttal of the Third Objection to Qiyas: Disagreement Among Jurists
Rebuttal to the argument that disagreement in qiyas proves it is not from Allah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between 'ikhtilaf' (juristic disagreement) and 'tanaqud' (contradiction) in the context of Quran 4:82, and explain why the existence of disagreement in qiyas does not invalidate it as a source of law.

Core Idea
Quran 4:82 negates contradiction, not all juristic disagreement; qiyas probabilistically reveals Allah's law.

Opponents of qiyas argue that jurists differ widely on rulings derived by analogy, and since the Quran says anything with much disagreement cannot be from Allah, qiyas must be rejected. In this lesson, we follow the Hanafi response: the Quranic verse refers to contradiction within a single text, not to legitimate ijtihadi differences. Moreover, qiyas is a tool for discovering divine law probabilistically, not a source of certainty, so ikhtilaf is expected.

Guided Path
1
Identify the opponent's syllogism
The opponent's argument has three premises: (1) qiyas involves much disagreement; (2) anything with much disagreement is not from Allah (supported by Quran 4:82); (3) what is not from Allah is rejected. The conclusion: qiyas is invalid. Grasp this structure before evaluating the rebuttal.
Note that the opponent treats 'ikhtilaf' in the verse as absolute disagreement.
2
Analyze the Hanafi reading of Quran 4:82
The Hanafis argue that the verse negates 'tanaqud' (contradiction) not 'ikhtilaf' (difference). The verse addresses the Quran's internal consistency: if it were from other than Allah, it would contain contradictions. It does not address derived rulings. Study the Arabic: 'iktilāfan kathīran' is interpreted as 'tanaquḍan' in context.
The term 'ikhtilaf' can mean both contradiction and divergence; the context determines the meaning.
3
Examine the logical critique of the opponent's use of the verse
The passage critiques even the opponent's own logical move. If it were already 'known' that qiyas is not from Allah, the verse would be redundant. But the Hanafis show that the verse only establishes a possibility, not necessity. The verse indicates that something from other than Allah may contain disagreement, but that does not prove qiyas is not from Allah in another sense.
This is a subtle point about necessity vs. possibility in conditional statements.
4
Understand the probabilistic nature of qiyas
The Hanafis conclude that qiyas is a kāshif (discloser) of Allah's law, but only probabilistically (ẓannan). This is similar to how the ẓāhir (apparent meaning) of the Quran can yield different interpretations. Thus, juristic differences in qiyas are natural and do not impugn its validity.
Distinguish between certainty (qaṭʿ) and probability (ẓann) in usul al-fiqh.
5
Apply the distinction to other potential objections
Now that you have learned the Hanafi response, test it: if someone says 'juristic disagreement in ijma' (consensus) should invalidate ijma',' apply the same principle. However, ijma' is by definition unanimous, so the issue differs. The key is that not all ikhtilaf is tanaqud.
This reinforces the idea that the verse's scope is limited.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(و) قالوا : (ثالثاً : القياس يوجد فيه اختلاف كثير (كما هو الواقع المشاهد وكل ما يوجد فيه اختلاف لا يكون من عند الله، وكل ما هو كذلك فهو مردود إجماعاً) إذ لا حكم إلا لله تعالى (أما) المقدمة (الثانية فلقوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَوْ كَانَ مِنْ عِندِ غَيْرِ اللَّهِ لَوَجَدُوا فِيهِ اخْتِلَافًا كَثِيرًا ﴾ [النساء : ۸۲] فإنه دل على أن ما عند الله لا يوجد فيه اختلاف لأنّ لو لانتفاء الثاني لأجل انتفاء الأول، فانتفاء الأول سبب يلزمه انتفاء الثاني وينعكس بعكس النقيض إلى تلك المقدمة وهي قولنا ما يوجد فيه اختلاف ليس من عند الله تعالى (و) قال (في) شرح المختصر أن في الآية إشارة إلى المقدمة الأولى أيضاً، وقرره التفتازاني بأنها دلت على أن ما ليس من عند الله يوجد فيه الاختلاف ومعلوم من الخارج (أن القياس ليس من عند الله بل بإخراج المجتهد برأيه، فهو مما يوجد فيه اختلاف (ثم أورد) هو نفسه بأنه لو كان هذا أي كون القياس لا من الله معلوماً لما احتيج إلى الآية) المذكورة (بل نضمه إلى المقدمة (الثالثة : ويتم الدليل (أقول) ليس تقريره ما ذكر بل تقريره أنها دلت على أن ما من عند غير الله ففيه اختلاف بصريحها. ومعلوم أن القياس من عند غير الله) وهو المجتهد (وهذا لا يستلزم ضرورة أن لا يكون من عند الله وإن استلزم بنظر دقيق (حتى يضم إلى) المقدمة (الثالثة) وإنما لا يستلزم ضرورة الجواز أن يكون شيء من شيئين) جوازاً عقلياً وإن لم يكن وقوعياً فلا بد من الرجوع إلى الآية لإثبات ما يضم إليها كما مر ) وإن قيل : يمكن إثباته بوجه آخر، قلت: لا يجب على الناظر تعيين الطريق (قلنا : المنفي هو التناقض أو الاضطراب المخل بالبلاغة عن القرآن الشريف، لا الاختلاف مطلقاً فإن اختلاف الأحكام) ثابت (لا ريب فيه فليس الآية مما نحن فيه والقياس أيضاً كاشف عما عند الله، لكن ظناً كظاهر الكتاب فافهم .
And they said, thirdly: Qiyas involves much disagreement, as is witnessed. And everything in which there is disagreement is not from Allah, and everything that is thus is rejected by consensus, for there is no ruling except from Allah Almighty. As for the second premise, it is due to His saying: 'If it had been from other than Allah, they would have found in it much disagreement' (Quran 4:82). This indicates that what is from Allah has no disagreement, because 'law' [conditional] indicates the negation of the second due to the negation of the first. The negation of the first necessarily entails the negation of the second, and by contrapositive we get the premise: what is found in it disagreement is not from Allah. And he said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar that there is in the verse an indication to the first premise as well. Al-Taftazani explained that it indicates that what is not from Allah has disagreement, and it is known externally that qiyas is not from Allah but is derived by the mujtahid's opinion, so it is something that has disagreement. Then he himself objected: If this (i.e., that qiyas is not from Allah) were known, there would be no need for the mentioned verse. Instead, one would join it to the third premise and the proof would be complete. I say: His explanation is not as mentioned. Rather, his explanation is that it indicates explicitly that what is from other than Allah has disagreement. And it is known that qiyas is from other than Allah (i.e., the mujtahid). But this does not necessarily entail that it is not from Allah, even if it entails by a precise look. So that one may join it to the third premise. But it does not necessarily entail by necessity; it only makes it possible that a thing can be from two sources, rationally possible even if not actual. So one must return to the verse to establish what is joined to it, as mentioned. And if it is said that it can be established in another way, I say: The researcher is not obligated to specify the path. We said: What is negated is contradiction or disorder that impairs eloquence from the noble Quran, not disagreement in general. For disagreement in rulings is established without doubt. So the verse is not about our issue. And qiyas also discloses what is with Allah, but as a probability, like the apparent meaning of the Book. So understand.
Source pages p. 363

Commentary
This passage deals with the third objection: that qiyas causes much disagreement among jurists, and according to the Quranic verse (4:82), anything with much disagreement cannot be from Allah, so qiyas is rejected. The Hanafi response has several layers. First, the verse refers to internal contradiction in a single discourse, not to legitimate juristic divergence. Second, qiyas is a method of discovering Allah's law, not a source itself; it reveals what is with Allah probabilistically. Third, the logical structure of the opponents' argument is discussed, with a critique of Taftazani's interpretation. Ultimately, the Hanafi position is that disagreement in qiyas does not invalidate it, because such disagreement is about probability, not contradiction.

Key Terms
الاختلاف (al-ikhtilaf): Disagreement or divergence among jurists in legal rulings.
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Logical contradiction or inconsistency within a single source.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi response to the third objection, what type of disagreement does the Quranic verse (4:82) negate?
A) Any disagreement among jurists.
B) Disagreement about the use of qiyas.
C) Contradiction or disorder that impairs eloquence, not valid juristic differences.
D) Disagreement in matters of 'ibadat only.

Key Takeaway
The Quranic verse (4:82) negates contradiction, not all juristic disagreement; thus, disagreement in qiyas does not invalidate it as a source of law.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

8 min
Proving the Legislation of Qiyas: The Hanafi Istinbat from Quran 59:2 and Rational Necessity
Establishing qiyas as a legislated source through istinbat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to construct and justify the Hanafi argument for the legislation of qiyas using both scriptural (Quran 59:2) and rational proofs, and evaluate the counter-arguments.

Core Idea
Qiyas is legislated: the general command 'i'tibar in Quran 59:2 and rational necessity prove it.

This passage tackles a foundational issue: is qiyas actually a legislated method? While some deny it, the Hanafi school affirms it. We will walk through the key proof from Quran 59:2 and the rational necessity argument, engaging with objections from the Zahiri school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Issue and Positions
State the question: Is ta'abbud bi al-qiyas waqi' (actually legislated)? Note that Dawud al-Zahiri denies it in 'ibadat, while al-Qasani and al-Nahrawani accept only when the 'illa is explicit. The majority (including Hanafis) affirm it by scripture, and some add rational proof.
2
Analyze the Scriptural Proof (Quran 59:2)
Locate the verse 'فَاعْتَبِرُوا يَأْولي الْأَبْصَرِ'. Identify the key word 'i'tibar. Classify the text as 'amm (general command) because the word is general, covering both admonition and analogical reasoning.
The Hanafi principle 'al-ibra li 'umum al-lafz' is applied.
3
Derive the Ruling through Istinbat
Apply the general command to include qiyas. The verse commands 'i'tibar, and since the term includes legal analogy, qiyas is legislated.
This is the core istinbat: from the general word to the specific ruling.
4
Examine the Rational Proof
Consider the argument: seeking knowledge of legal rulings is fard by consensus. Qiyas produces such knowledge, so the means (qiyas) is also fard. Therefore, qiyas must be legislated.
This rational argument supplements the scriptural proof.
5
Respond to Objections
Address the Zahiri objection that 'i'tibar only means admonition. Use the principle that the word is general and admonition is just one type. Also respond to the charge that the proof is probabilistic, clarifying that the scriptural proof is probabilistic but the rational proof is definitive.
Understanding counter-arguments strengthens the derivation.

المعنى والاتعاظ نوع منه فيحمل على العموم، وليس له اختصاص بالقياس العقلي، بل هو أيضاً نوع منه، ولا يرجع الحاصل إلى ما ذكرتم، بل إلى أنا فعلنا ما هو
Issue: That ta'abbud (legislative enactment) — i.e., the ta'abbud by qiyas which was permissible — is actually occurred, definitely. Contrary to Dawud al-Zahiri, al-Qasani, and al-Nahrawani, for although they permitted ta'abbud by it rationally, they prevented it scripturally. It is narrated that Dawud denied qiyas in 'ibadat specifically, not in mu'amalat. From al-Qasani and al-Nahrawani, it is that it is actual if the 'illa is explicitly stated, even by indication (ima'), and they only denied it beyond that. As for those who affirm occurrence, the majority of them affirm occurrence by scripture (sam'), and a group from the Hanafis and Shafi'is said it occurs also by reason ('aql), and this is the chosen view. Then the scriptural proof is definitive (qat'i) according to most of those who affirm, contrary to Abu al-Husayn, for he says it is probabilistic. If you say: He previously stated that it is rationally obligatory, and here he says it is probabilistic; there is contradiction between them. It is said: This — i.e., the probabilistic nature of the occurrence of ta'abbud — does not contradict the rational obligation of ta'abbud by it, for a thing is first obligatory then occurs, so it is possible for its obligation to be definitive and its occurrence to be probable. I say: The meaning of the obligation of ta'abbud according to him is that it is obligatory upon the Lawgiver or from Him in view of the eternal wisdom established for Him, and whatever is obligatory upon the Lawgiver or from Him occurs definitely. So the definitiveness of obligation is concomitant with the definitiveness of occurrence, and contradicting the concomitant is contradictory to the original, so contradiction is necessary. So the more appropriate answer is that the definitiveness of occurrence according to him is by reason, while the scriptural evidence for it is probabilistic, meaning he did not say the occurrence is probabilistic, but rather that the scriptural proof for it is only probabilistic, and it is possible for it to be definitively known by rational proof. For us, first: just as I say qiyas is a proof for a legal ruling and produces it, and everything like that, ta'abbud by it is actual, because seeking knowledge of legal rulings is an obligation by consensus, so seeking that by which knowledge is attained is also an obligation. As for the probative force (hujjiyya), it is because it provides affirmation of the legal ruling. And therefore, because it provides affirmation, the philosophers and theologians have used it to establish some of their claims. However, if the original is rational (as in philosophy and theology), then the branch is also rational; if the original is legal (shar'i), then the branch is legal. So then it is a proof for the legal ruling. And secondly for us, His statement: 'So take heed, O people of insight' (Quran 59:2). For if it is said: what is meant here is taking admonition ('itaz), not qiyas; otherwise the meaning would be that Allah did to Banu al-Nadir what He did, so compare rice to barley — which is as you see. Also, 'i'tibar is apparently used for rational qiyas, not legal qiyas, like measuring the need of the world for a Creator to the need of a building for a builder. He said: i.e., refer something to its counterpart in its effective cause (manat) in similar cases and others, because consideration is given to the generality of the word (al-ibra li 'umum al-lafz). The word 'i'tibar is defined for this meaning, and taking admonition is a type of it, so it is understood as general. It is not specific to rational qiyas; rather, it is also a type of it. And the conclusion does not come to what you mentioned, but rather that we did that which is...
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage addresses the crucial question: Is qiyas actually legislated (waqi') as a valid method? The author presents the positions: Dawud al-Zahiri rejects it in 'ibadat, while al-Qasani and al-Nahrawani accept it only when the 'illa is explicitly stated. The majority affirm it by scriptural proof (sam'an), with some Hanafis and Shafi'is adding rational proof. The author then discusses the nature of the obligation and occurrence of ta'abbud, clarifying that the rational obligation (wujub 'aqli) does not conflict with probabilistic scriptural evidence for its occurrence. Two proofs are given: (1) Qiyas is a proof that produces legal rulings, and since seeking knowledge of these rulings is fard, so is the means (qiyas). (2) Quran 59:2 commands 'i'tibar, which is argued to mean analogical reasoning (qiyas), not mere admonition, because the general word 'i'tibar includes both. Thus qiyas is established as a legislated source.

Key Terms
التعبد (al-ta'abbud): Legislative enactment; the act of making something a prescribed religious practice.
الاعتبار (al-i'tibar): Taking heed; analogical reasoning by considering similar cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which Quranic verse does the Hanafi school use as a primary scriptural proof for the legislation of qiyas?
A) Quran 4:82
B) Quran 59:2
C) Quran 2:282
D) Quran 49:6

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school proves the legislation of qiyas through both the general command in Quran 59:2 and the rational necessity of having a method to derive legal rulings.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
8 min
The Reality of Qiyas: Evidence from the Quran and Rational Proof
Dispute over whether qiyas is actually established as a source of law

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi position on the reality of qiyas, evaluate the Quranic proof from verse 59:2, and defend the Hanafi interpretation against Zahiri objections.

Core Idea
Qiyas is a binding source of law, established by revelation and reason, despite Zahiri objections.

This passage addresses a foundational usul dispute: is the obligation to use analogical reasoning (qiyas) actually established as a binding source of law? The author presents the Hanafi position that qiyas is established both by revelation and reason, and provides the Quranic verse 'fa'tabiru' as key evidence, responding to objections from the Zahiri school. The passage also includes a rational argument based on the obligation to seek legal knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Identify the dispute
Determine the central issue: whether ta'abbud bi al-qiyas (the binding nature of analogical reasoning) is actually established. Note the key positions: Hanafi (majority) affirm it, Zahiri and others restrict or deny it.
2
Analyze the rational argument
Examine the first proof: seeking knowledge of legal rulings is obligatory by consensus, and qiyas produces such knowledge, so it must be binding. Consider the objection that this only shows permissibility, not obligation.
This argument is based on the principle of 'what is necessary to fulfill an obligation is itself obligatory'.
3
Examine the Quranic proof (59:2)
Focus on the verse 'fa'tabiru ya uli al-absar'. Understand the Hanafi claim that 'i'tibar' is general and includes qiyas, and the fa' of subordination indicates causation. Study the rebuttals to objections that it means only admonition or rational analogy.
The linguistic debate centers on the scope of 'i'tibar' and the function of the letter 'fa'.
4
Synthesize the Hanafi tarjih
After considering the objections and responses, conclude why the Hanafi position is the chosen view (al-mukhtar). The passage argues that the fa' entails full entailment (istilzam) linguistically, and the command is general.
The author also mentions the hadith of Mu'adh as a third proof, though not detailed here.

على أن العلم بوجود السبب يوجب الحكم بوجود المسبب) فيجب في كل ما هو سبب ومسبب (وهو) معنى القياس الشرعي) وهذا هو التقرير الذي عبر عنه الإمام فخر الإسلام بالدليل المعقول (وأورد في التلويح) أن هذا إنما يتم التفريع على أن ما قبله سبب تام و أن الفاء بل صريح الشرط، والجزاء لا يقتضي العلية التامة ( بل) إنما يقتضي (الدخل في الجملة، فلا يدل على أن كل من علم بوجود السبب يجب عليه الحكم بوجود المسبب . أقول في الجواب (لو صح هذا أي عدم إيجاب التفريع بالفاء تمامية المتفرع عليه بل المداخلة في الجملة (الصح، ثم كالفاء في الجزاء، لأن الدخل في الجملة لا ينافي التراخي فإنّ المعلول يتخلف عن العلة الناقصة كثيراً، ويمكن لمن ليس له ذريعة فوق الجدال أن يقول : هذا قياس في اللغة فإنّ الحروف وإن كان معانيها متقاربة إلا أنهم وضعوا بعضها لأن يستعمل في محل دون الآخر، ألا ترى أن معنى ما ولا ولم واحد وما لا لنفي الجنس، ولم لا يدخل إلا على المضارع ، وهكذا فيجوز أن يكون حال كلمة الفاء وثم كذلك، فالفاء وإن كان لا ينافي التراخي ويفيد مطلق الدخل لكن وضعت لدخولها على الأجزية دون غيرها، وإن قرب معناه ولما كان لهذا توهم أردف المصنف ما يقطع الإيراد عن أصله وقال : (بل الصحيح أن الفاء يستلزم الاستلزام) أي استلزام الأول للثاني (لغة كما في) شرح (الرضى) للكافية . ثم لما كان يورد على الدليل أيضاً أن الأمر يجوز أن يكون للندب، فلا يفيد وجوب التعبد أو للمرة فلم يلزم إلا التعبد مرة وهو يتحقق في ضمن الاتعاظ الواجب خصوصاً أيضاً، أو يكون الأمر للحاضرين فقط، فلا يجب التعبد به علينا. أجاب بقوله (ثم كون الأمر للندب أو المرة أو للحاضرين فقط ونحو ذلك احتمالات مردودة) لا ينبغي أن يلتفت إليها. أما الأول فلأنه لو كان كذلك لندب الاتعاظ وغيره من الاعتبارات، وأما الثاني فيأبى عنه التفريع، فإنه يوجب العلية والتكرر وكذا المقام، وأما الثالث فلأن الشريعة المطهرة عامة هذا. واعلم أنه لعل مرادهم بالقطع الذي ادعى من قبل القطع بالمعنى الأعم، وهو الذي يقطع احتمالاً ناشئاً عن دليل، ولو كان احتمالاً ناشئاً عن غير دليل مما يعد في العرف واللغة كلا احتمال فلا ينافي وجود مطلق الاحتمال البعيد عرفاً ولغة ولو لم يكن المراد هذا، بل المعنى الأخص ، لما صح الاستدلال بهذه الآية فإنّ احتمال التجوز وإرادة الاتعاظ وعدم الفاء في اللزوم قائم، ولو كان بعيداً يعدّ عرفاً كلا احتمال وينسب العرف لمبديه ما يكره فافهم (و) لنا (ثالثاً حديث معاذ وقد تقدم، وهو يدل على الاجتهاد بالرأي، وأورد عليه أن الاجتهاد بالرأي غير منحصر في القياس، بل يجوز أن يكون بنحو آخر، كالاجتهاد في تأويل
Issue: That ta'abbud (binding nature), i.e., ta'abbud by qiyas which was permissible, is actually established definitively, contrary to Dawood al-Zahiri, al-Qasani, and al-Nahrawani, for they, although they permitted ta'abbud by it rationally, prevented it by revelation. And it is reported from Dawood that he denied qiyas in acts of worship specifically, not in transactions. From al-Qasani and al-Nahrawani it is reported that it is established when the 'illa is textually stated, even by indication, and they denied it beyond that. As for those who affirm occurrence, the majority affirm occurrence by revelation, and a group of Hanafis and Shafi'is said it occurs by reason as well, and that is the chosen view. Then the revealed evidence is definitive according to most of the proponents, contrary to Abu al-Husayn, who says it is speculative. If it is said: He previously stated obligation by reason, and here he says speculative, and there is contradiction between them. It is said: This, i.e., the speculativeness of the occurrence of ta'abbud, does not negate the obligation of ta'abbud by reason, for a thing is obligatory first then occurs, so it is possible that its obligation is certain and its occurrence is speculative. I say: The meaning of obligation of ta'abbud with him is that it is obligatory upon the Lawgiver or from Him, in view of the eternal wisdom established for Him, and what is obligatory upon the Lawgiver or from Him occurs certainly. So the certainty of obligation necessitates certainty of occurrence, and negating the necessary negates the necessitator, so contradiction is necessary. So the best answer is that the certainty of occurrence with him is by reason, while the revealed evidence indicating it is speculative, meaning he did not say speculativeness of occurrence, but speculativeness of the revealed evidence indicating it only, and it is possible that it is certain by rational evidence. For us: First: As I say, qiyas is a proof for a legal ruling and produces it, and everything like that is that ta'abbud by it is actual because seeking knowledge of legal rulings is obligatory by consensus, so seeking what obtains knowledge is also obligatory. As for the proof (hujiyya), it is because it gives certainty of the legal ruling. Therefore, i.e., because it gives certainty, the philosophers and theologians established it to prove some of their claims, but if the original is rational, as in philosophy and theology, then the derivative is also rational, and if it is legal, then it is legal, i.e., the derivative is legal. So it is proof of a legal ruling. Second: The saying of Allah the Exalted: {So take warning, O you with eyesight} [59:2]. If it is said: What is meant here is admonition, not qiyas, otherwise the meaning would be that Allah did to Banu al-Nadir what He did, so compare rice to barley, which is as you see. Also, i'tibar is clearly used for rational qiyas, not legal, like comparing the world's need for a maker to the builder's need for a builder. He said: i.e., refer the thing to its counterpart in its ratio (manat) in retributions and other matters because the consideration is for the generality of the wording. The word i'tibar is assigned to this meaning, and admonition is a type of it, so it is taken generally, and it is not specific to rational qiyas, but it is also a type of it. The outcome is not what you mentioned, but rather that We did what We did, so compare matters to their likes, O you people of insight. So it includes qiyas of our actions on their actions in the arrival of recompense, thus admonition is obtained, and this meaning is extremely subtle and eloquent. And if it is taken only for admonition without the general, it also indicates qiyas by textual implication (dalalat al-nass), as Sadr al-Shari'ah said in al-Tawdih, because the fa' of subordination in His saying {fa'tabiru} indicates that the previous story is the cause for the obligation of admonition, based on the idea that knowledge of the existence of the cause necessitates judgment of the existence of the effect, so it is necessary in everything that is cause and effect, and that is the meaning of legal qiyas. This is the exposition that Imam Fakhr al-Islam called the rational proof. It was objected in al-Talwih that this subordination is only complete if what precedes is a complete cause, and the fa' is purely conditional conjunction, not necessitating complete causation, but only partial interference, so it does not indicate that everyone who knows the existence of a cause must judge the existence of the effect. I say in response: If this were correct, i.e., that the fa' of subordination does not necessitate the completeness of what is subordinated but only partial interference, then it would be valid, then it would be like the fa' in the conditional clause, because partial interference does not negate delay, for the effect often lags behind the incomplete cause. And someone without excessive argumentation could say: This is a linguistic analogy (qiyas fi al-lugha), for letters, although their meanings are close, they are placed for use in one context not another. Do you not see that the meanings of 'ma', 'la', and 'lam' are one, but 'ma' is for negation of the genus, and 'lam' only enters upon the imperfect tense, and so on. Thus it is possible that the case of the word 'fa'' and 'thumma' is similar. So the fa', although it does not negate delay and indicates absolute interference, is placed for entry upon consequences (jaza') not other things, even if its meaning is close. Since this could be conjectured, the author appended what cuts off the objection at its root and said: Rather, the correct view is that the fa' necessitates entailment, i.e., entailment of the first by the second, linguistically, as in Sharh al-Radi on al-Kafiya. Then, since it might be objected to the proof that the command could be for recommendation, so it does not necessitate obligation of ta'abbud, or for once, so it only necessitates ta'abbud once, which is realized within the obligatory admonition specifically, or that the command is only for those present, so it is not obligatory upon us. He responded by saying: The command being for recommendation, or for once, or for those present only, and the like, are rejected probabilities that should not be heeded. As for the first, because if it were so, it would recommend admonition and other i'tibars. As for the second, the subordination prevents it, for it necessitates causation and repetition, and so is the context. As for the third, because the pure Shari'ah is universal. And know that perhaps what they mean by certainty, which was previously claimed, is certainty in the broader sense, which cuts off a probability arising from evidence, even if it is a probability arising from non-evidence, which in custom and language is considered no probability, so it does not negate the existence of an absolute improbable probability customarily and linguistically. And if this were not meant, but the narrower sense, the inference by this verse would not be valid, because the possibility of figurative interpretation and intending admonition and the fa' not indicating necessitation exist, even if improbable, customarily considered no probability, and custom attributes to its initiator what he dislikes. Understand. And thirdly, for us is the hadith of Mu'adh, which has preceded, and it indicates ijtihad by opinion. It was objected that ijtihad by opinion is not confined to qiyas, but could be by another method, such as ijtihad in interpretation.
Source pages p. 364

Commentary
This passage is a usul dispute concerning the reality (wuqu') of ta'abbud bi al-qiyas — i.e., whether analogical reasoning is actually established as a binding source of law. The author presents the view of the majority that qiyas is established by Revelation (sam'), with some Hanafis and Shafi'is adding that it is also established by reason. The dissenting positions of Dawood al-Zahiri (who denied qiyas in 'ibadat) and others who limit it to cases where the 'illa is textually stated are noted. A key nuance is the disagreement over the certainty of the revealed proof: most say it is qat'i, while Abu al-Husayn al-Basri holds it is zanni. The text resolves an apparent contradiction in Abu al-Husayn's position by distinguishing between the certainty of obligation (by reason) and the speculativeness of the revealed evidence. The primary proof presented is the Qur'anic verse 'fa'tabiru ya uli al-absar' (59:2). The author defends its use as evidence for qiyas against several objections: that 'i'tibar' means admonition, not analogy; that it applies only to rational analogy; and that the letter 'fa'' indicates only partial causation, not full entailment. He counters by arguing that 'i'tibar' is a general term encompassing both admonition and analogy, and the 'fa'' of subordination implies complete causation linguistically. He also mentions the hadith of Mu'adh (though not quoted in full) as a third proof. The commentary underscores the Hanafi commitment to qiyas as a legitimate and binding source, while carefully addressing linguistic and rational critiques.

Key Terms
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning: applying a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to shared effective cause ('illa).
تعبد (ta'abbud): The binding nature or obligation to act upon a proof; here specifically referring to the binding status of qiyas as a method of legal derivation.
اعتبار (i'tibar): Linguistically, considering something to derive a lesson; technically in this verse, it encompasses both admonition (itti'az) and analogical deduction (qiyas).
فاء التفريع (fa' al-tafri'): The letter 'fa' used for subordination, indicating that what follows is a consequence or effect of what precedes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Hanafi interpretation of the letter 'fa' in the verse 'fa'tabiru' (59:2)?
A) It indicates a simple sequence.
B) It implies full causation (istilzam).
C) It is for emphasis only.
D) It has no legal implication.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses the Quranic command 'fa'tabiru' and the rational necessity of seeking knowledge to establish that qiyas is a binding source of law, overcoming Zahiri objections through linguistic analysis.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
15 min
The Fourth Proof for Qiyas: Tawatur and Ijma of the Sahaba
Proof of qiyas legitimacy from Sahaba practice and implicit ijma

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi proof for qiyas from Sahaba practice, identify the role of tawatur and implicit ijma, and refute common objections.

Core Idea
The Sahaba's widespread use of qiyas without denial proves its authority via tawatur and implicit ijma.

In this passage, the author presents the fourth proof for the validity of qiyas as a source of Islamic law. He argues that the Companions (Sahaba) – especially the Rightly Guided Caliphs – regularly employed analogical reasoning when no explicit text existed, and their collective practice was transmitted en masse (tawatur). Moreover, their silent acceptance of each other's analogies constitutes implicit consensus (ijma sukuti). This lesson examines the evidence and the Hanafi response to objections, strengthening your understanding of how usul al-fiqh establishes authority.

Guided Path
1
Identify the core claim
The Hanafi claim is that qiyas is proven by tawatur of Sahaba practice and their implicit ijma. Understand the meaning of 'tawatur' and why it yields certainty.
Tawatur here is in meaning (ma'nawi), not literal.
2
Distinguish the common factor from individual reports
Even though each specific incident is a khabar wahid, the common factor – the practice of qiyas – is mutawatir. This is key to the proof.
The author emphasizes that the common factor (al-qadr al-mushtarak) is mutawatir.
3
Examine the examples of Sahaba using qiyas
The author lists examples: Abu Bakr on zakat, Umar on divorce, Ali on drunkard, etc. For each, identify the original case (asl) and the new case (far').
These examples illustrate the widespread nature of qiyas among Sahaba.
4
Analyze the refutation of objections
The author addresses three main objections: (a) reports are ahad, (b) Sahaba might have had hidden texts, (c) silence may be due to fear. See how tawatur and historical evidence are used to refute them.
Pay attention to the response regarding Sahaba character and frequent opposition to caliphs.
5
Conclude the proof's weight
Why does the author assert that this proof yields certain knowledge (qat'i) about the authority of qiyas? How does it complement other proofs?
The proof is considered definitive (qat'i) in establishing qiyas as a source.

مكابرة، ونسبة المعاداة إلى الخلفاء الراشدين بمخالفة ما اتخذوه مذهباً حماقة عظيمة، فإنهم كانوا ألين للحق. ومن تتبع التواريخ والسير علم علماً قاطعاً أنهم كانوا يخالفون قول الخلفاء كثيراً. وإذا لم يكن And) for us (fourthly): mass transmission from the Companions (may Allah be pleased with them) the mujtahid just ones, of acting by it (qiyas) when there is no text. Even though the details (i.e., the details of their actions) are ahad, yet the common factor is mutawatir. And custom is like that with the existence of the decisive proof of its authority and knowledge of it. So this is a proof in reality by the decisive proof that was a judge among them, and their widespread practice is evidence for it. (And also) it was prevalent among them to argue by it and to discuss it and to give precedence to it when there was conflict, without any one denier. And custom dictates that silence in such a matter of general principles binding for action is (implicit) agreement. And this is a proof by their very consensus on its authority, for they acted by it and argued by it without denial. And he pointed to refuting what is objected that if consensus is silent, it only yields zann and does not avail in principles. (He replied) that it is necessary knowledge that all were in agreement, and their silence was due to agreement, for silence regarding such a principle is only from approval. (Among that) is the argument that the best of mankind after the prophets, Abu Bakr al-Siddiq (RA), analogized zakat to salah in fighting and said: By Allah, I will fight whoever differentiates between salah and zakat – narrated by the two shaykhs. So they referred to him and accepted his qiyas, and this is consensus from them on the authority of qiyas. And that Siddiq inherited (the mother of the mother without the mother of the father; it was said in al-Taysir: the speaker was Abd al-Rahman ibn Sahl: 'You left one who if she were the deceased would inherit all' so he made them equal in the sixth, so he took the analogy of this speaker. And in Sharh al-Siraji, the speaker was the mother of the father. And this does not contradict the report's authenticity and hearing from Muhammad ibn Salamah, as is not hidden. And the Commander of the Faithful Umar (RA) ruled regarding the irrevocably divorced woman during the husband's terminal illness by opinion. And that Commander referred back in the case of killing by a group for one to the opinion of the Commander of the Faithful Ali (RA) when he said: What if a group cooperated in theft, would you cut off their hands? He said: Yes. He said: Likewise here. As in al-Hashiyah. And the Commander of the Faithful Uthman (RA) said to Umar: If you follow your opinion, it is correct, and if you follow the opinion of the one before you, what an excellent opinion! So he permitted action by opinion. And the Commander of the Faithful Ali analogized the drunkard to the slanderer in the hadd penalty, and they all agreed on it, as previously. And he (RA) said: My opinion and the opinion of Umar met regarding the umm walad, as previously. And Ibn Mas'ud (whom the Companions were ordered to adhere to his covenant) analogized the death of the husband of the mufawwidah to the death of another husband, as his story was previously presented. And they differed regarding the inheritance of the grandfather with siblings by opinion. Imam Abu Hanifah narrated in his Musnad from Ali (RA) that he said to Umar when he consulted about the grandfather with siblings: Do you not see, O Commander of the Faithful, if a tree has two branches sprouting from a branch, which is closer to one of the two branches: the one that emerged from it or the tree? And Zayd ibn Thabit said: If a stream has a channel branching from it, then from the channel two channels, which is closer: one of the two channels to its companion or the stream? Their purpose was to prove the inheritance of the brother with the grandfather by analogy to the inheritance of other agnates due to the common factor of closeness in kinship. The tree and stream are representations of closeness, not that it is the qiyas in dispute. And it is established from Ibn Abbas that he sent to Zayd ibn Thabit and said: Does Zayd not fear Allah? He makes the son's son a son, but does not make the father's father a father. So observe his severity when he opposed this qiyas, so understand. (And that) i.e., arguing by qiyas, is more, as narrated in lengthy books of sirah. Know that in this proof there was ambiguity for the deceitful, and some of its refutation has been indicated. We will mention most of them: Among them: We do not accept that any of the Companions performed qiyas, and what you have transmitted are ahad reports that do not yield certainty, so they may be unsound. Among them: What you have transmitted from them does not clearly prove that their fatwas were by qiyas; rather, it is possible they had clear or hidden texts that they did not mention. Among them: Even if we accept that their fatwas were by qiyas, they were for specific partial analogies of a certain type, so they do not prove the validity of analogical reasoning. And the answer to these: All the transmitted reports, even though each one is a khabar wahid, yet the common factor among them – that is, fatwa by qiyas and that their custom was that – is mutawatir, producing knowledge by frequent study of their judgments and histories. And it is also known by the repetition of their practice of analogies that it was not specific to a type or individual. And it is also known by decisive indications that they had no text. And also, customary necessity dictates that if they had a text, they would have cited it in their fatwas and shown it. Denying this is obstinacy. And what we have transmitted from them are multiple incidents as examples, so the lack of clear indication of some of them being by qiyas does not harm. And all of this he indicated by his saying 'tawatur'. And by this the corruption of what is in al-Mahsul is apparent: that the transmitters are ten or twenty and that does not yield tawatur, so this definitive issue is not proven. And there is no need for what he answered that the issue is zanni practical, where zann suffices. Among them: Even if we accept that action by qiyas is established from some Companions, it does not necessitate consensus. Rather, it would necessitate only if their silence was for approval; it could be for fear. Al-Nazzam said: Only a few Companions acted by it, but since they were like Umar, Uthman, and Ali, who were rulers, others feared opposing them, because custom dictates enmity against those who oppose a taken opinion. So there is no consensus at all. And the answer: Frequent silence in countless incidents is customarily only from approval, especially in matters that are a principle of religion. This silence, secretly and openly, from everyone in every incident, and their adherence to the rulings of the Rightly Guided Caliphs arising from analogies, yields customary knowledge that is necessarily of approval and agreement. The delusion of attributing fear to them is fabrication. For their noble character, which is mutawatir, is that they did not fear anyone in religious matters, especially over a long period. Denying this is obstinacy. And attributing enmity to the Rightly Guided Caliphs for opposing what they had taken as a madhhab is great foolishness, for they were more receptive to truth. Whoever follows histories and sirah knows with decisive knowledge that they often opposed the sayings of the caliphs. And if they had no fear in opposing in many incidents, what fear would they have in one incident? And among them:
Source pages p. 366

Commentary
This passage presents the fourth proof for the authority of qiyas (analogical reasoning) as a source of Islamic law. The author argues from the practice of the Companions (Sahaba). He claims there is tawatur (mass transmission) that the Companions, especially the mujtahids among them, acted by qiyas when no explicit text (nass) existed. Even though the specific reports of individual instances (tafasil) are ahad (single-transmitter), the common factor — i.e., that they practiced qiyas — is mutawatir and yields certain knowledge. Additionally, the Companions' widespread debate, preference, and use of qiyas without any denial constitutes implicit ijma (consensus). The author supports this with several examples: Abu Bakr's analogy of zakat to salah in fighting apostates; his inheritance ruling regarding grandmothers; Umar's rulings by opinion; Uthman's approval of opinion; Ali's analogy of the drunkard to the slanderer for hadd; and Ibn Mas'ud's analogy regarding the mufawwidah. The dispute between Ali and Zayd ibn Thabit on the inheritance of the grandfather with siblings is also presented as an example of qiyas. The author then refutes objections: that the reports are ahad and not certain; that the Companions might have had hidden texts; that these were specific analogies not general; that silence could be due to fear of rulers. He counters that the cumulative effect of numerous reports yields tawatur, that custom and necessity prove they had no texts, that the repetition of qiyas in diverse cases shows it was a general method, and that the well-known character of the Sahaba and their frequent opposition to caliphs negates any fear of opposition. Thus, the proof establishes qiyas as a valid source of law.

Key Terms
تواتر (tawatur): Mass transmission of reports that yields certain knowledge; here referring to the common factor of Sahaba's practice of qiyas being transmitted by so many that it becomes definitive.
إجماع سكوتي (ijma sukuti): Silent consensus: when some mujtahids opine and others remain silent, their silence is considered implicit approval if the issue is widespread and no fear is present.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning: extending a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far') due to shared effective cause (illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main claim made by the Hanafi author in the fourth proof for qiyas?
A) Qiyas is proven by a single clear text from the Quran.
B) The Sahaba's widespread practice of qiyas without denial constitutes tawatur and implicit ijma.
C) Qiyas is only valid if explicitly permitted by the Prophet in a hadith.
D) The Sahaba never used qiyas; they only relied on texts.

Key Takeaway
The Sahaba's mutawatir practice of qiyas and their silent consensus establish qiyas as a valid, proven source of Islamic law.
