Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
6 min
Preponderance of Qiyas: Definite Cause over Stated Cause
Critical refinement and initial tarjih rules

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the rules for preferring one qiyas over another based on the nature of the cause, and criticise the use of immediate understanding (tabādu?) as a valid basis for preponderance.

Core Idea
Preference in qiyas is not by immediate understanding but by objective preponderances—definite cause prevails over stated, stated over indicated.

This passage refines the earlier classification of qiyas and istihsan by rejecting the notion that strength or weakness is exclusive to either. It then explores possible conflict scenarios and introduces the first rules of qiyas preponderance: a qiyas with a definite cause (qat'ī al-‘illah) is preferred over one with a stated cause (manṣūṣ), and a stated cause is preferred over one established by indication (īmā').

Guided Path
1
Refine the classification
Understand that the earlier attribution of strength to istihsan and weakness to qiyas is reinterpreted in terms of hiddenness/apparentness, not actual strength.
Imam Fakhr al-Islam's wording is explained.
2
Identify the four-by-four conflict matrix
Recognise that qiyas and istihsan can be strong/weak and sound/corrupt in apparent/inward, leading to 16 forms. The text drops both corrupt inward and outward as having no authority.
This is a systematic mapping of possible scenarios.
3
Grasp the impossibility of genuine conflict
Sadr al-Shari'ah argues that if both qiyas and istihsan are sound inwardly or both are strong, they cannot truly conflict because that would entail contradiction in the Law. Conflict only arises from ignorance.
Thus, true conflict between two sound proofs is impossible.
4
Apply the first preponderance rule
Learn: a qiyas with a definite cause (qat'ī) is preferred over one with a stated cause (manṣūṣ), and a stated cause is preferred over one indicated (īmā'). This is a clear hierarchy based on the clarity of the cause's basis.
The text states this by consensus (ijmā').

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم الحق عدم اختصاص القوة وفساد الباطن فقط بالاستحسان وقلبه أي قوة الباطن والضعف بالقياس (وقول الإمام (فخر الإسلام سميناً ما ضعف أثره قياساً وما قوي أثره استحساناً إما مؤوّل بأنه ليس مقصوده التسمية باعتبار القوة والضعف، بل باعتبار الظهور والخفاء، بدليل ما قدمه وإنما ذكر نوعاً منهما، وإنما ذكرهما إشارة إلى معنى كلام الإمام محمد حيث قال في مواضع : أنا أخذنا بالاستحسان وتركنا القياس إن المراد بالاستحسان القياس الخفي القوي الأثر وبالقياس الجلي الضعيف الأثر، وإلى دفع ما يورد عليه إن كان القياس قوياً، فلا معنى للعدول عنه للاستحسان الذي ليس هو حجة، وإن كان ضعيفاً فلا معنى لتركه للاستحسان، بل هو واجب الترك فافهم أو اصطلاح) خاص (منه فقط) لا يشاركه فيه غيره ولا مشاحة فيه وكأنه بعيد جداً فباعتبار القوة) القياس والاستحسان إما قويان أو ضعيفان، أو القياس قوي والاستحسان ضعيف أو بالعكس) أي الاستحسان قوي والقياس ضعيف ولا ريب في رجحان القوي على الضعيف لأن من قضية العقل والإجماع تقديم الراجح على المرجوح وأما ترجيح القياس في (الأولين أي إذا كانا قويين أو ضعيفين (بالتبادر) فيكون القياس أرجح ففيه نظر) إذ لا دخل للتبادر في الرجحان أصلاً، بل ربما يكون غير المتبادر راجحاً بما ينضم إليه مما يقويه (بل) الترجيح إنما يكون (بالمرجحات الآتية إن أمكن، وبالاعتبار الآخر غير القوة والضعف كل منهما صحيح الظاهر والباطن، أو فاسدهما، أو فاسد الظاهر صحيح الباطن أو بالعكس صحيح الظاهر فاسد الباطن (فصور التعارض ستة عشر) حاصلة (من ضرب أربعة) من القياس (في أربعة من الاستحسان، لكن الأليق إسقاط فاسد الظاهر والباطن إذ لا شائبة للحجية فيه (قيل) القائل صدر الشريعة (الظاهر امتناع التعارض في الصحيحين باطناً من القياس والاستحسان، سواء كانا صحيحين ظاهراً أيضاً أو لم يكونا (والقويين) منهما أثر اللزوم التناقض في الشرع بتحققهما وهو محال . ونعم : يقع التعارض للجهل، وإذا فرضا صحيحين باطناً أو قويين باطناً فلا دخل فيه لجهلنا وعلمنا فافهم. , الترجيحات القياسية لما ذكر معارضة القياس والاستحسان وفهم منه إمكان المعارضة بين قياسين عقب البحث ببحث الترجيح (يقدم) القياس (قطعي العلة على منصوصها) الظنية (إجماعاً و) يقدم (منصوصها) من القياس صريحاً على ما ثبت فيه العلة (بالإيماء) لأن الصريح أقوى دلالة من
Then the truth is that strength and inward corruption are not exclusive to istihsan, nor its opposite (i.e., inward strength and weakness) to qiyas. And the saying of Imam Fakhr al-Islam: 'What has weak effect is qiyas? and what has strong effect is istihsan?' is either interpreted that his intention is not naming based on strength and weakness, but on appearance and hiddenness, as indicated by what he presented. He only mentioned two types and indicated the meaning of Imam Muhammad's statement where he said in places: 'We took by istihsan and left qiyas,' meaning by istihsan the hidden qiyas with strong effect, and by qiyas the apparent qiyas with weak effect. And to refute what is objected: if qiyas is strong, there is no meaning to turning away from it for istihsan which is not a proof; if it is weak, there is no meaning to leaving it for istihsan; rather, it is obligatory to leave it (the weak qiyas) for istihsan. So understand. Or it is a special terminological convention from him only, not shared by others, and there is no harm in it, though it seems very remote. So by consideration of strength, qiyas and istihsan are either both strong, both weak, or qiyas strong and istihsan weak, or the opposite (i.e., istihsan strong and qiyas weak). There is no doubt about the preponderance of strong over weak, because it is a requirement of reason and consensus to give preference to the preponderant over the preponderate. As for giving preference to qiyas in the first two (i.e., when both are strong or both weak) by tabādu? (immediate understanding) so that qiyas is preponderant, that is questionable, because tabādu? has no role in preponderance at all; rather, sometimes the non-immediate is preponderant due to what joins it to strengthen it. Rather, preponderance is only by the preponderances to come, if possible. And by the other consideration (besides strength and weakness): each of them either sound in apparent and inward, or both corrupt, or corrupt in apparent and sound in inward, or vice versa: sound in apparent and corrupt in inward. So the forms of conflict are sixteen, resulting from multiplying four (from qiyas) by four (from istihsan). However, it is more fitting to drop both corrupt in apparent and inward, as there is no element of authority in it. It was said (the speaker is Sadr al-Shari'ah): it is apparently impossible for conflict between two that are both sound inwardly from qiyas and istihsan, whether they are sound in apparent also or not, and the two that are strong in effect, because the occurrence of both in the Law would necessarily involve contradiction, which is impossible. Yes, conflict occurs due to ignorance, and if we assume both to be sound or strong inwardly, then our ignorance and knowledge have no bearing on it, so understand. The Preponderances of Qiyas: When the conflict between qiyas and istihsan was mentioned, and from it the possibility of conflict between two qiyas was understood, he followed the inquiry with a discussion of preponderance. The qiyas with a definite cause is given preference over one with a stated cause (which is presumptive), by consensus. And the qiyas with a explicitly stated cause is given preference over one where the cause is established by indication (īmā'), because explicit is stronger in indication than.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
Passage type: Critical refinement of the classification and introduction to preponderance of qiyas. The passage first refines the earlier classification: strength/inward corruption is not exclusive to istihsan or qiyas. Imam Fakhr al-Islam's wording is interpreted in terms of hiddenness/apparentness, not strength. Then it discusses the possible combinations of strength/weakness and soundness/corruption, leading to 16 possible conflict scenarios, excluding those with no authoritative basis. It then mentions that Sadr al-Shari'ah argued that genuine conflict between two sound or strong proofs is impossible. The text ends with the beginning of the section on preponderances (tarjihat) of qiyas: a qiyas with a definite cause is preferred over one with a stated (presumptive) cause, and a stated cause is preferred over one indicated by īmā'.

Key Terms
المرجحات القياسية (al-murajjihāt al-qiyāsiyyah): Considerations that give preponderance to one qiyas over another, such as clearness of the cause.
قطعي العلة (qat‘ī al-‘illah): A cause that is definite, established by certainty (qat‘).
منصوص العلة (manṣūṣ al-‘illah): A cause that is explicitly stated in a text (like a hadith or Qur'anic verse) but is presumptive (zannī).
الإيماء (al-īmā’): An inferred indication of the cause from a text, weaker than an explicit statement.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which qiyas is given preference over the other?
A) Qiyas with a stated cause over one with a definite cause
B) Qiyas with a definite cause over one with a stated cause
C) Qiyas with an indicated cause over one with a stated cause
D) Qiyas with a definite cause over one with an indicated cause

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, preponderance between conflicting qiyas is determined by the certainty of the cause: definite (qat‘ī) prevails over stated (manṣūṣ), and stated over indicated (īmā’).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
Preference Rules in Qiyas (Tarjih al-Qiyas)
Enumeration of tarjih (preference) rules between conflicting qiyas arguments.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi preference rules to resolve conflicts between two qiyas arguments and explain the dispute with the Shafi'i school on the status of simple vs compound descriptions.

Core Idea
Qiyas with a definitive cause outranks all others; within zanni causes, explicit indication trumps implied, and specific effect trumps generic.

When two qiyas arguments conflict, the Hanafi school provides a detailed hierarchy to determine which one is stronger. This passage from the usul tradition systematically enumerates these tarjih rules, covering the cause's certainty, explicitness, type of effect, and even the type of interest (maslahah). It also highlights a key intra-madhhab and inter-madhhab dispute, particularly with the Shafi'is regarding the preference for simple vs compound descriptions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary hierarchy: qat'i 'illah first
The passage begins by stating that qiyas with a definitive cause (qat'i al-'illah) is given precedence over one with a zanni (mansus) cause, by consensus. This is the highest level of preference.
This is based on certainty of transmission or consensus.
2
Distinguish between types of zanni 'illah
Among zanni causes, the explicit text (mansus) is preferred over the implied (ima'). Within ima', further ranks exist: established by apparent text, clear text, or hidden text, with the predominant (ghalib) taking precedence.
The passage mentions three ranks under ima'.
3
Analyze the effect: specific vs generic, compound vs simple
The effect of the cause on the ruling is considered: effect on the specific ruling ('ayn) is stronger than effect on the genus (jins). Compound effects (murakkab) are preferred over simple ones. Also, if effects are equal, the presence of the 'part' (juz') tips the balance.
The Hanafi position is that simple and compound are equal in strength.
4
Compare other criteria: mazannah vs hikmah, wujudi vs 'adami, hukm shar'i vs other
Presumption (mazannah) is given precedence over wisdom (hikmah) because rulings are mostly attached to presumptions. Existential causes (wujudi) are preferred over non-existential ones ('adami). A legal ruling (hukm shar'i) is preferred over other descriptions due to agreement of principles.
There is a minority view reversing the mazannah-hikmah order.
5
Understand the maslahah hierarchy and the dispute over simple vs compound
Interests are prioritized: necessary (daruriyyah) over needs (hajiyyah) over improvements (tahsiniyyah). Within necessary, the order is religion, life, lineage, intellect, wealth. A minority view prioritizes human rights over divine rights, exemplified by qisas over apostasy punishment. The Shafi'is prefer simple descriptions over compound, while Hanafis consider them equal, a key dispute.
The dispute on simple vs compound is a notable difference between the schools.

Source Text

الترجيحات القياسية
لما ذكر معارضة القياس والاستحسان وفهم منه إمكان المعارضة بين قياسين عقب البحث ببحث الترجيح (يقدم) القياس (قطعي العلة على منصوصها) الظنية (إجماعاً و) يقدم (منصوصها) من القياس صريحاً على ما ثبت فيه الحكم (وهذا أولى من عكسه أي مما لعينه تأثير في جنس الحكم، لأن الظن الحاصل بسبب التأثير في العين أقوى من الحاصل بسبب التأثير في الجنس (وقيل بالعكس نظراً إلى الوصف (وكل منهما أي مما لعينه في الجنس ولجنسه في العين تأثير أولى من الجنس في الجنس) أي مما لجنسه تأثير في جنس الحكم وللقريب من (البعيد أي لما لجنسه القريب تأثير أولوية مما البعيده تأثير والمركب من بسيطه أي ما فيه تأثير مركب من هذه الأربعة الدوران (و) رجحوا السبر عليهما لما فيه من التعرض لنفي المعارض دونهما ولا يتأتى من الحنفية لإنكارهم الثلاثة إلا ما يرجع من السبر إلى الظن (وقيل: بل الدوران مقدم عليهما الزيادة الانعكاس فيه وليس فيهما والحق أنه ليس بشرط) في العلية فلا دخل له في القوة وفيه ما فيه لأنه وإن لم يكن شرطاً لاحتمال التعليل بعلل شتى لكن الأصل في العلة التوحد، فالأصل الانعكاس فيصلح مرجحاً (وما في التحرير من ثبوت الإنعكاس في على التحسينية ومكمل كل مثل المكمل فمكمل الضرورية مقدم على مكمل الحاجية. وهكذا (و) في الضرورية (يقدم حفظ الدين ثم حفظ النفس ثم حفظ النسب ثم ) حفظ (العقل ثم حفظ (المال) وهو ظاهر لأهمية الدين ثم النفس ثم النسب ثم العقل ثم المال (وقيل بتقدم هذه الأربعة على الدين، لأنها أي هذه الأربعة (حق الآدمي والدين حق الله تعالى، وحق الآدمي مقدم (ولذلك قدم القصاص على قتل الردة إذا قتل شخص ثم ارتد العياذ بالله (فيسلم إلى الولي ليقتله قصاصاً (لا) إلى (الإمام) لقتل الردة ، مع أن الثاني حق الله تعالى دون الأوّل (يترك الجمعة والجماعة
Qiyasi Preferences. When the conflict between qiyas and istihsan was mentioned, and from it the possibility of conflict between two qiyases was understood, he followed the discussion with a discussion of preference. (It is given precedence) the qiyas with a definitive cause over the one with a stated cause (which is zanni), by consensus. And (it is given precedence) the stated cause from qiyas explicitly over what is established through indication, because explicit is stronger in indication. From the fourth principle: Qiyas of Indication. (In it) i.e., in the stated causes, there are ranks: as it is established by apparent text or by its clear text or by its hidden; so the predominant is given precedence over the dominated. And (it is given precedence) what is established by indication over what is established by suitability. And if they agree in suitability, then 'specific in specific' is more appropriate than 'genus in specific', i.e., the qiyas in which the cause has effect on the specific ruling is more appropriate than the qiyas in which the cause has effect on the genus of the ruling. And this is more appropriate than its reverse, i.e., what has an effect on the genus of the ruling, because the certainty obtained due to effect on the specific is stronger than that due to effect on the genus. And it is said the opposite, considering the description. And each of them, i.e., what has effect on the genus and what has effect on the specific, is more appropriate than 'genus in genus'. And for the near over the far, i.e., what has near genus effect takes priority over what has far effect. And the compound over its simple, i.e., what has composite effect from these four is given precedence over what has a single simple effect. And the more composite in its effect is given precedence over the less composite. And in equality, i.e., when the effects are equal in consideration, for the preference of the part, so what has effect of specific in specific as a part is more appropriate than what does not have this effect as a part. And it has preceded. Then, the presumption is given precedence over the wisdom, because rulings are mostly tied to presumptions rather than wisdom, and certainty follows the majority. And it is said the opposite: wisdom is given precedence over presumption, since there is no causation except when it is disciplined, and then it is more deserving of consideration. And the existent is given precedence over the non-existent due to its frequency, to the extent that there is disagreement on whether the non-existent can be a cause. And the legal ruling is given precedence over others due to the agreement of principles because it is a legal ruling. And the simple description like taste over the compound description like quantity and genus, except according to the Hanafis (may Allah have mercy on them and increase them) for they say: they are equal, and it is the apparent view, because the consideration is effect and consideration, and simple and compound are the same in that. And the Shafi'is preferred conjecture over correlation, and they preferred tabulation over both, because it involves exposing the denial of the opponent unlike them. And the Hanafis cannot do that because they reject the three except what returns from tabulation to certainty. And it is said: rather correlation is given precedence over them both due to the additional point of counterproof in it, which is not in them. And the truth is that it (counterproof) is not a condition in causation, so it has no role in strength. And there is something in it, because although it is not a condition due to the possibility of multiple causes, the principle in causation is unity, so the principle is counterproof, so it can be a preponderance factor. And what is in al-Tahrir about the establishment of counterproof in tabulation also due to exclusive examination and invalidation of what remains, then the unity of cause is necessary, then counterproof is necessary, is an error, because it does not invalidate the independence of other than the remaining, but only partiality as previously. Then, the necessary interests are given precedence over needs, and needs over improvements. And the complement of each is like the complement, so the complement of necessary is given precedence over the complement of needs, and so on. And in the necessary (interests), preservation of religion is given precedence, then preservation of life, then preservation of lineage, then preservation of intellect, then preservation of wealth. This is apparent due to the importance of religion, then life, then lineage, then intellect, then wealth. And it is said: these four are given precedence over religion, because they are the right of humans and religion is the right of Allah, and the right of humans is given precedence. Therefore, retaliation is given precedence over execution for apostasy if a person kills and then apostatizes (may Allah protect us): he is handed over to the guardian to execute him as retaliation, not to the Imam for execution for apostasy, even though the second is the right of Allah without the first. He abandons Friday and congregation.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage is a systematic enumeration of tarjih (preference) rules between different types of qiyas. It begins with the strongest preference: qiyas with a definitive cause (qat'i al-'illah) is prioritized over one with a zanni cause (mansus), by consensus. Next, among those with stated causes, explicit textual indication (sarih) is preferred over implied indication (ima'). Within the category of implied indication, there are further ranks: cause established by apparent text, clear text, or hidden text, with the predominant (ghalib) taking precedence. The discussion then moves to the nature of the cause's effect: effect on the specific ruling (ayn) is stronger than effect on the genus (jins), and compound effects (murakkab) are preferred over simple ones. Preferences also consider the part (juz) when effects are equal. Other criteria include mazannah (presumption) vs hikmah (wisdom), with the majority favoring mazannah. Wujudi (existential) causes are preferred over 'adami (non-existential). A legal ruling (hukm shar'i) is preferred over other descriptions. The passage highlights a key difference between Hanafis and Shafi'is: Hanafis consider simple and compound descriptions equal, while Shafi'is prefer simple. Shafi'is also prefer ikhalah (conjecture) over dawaran (correlation) and sabr (tabulation) over both, but Hanafis reject these methods. Finally, the passage prioritizes masalih (interests): daruriyyah (necessities) over hajiyyah (needs) over tahsiniyyah (improvements), and within daruriyyah, the order is din, nafs, nasab, 'aql, mal. A minority view gives precedence to human rights over divine rights, exemplified by qisas (retaliation) over apostasy punishment.

Key Terms
قطعي العلة (qat'i al-'illah): العلة المقطوع بها من النص أو الإجماع
منصوص العلة (mansus al-'illah): العلة المنصوص عليها ظناً
المصالح الضرورية (al-masalih al-daruriyyah): المصالح التي لا بد منها لقيام الدين والدنيا
الإيماء (al-ima'): الدلالة على العلة بطريق الإشارة لا بالصراحة
المناسبة (al-munasabah): العلة المدركة بمناسبتها للحكم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT a preference rule according to the passage?
A) Qat'i 'illah is given precedence over mansus 'illah.
B) Ima' is given precedence over munasabah.
C) Simple descriptions are given precedence over compound descriptions (Hanafi view).
D) Existential causes are given precedence over non-existential causes.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school provides a detailed hierarchy of preferences for resolving conflicts between qiyas arguments, emphasizing the certainty and explicitness of the cause and the nature of its effect.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
Hierarchy of Causal Effects and Weighting in Qiyas
Ranks of causal indications and principles of weighting

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to rank the four types of 'illah effects ('ayn fī 'ayn, jins fī 'ayn, 'ayn fī jins, jins fī jins) in order of strength and apply the weighting principles (e.g., preference of īmā' over munāsaba) to a given qiyas argument.

Core Idea
The strongest 'illah is al-īmā', then al-munāsaba; within effects, specific on specific beats generic on generic.

When engaging in qiyas, not all causes are equal. The Hanafi school has developed a sophisticated hierarchy of causal indications (dalālāt) and effects (athar) to determine which analogy carries greater weight. This lesson unpacks the foundational ranking system that governs preference among competing causes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main sources of 'illah
Understand that causes can be established by explicit text (nass), indication (īmā'), or suitability (munāsaba). Īmā' is preferred over munāsaba because it provides a stronger presumption.
Īmā' includes contexts like question and answer, or explicit connection.
2
Understand the four combinations of effect
The effect of a cause can be specific ('ayn) or generic (jins) on the ruling's specific ('ayn) or generic (jins). This yields four types: 'ayn fī 'ayn, jins fī 'ayn, 'ayn fī jins, jins fī jins.
Al-'ayn fī al-'ayn is the strongest, e.g., intoxication causing prohibition of wine.
3
Rank them by strength
The presumption (ẓann) from 'ayn fī 'ayn is strongest, followed by jins fī 'ayn, then 'ayn fī jins, then jins fī jins. Some reverse the middle two, but the majority Hanafi view is as given.
The text mentions 'Qīla bil-'aks' (it is said the opposite) regarding 'ayn fī jins vs jins fī 'ayn.
4
Apply additional weighting factors
Consider whether the cause is an indicator (maẓanna) or wisdom (ḥikma), existent (wujūdī) or non-existent ('adamī), simple or compound, legal ruling or other, and the strength of the original analogy's source.
Maẓanna is generally preferred over ḥikma because rulings are most often linked to clear indicators.
5
Integrate into a qiyas comparison
When two analogies conflict, apply these ranking principles to determine which yields a stronger presumption and thus should be preferred.
The ultimate aim is to achieve ghālib al-ẓann (predominant presumption).

بأنه لا خسة كالكفر . وقد جاز نكاح المسلم مع طول المسلمة بالكتابية اتفاقاً) فلو كان الأخسية مانعة لكونها تحت الإشراف لما جاز الكتابية إلا ضرورة ( وأما الإرقاق) الذي جعله الشافعي رضي الله عنه علة للحرمة فمنقوض بالعبد المقيس عليه فإن ماءه حر، إذ الرق من جهة (الأم) لا الأب وإذ قد جاز له النكاح مع الأمة مع طول الحرة لزم إرقاق مائه (على أن العزل ونكاح الصغيرة والعجوز والعقيم جائز اتفاقاً مع أنه إتلاف) له (حقيقة) فالإرقاق الذي هو اتلاف حكمي أولى أن يجوز (تدبر) واعلم أن جواز نكاح الحر مع الأمة وإن كان عنده الطول ثابت بالعمومات وهذا القياس ،ممد له والذي جرأ الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه قوله بالمفهوم كما الركنية في التثليث، لكن على هذا يفسد القياس، والكلام كان في الترجيح، ولذا لم يذكره وقال : (لو سلم تأثير الركنية في التثليث فتشريع المسح سيما مع عدم الاستيعاب ليس إلا للتخفيف) فللمسح تأثير في التخفيف فلا يثلث، وأما الركنية فإنما تأثيره في التكميل، وقد سن بالاستيعاب (ومنها) أي من الترجيحات الصحيحة (الثبات على الحكم أي كثرة اعتبار الشارع للوصف فيه أي في الحكم، فله قوة تفيد
The fourth principle: qiyas al-imā' (indication). Within it (i.e., within the stated text) are ranks: as established by the apparent text (zāhir), its explicit text (nass), or its hidden meaning (khafī). The predominant is preferred over the subordinate. And that whose cause is established by indication (imā') is preferred over that established by suitability (munāsaba). And if they agree in suitability, then the specific effect on the specific ruling ('ayn fī 'ayn) is more worthy than the generic effect on the specific ruling (jins fī 'ayn). That is, the analogy whose cause's specific effect influences the specific ruling is more worthy than the analogy whose cause's generic effect influences the specific ruling. And this is more worthy than its opposite, i.e., that whose specific effect influences the generic ruling, because the presumption (ẓann) arising from the effect on the specific is stronger than that arising from the effect on the generic. And it is said the opposite, considering the description. And each of them (i.e., that whose specific effect influences the generic, and that whose generic effect influences the specific) is more worthy than the generic on the generic, i.e., that whose generic effect influences the generic ruling. And the close is more worthy than the distant, i.e., that whose close generic effect influences is more worthy than that whose distant generic effect influences. And the compound is more worthy than the simple, i.e., that which has a compound effect from these four is preferred over that which has a single simple effect; and the more composed in its effect is preferred over the less composed. And in equality, i.e., when the effects are compounded and equal in consideration, preference is given to the part: that in which the specific effect on the specific ruling is a part is more worthy than that in which this effect is not a part; and it has preceded. Then the indicator (maẓanna) is preferred over the wisdom (ḥikma), because rulings are most often linked to indicators rather than wisdom, and presumption follows the majority. And it is said the opposite: that wisdom is preferred over the indicator, since there is no causation except when it is well-defined; then it is more deserving of consideration. And the existent is preferred over the non-existent due to frequency, to the extent that there is disagreement whether the non-existent can be a valid cause. And the legal ruling (ḥukm shar'ī) is preferred over others due to the harmony of principles. And the simple description (e.g., taste) is preferred over the compound description (e.g., quantity or genus) except according to the Ḥanafīs (may Allah have mercy on them and multiply them), for they say the two are equal, and this is more apparent, since what matters is effect and consideration, and the simple and compound are alike in that. The Shāfi'īs preferred 'ikhāla (conjecture) over dawrān (correlation), and they preferred sabr (probing) over both, because it involves dismissing the objection, unlike them; and this is not possible from the Ḥanafīs because they deny the three except what returns from sabr to presumption. And it is said: rather dawrān is preferred over both due to the increase of co-occurrence in it which is not in them. But the truth is that it is not a condition in causation, so it has no role in strength, though there is something to it, because even if it is not a condition (due to possibility of multiple causes), the principle is that the cause is singular, so co-occurrence is originally present, thus it can be a weighting factor. And what is in al-Taḥrīr about the establishment of co-occurrence also in sabr due to restriction and invalidation of what remains, thus necessitating unity of cause and thus co-occurrence, is mistaken, because it does not invalidate the independence of what remains, only partiality, as previously mentioned. Then the necessary interests (ḍarūriyyāt) are preferred over the needs (ḥājiyyāt), and the needs over the improvements (taḥsīniyyāt); and the complement of each is like the complement: the complement of the necessary is preferred over the complement of the need, and so forth. And within the necessary, preservation of religion is preferred, then preservation of life, then lineage, then intellect, then wealth; this is apparent due to the importance of religion, then life, then lineage, then intellect, then wealth. And it is said that these four are preferred over religion, because they are rights of the human, while religion is the right of Allah, and the right of the human is preferred. For that reason, qiṣāṣ (retaliation) is given precedence over killing for apostasy: if a person kills and then apostatizes (may Allah forbid), he is handed over to the guardian to kill him as qiṣāṣ, not to the imam for killing for apostasy, even though the latter is Allah's right and the former is not. He abandons Friday and congregation to preserve wealth, e.g., fear of theft and the like, so he leaves the right of Allah for the right of the slave. And it is refuted by saying that qiṣāṣ also involves the right of Allah, for the killer destroyed the building of the Lord and committed what Allah forbade. Yes: the predominant in it is the right of the slave, because Allah gave his guardian clear authority, so handing over to the guardian combines both rights. Therefore, the preference is for this reason, not because fulfilling the right of the slave is preferred and leaving to the imam is different, as in Friday and congregation, it is not from the preference under discussion, for in it the other right is completely abandoned. And as for preferring one of the two conflicting analogies by preferring its source over the other, that preference is accidental for the analogy and essential for the text; its aspects have preceded in the chapter on sunna. And what we have mentioned is sufficient for the insightful. And the foundation of the chapter on preference is giving precedence to the preponderance of presumption: whatever gives predominant presumption is preferred over what gives subordinate presumption. Then the Ḥanafīs (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned in this chapter eight preferences: four sound and four unsound. As for the four sound: among them is strength of effect, because by it the analogy is strengthened and it gives predominant presumption, such as the marriage of a slave woman in the presence of a free woman: it is permissible for a free man by analogy to a slave, for it is permissible by consensus. And al-Shāfi'ī said: this marriage is not permissible, by analogy to one under whom there is a free woman, under the commonality of enslaving the water (i.e., offspring) while being free of need of it. And our analogy is stronger, because the effect of freedom in broadening permissibility, which is a blessing, is stronger than the effect of slavery in broadening permissibility, as an honor for him over the slave. Hence, because freedom is effective in broadening permissibility, the free man is allowed four, and the slave only two. So the restriction for the free and expansion for the slave is a reversal of the legislated and contrary to reason. And what is said in al-Talwīḥ, that this restriction is from the category of honor, so there is no problem; how is it that the broadening of permissibility is for honor, so it cannot be established in a way that loses it, since the honorable is prevented from marrying the low, just as marriage to a fire-worshipping woman is permissible for an unbeliever but not for a Muslim? And in the chain there is something, for its permissibility is not from the category of expansion. It is refuted by saying that there is no lowness like disbelief. And the marriage of a Muslim to a Kitābiyya (scriptural woman) in the presence of a Muslim woman is permissible by consensus; so if lowness were a preventer due to being under supervision, then the Kitābiyya would not be permissible except in necessity. And as for enslaving the water, which al-Shāfi'ī (may Allah be pleased with him) made the cause for prohibition, it is invalidated by the slave who is the analogue upon, for his water is free, since slavery is from the mother's side, not the father; and since his marriage to a slave woman in the presence of a free woman is permissible, it necessitates the enslaving of his water. Moreover, 'azl (coitus interruptus) and marriage to a minor, an old woman, or a barren woman are permissible by consensus, despite being a destruction of him (i.e., offspring) in reality, so enslaving the water (which is a virtual destruction) is more deserving of permissibility. Reflect. And know that the permissibility of a free man's marriage to a slave woman when he has the means is established by general texts, and this analogy supports it. What emboldened Imam al-Shāfi'ī (may Allah be pleased with him) was his reliance on the concept (mafhūm) as previously mentioned. And from it (i.e., from the instances of strength of effect) is the analogy of wiping the head like wiping the khuff (leather socks), in that both are wipes, so it is not to be repeated thrice; this is stronger than the analogy of al-Shāfi'ī that wiping the head is a pillar of wuḍū' and thus should be repeated thrice like the washed limbs. And our analogy is stronger because the effect of being a pillar on repetition is not apparent. But on this, the analogy would be invalid, and the discussion was about weighting; therefore he did not mention it and said: Even if the effect of being a pillar on repetition is granted, the legislation of wiping, especially without complete coverage, is only for lightening, so wiping has an effect in lightening, so it should not be repeated thrice. As for being a pillar, its effect is on completion, and it has been legislated with complete coverage. And among them (i.e., among the sound preferences) is constancy on the ruling, i.e., the frequency of the Lawgiver's consideration of the description in that ruling, so it has strength that gives advantage.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a continuous exposition on the principles of weighting (tarjīḥ) in qiyas (analogical reasoning) from the Hanafi perspective. The author first categorizes the types of 'illah (causal indicators) based on their clarity and effect. The strongest type is al-īmā' (indication), where the cause is directly pointed to by the text, followed by al-munāsaba (suitability), where the cause is derived from the wisdom behind the ruling. He then ranks the effects of the 'illah: the most powerful is when the 'illah's specific effect ('ayn) impacts the specific ruling ('ayn) (e.g., wine's intoxication causing its prohibition), followed by the generic effect (jins) on the specific ruling, then specific on generic, and finally generic on generic. Each successive rank is weaker in presumption. The author then discusses other weighting factors: the indicator (maẓanna) is generally preferred over the underlying wisdom (ḥikma) because rulings are usually attached to clear indicators rather than subtle wisdoms, though some reverse this. Existence (wujūdī) of a description is preferred over non-existence ('adamī) because the latter's validity as a cause is disputed. The legal ruling (ḥukm shar'ī) as a description is preferred over other types due to its harmonious nature. Regarding simple vs. compound descriptions, the Hanafis hold them equal, while the Shāfi'īs favor the simple. The author then defends the Hanafi position on correlation (dawrān) and probing (sabr), arguing that co-occurrence is not a condition for causality. He then lists the hierarchy of public interests (maṣāliḥ): necessary (ḍarūriyyāt), needs (ḥājiyyāt), and improvements (taḥsīniyyāt), with their complements. Within the necessary, the order is: religion, life, lineage, intellect, wealth, though some argue the last four take precedence because they are rights of humans (ḥuqūq al-'ibād) while religion is the right of Allah (ḥaqq Allāh). The author illustrates this with the example of a murderer who apostatizes: he is handed over for qiṣāṣ (retribution) rather than execution for apostasy, because the right of the victim's family is dominant. Similarly, one may abandon Friday and congregation to protect wealth from theft, showing that human rights can override divine obligations in some cases. However, the author clarifies that this is not the same as completely abandoning one for the other. The final section enumerates the eight tarjīḥ (preferences) in qiyas that the Hanafis discuss: four sound and four unsound. Two sound examples are given in detail: the marriage of a free man to a slave woman despite having a free woman, and the wiping over the head (masḥ) not requiring repetition three times. In both, the Hanafi analogy is argued to be stronger due to the effect of freedom and the nature of wiping.

Key Terms
الإيماء (al-īmā'): A textual indication that points to the 'illah without explicit statement, e.g., a question and answer context.
المناسبة (al-munāsaba): A cause derived from the suitable wisdom (ḥikma) that fits the ruling, even if not explicitly indicated.
العين في العين (al-'ayn fī al-'ayn): A specific cause (e.g., intoxication) affecting a specific ruling (e.g., prohibition of wine).
الجنس في العين (al-jins fī al-'ayn): A generic cause (e.g., intoxicating quality) affecting a specific ruling (e.g., prohibition of a specific intoxicant).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi hierarchy of effects in qiyas, which of the following combinations yields the strongest presumption?
A) 'Ayn fī 'ayn
B) Jins fī 'ayn
C) 'Ayn fī jins
D) Jins fī jins

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school ranks causal effects from strongest 'ayn fī 'ayn to weakest jins fī jins, prioritizing presumption (ẓann) and preferring īmā' over munāsaba.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
15 min
The Eight Preferences (Tarjīḥ) in Qiyas: Sound and Unsound
Hanafi eight tarjīḥ with detailed examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the four sound and four unsound tarjīḥ (preferences) in qiyas as discussed by the Hanafis, using the examples of marriage to a slave woman and wiping over the head.

Core Idea
Among the four sound tarjīḥ, strength of effect (quwwat al-athar) is paramount, as shown in the marriage and wiping cases.

The Hanafi scholars enumerate eight preferences (tarjīḥ) in qiyas, four of which are sound and four unsound. This lesson focuses on the sound ones, particularly the principle of 'strength of effect' (quwwat al-athar), and demonstrates its application through two detailed examples: the permissibility of a free man marrying a slave woman despite having a free wife, and the ruling that wiping over the head (masḥ) is not repeated three times. These examples illustrate how the effect of a description (e.g., freedom or lightening) outweighs other considerations.

Guided Path
1
Understand the framework of the eight tarjīḥ
Recognize that the Hanafis discuss four sound tarjīḥ and four unsound ones. The sound ones include: strength of effect, constancy on the ruling, etc. The unsound ones are not detailed here.
The text only elaborates on two sound tarjīḥ: strength of effect and constancy on the ruling.
2
Analyze the first sound tarjīḥ: strength of effect (quwwat al-athar)
An analogy is strengthened when its 'illah has a strong effect. This is exemplified by the marriage of a free man to a slave woman: the effect of freedom in broadening permissibility is stronger than the effect of slavery, so our analogy (to a slave) is stronger than the Shafi'i analogy (to a free man with a free wife).
The text also provides the analogy of wiping over the head to show that lightening effect outweighs pillar effect.
3
Follow the Hanafi response to the Shafi'i argument
The Shafi'i argument uses 'enslaving offspring' (irqāq al-mā') as the common cause. The Hanafis refute this by pointing out that the slave's offspring are free (since slavery is from the mother), and other actions like 'azl and marrying barren women are allowed despite destroying offspring.
This shows that the Shafi'i cause is invalid (manqūḍ), and the Hanafi cause (freedom's effect) is stronger.
4
Apply the second sound tarjīḥ: constancy on the ruling (thabāt 'alā al-ḥukm)
If the Lawgiver frequently considers a particular description in a ruling, that description gains strength. The text mentions this but does not elaborate with an example.
This tarjīḥ is based on the frequency of the description's appearance in the Sharī'ah.
5
Distinguish sound from unsound tarjīḥ
The unsound preferences are not detailed in this passage, but understanding that some arguments are invalid (fāsid) is important. The text only notes four sound and four unsound, without listing the unsound ones.
Focus on the sound ones for application.

الكرامة فلا بأس به، كيف وإنما كان اتساع الحل للكرامة فلا يثبت بوجه يفوّتها حيث منع الشريف من تزوج الخسيس، كما جاز نكاح المجوسية للكافر دون المسلم وفي السند شيء فإن جوازه ليس من باب التوسيع (فمدفوع بأنه لا خسة كالكفر . وقد جاز نكاح المسلم مع طول المسلمة بالكتابية اتفاقاً) فلو كان الأخسية مانعة لكونها تحت الإشراف لما جاز الكتابية إلا ضرورة ( وأما الإرقاق) الذي جعله الشافعي رضي الله عنه علة للحرمة فمنقوض بالعبد المقيس عليه فإن ماءه حر، إذ الرق من جهة (الأم) لا الأب وإذ قد جاز له النكاح مع الأمة مع طول الحرة لزم إرقاق مائه (على أن العزل ونكاح الصغيرة والعجوز والعقيم جائز اتفاقاً مع أنه إتلاف) له (حقيقة) فالإرقاق الذي هو اتلاف حكمي أولى أن يجوز (تدبر) واعلم أن جواز نكاح الحر مع الأمة وإن كان عنده الطول ثابت بالعمومات وهذا القياس ،ممد له والذي جرأ الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه قوله بالمفهوم كما الركنية في التثليث، لكن على هذا يفسد القياس، والكلام كان في الترجيح، ولذا لم يذكره وقال : (لو سلم تأثير الركنية في التثليث فتشريع المسح سيما مع عدم الاستيعاب ليس إلا للتخفيف) فللمسح تأثير في التخفيف فلا يثلث، وأما الركنية فإنما تأثيره في التكميل، وقد سن بالاستيعاب (ومنها) أي من الترجيحات الصحيحة (الثبات على الحكم أي كثرة اعتبار الشارع للوصف فيه أي في الحكم، فله قوة تفيد
The fourth principle: qiyas al-imā' (indication). Within it (i.e., within the stated text) are ranks: as established by the apparent text (zāhir), its explicit text (nass), or its hidden meaning (khafī). The predominant is preferred over the subordinate. And that whose cause is established by indication (imā') is preferred over that established by suitability (munāsaba). And if they agree in suitability, then the specific effect on the specific ruling ('ayn fī 'ayn) is more worthy than the generic effect on the specific ruling (jins fī 'ayn). That is, the analogy whose cause's specific effect influences the specific ruling is more worthy than the analogy whose cause's generic effect influences the specific ruling. And this is more worthy than its opposite, i.e., that whose specific effect influences the generic ruling, because the presumption (ẓann) arising from the effect on the specific is stronger than that arising from the effect on the generic. And it is said the opposite, considering the description. And each of them (i.e., that whose specific effect influences the generic, and that whose generic effect influences the specific) is more worthy than the generic on the generic, i.e., that whose generic effect influences the generic ruling. And the close is more worthy than the distant, i.e., that whose close generic effect influences is more worthy than that whose distant generic effect influences. And the compound is more worthy than the simple, i.e., that which has a compound effect from these four is preferred over that which has a single simple effect; and the more composed in its effect is preferred over the less composed. And in equality, i.e., when the effects are compounded and equal in consideration, preference is given to the part: that in which the specific effect on the specific ruling is a part is more worthy than that in which this effect is not a part; and it has preceded. Then the indicator (maẓanna) is preferred over the wisdom (ḥikma), because rulings are most often linked to indicators rather than wisdom, and presumption follows the majority. And it is said the opposite: that wisdom is preferred over the indicator, since there is no causation except when it is well-defined; then it is more deserving of consideration. And the existent is preferred over the non-existent due to frequency, to the extent that there is disagreement whether the non-existent can be a valid cause. And the legal ruling (ḥukm shar'ī) is preferred over others due to the harmony of principles. And the simple description (e.g., taste) is preferred over the compound description (e.g., quantity or genus) except according to the Ḥanafīs (may Allah have mercy on them and multiply them), for they say the two are equal, and this is more apparent, since what matters is effect and consideration, and the simple and compound are alike in that. The Shāfi'īs preferred 'ikhāla (conjecture) over dawrān (correlation), and they preferred sabr (probing) over both, because it involves dismissing the objection, unlike them; and this is not possible from the Ḥanafīs because they deny the three except what returns from sabr to presumption. And it is said: rather dawrān is preferred over both due to the increase of co-occurrence in it which is not in them. But the truth is that it is not a condition in causation, so it has no role in strength, though there is something to it, because even if it is not a condition (due to possibility of multiple causes), the principle is that the cause is singular, so co-occurrence is originally present, thus it can be a weighting factor. And what is in al-Taḥrīr about the establishment of co-occurrence also in sabr due to restriction and invalidation of what remains, thus necessitating unity of cause and thus co-occurrence, is mistaken, because it does not invalidate the independence of what remains, only partiality, as previously mentioned. Then the necessary interests (ḍarūriyyāt) are preferred over the needs (ḥājiyyāt), and the needs over the improvements (taḥsīniyyāt); and the complement of each is like the complement: the complement of the necessary is preferred over the complement of the need, and so forth. And within the necessary, preservation of religion is preferred, then preservation of life, then lineage, then intellect, then wealth; this is apparent due to the importance of religion, then life, then lineage, then intellect, then wealth. And it is said that these four are preferred over religion, because they are rights of the human, while religion is the right of Allah, and the right of the human is preferred. For that reason, qiṣāṣ (retaliation) is given precedence over killing for apostasy: if a person kills and then apostatizes (may Allah forbid), he is handed over to the guardian to kill him as qiṣāṣ, not to the imam for killing for apostasy, even though the latter is Allah's right and the former is not. He abandons Friday and congregation to preserve wealth, e.g., fear of theft and the like, so he leaves the right of Allah for the right of the slave. And it is refuted by saying that qiṣāṣ also involves the right of Allah, for the killer destroyed the building of the Lord and committed what Allah forbade. Yes: the predominant in it is the right of the slave, because Allah gave his guardian clear authority, so handing over to the guardian combines both rights. Therefore, the preference is for this reason, not because fulfilling the right of the slave is preferred and leaving to the imam is different, as in Friday and congregation, it is not from the preference under discussion, for in it the other right is completely abandoned. And as for preferring one of the two conflicting analogies by preferring its source over the other, that preference is accidental for the analogy and essential for the text; its aspects have preceded in the chapter on sunna. And what we have mentioned is sufficient for the insightful. And the foundation of the chapter on preference is giving precedence to the preponderance of presumption: whatever gives predominant presumption is preferred over what gives subordinate presumption. Then the Ḥanafīs (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned in this chapter eight preferences: four sound and four unsound. As for the four sound: among them is strength of effect, because by it the analogy is strengthened and it gives predominant presumption, such as the marriage of a slave woman in the presence of a free woman: it is permissible for a free man by analogy to a slave, for it is permissible by consensus. And al-Shāfi'ī said: this marriage is not permissible, by analogy to one under whom there is a free woman, under the commonality of enslaving the water (i.e., offspring) while being free of need of it. And our analogy is stronger, because the effect of freedom in broadening permissibility, which is a blessing, is stronger than the effect of slavery in broadening permissibility, as an honor for him over the slave. Hence, because freedom is effective in broadening permissibility, the free man is allowed four, and the slave only two. So the restriction for the free and expansion for the slave is a reversal of the legislated and contrary to reason. And what is said in al-Talwīḥ, that this restriction is from the category of honor, so there is no problem; how is it that the broadening of permissibility is for honor, so it cannot be established in a way that loses it, since the honorable is prevented from marrying the low, just as marriage to a fire-worshipping woman is permissible for an unbeliever but not for a Muslim? And in the chain there is something, for its permissibility is not from the category of expansion. It is refuted by saying that there is no lowness like disbelief. And the marriage of a Muslim to a Kitābiyya (scriptural woman) in the presence of a Muslim woman is permissible by consensus; so if lowness were a preventer due to being under supervision, then the Kitābiyya would not be permissible except in necessity. And as for enslaving the water, which al-Shāfi'ī (may Allah be pleased with him) made the cause for prohibition, it is invalidated by the slave who is the analogue upon, for his water is free, since slavery is from the mother's side, not the father; and since his marriage to a slave woman in the presence of a free woman is permissible, it necessitates the enslaving of his water. Moreover, 'azl (coitus interruptus) and marriage to a minor, an old woman, or a barren woman are permissible by consensus, despite being a destruction of him (i.e., offspring) in reality, so enslaving the water (which is a virtual destruction) is more deserving of permissibility. Reflect. And know that the permissibility of a free man's marriage to a slave woman when he has the means is established by general texts, and this analogy supports it. What emboldened Imam al-Shāfi'ī (may Allah be pleased with him) was his reliance on the concept (mafhūm) as previously mentioned. And from it (i.e., from the instances of strength of effect) is the analogy of wiping the head like wiping the khuff (leather socks), in that both are wipes, so it is not to be repeated thrice; this is stronger than the analogy of al-Shāfi'ī that wiping the head is a pillar of wuḍū' and thus should be repeated thrice like the washed limbs. And our analogy is stronger because the effect of being a pillar on repetition is not apparent. But on this, the analogy would be invalid, and the discussion was about weighting; therefore he did not mention it and said: Even if the effect of being a pillar on repetition is granted, the legislation of wiping, especially without complete coverage, is only for lightening, so wiping has an effect in lightening, so it should not be repeated thrice. As for being a pillar, its effect is on completion, and it has been legislated with complete coverage. And among them (i.e., among the sound preferences) is constancy on the ruling, i.e., the frequency of the Lawgiver's consideration of the description in that ruling, so it has strength that gives advantage.
Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage is a continuous exposition on the principles of weighting (tarjīḥ) in qiyas (analogical reasoning) from the Hanafi perspective. The author first categorizes the types of 'illah (causal indicators) based on their clarity and effect. The strongest type is al-īmā' (indication), where the cause is directly pointed to by the text, followed by al-munāsaba (suitability), where the cause is derived from the wisdom behind the ruling. He then ranks the effects of the 'illah: the most powerful is when the 'illah's specific effect ('ayn) impacts the specific ruling ('ayn) (e.g., wine's intoxication causing its prohibition), followed by the generic effect (jins) on the specific ruling, then specific on generic, and finally generic on generic. Each successive rank is weaker in presumption. The author then discusses other weighting factors: the indicator (maẓanna) is generally preferred over the underlying wisdom (ḥikma) because rulings are usually attached to clear indicators rather than subtle wisdoms, though some reverse this. Existence (wujūdī) of a description is preferred over non-existence ('adamī) because the latter's validity as a cause is disputed. The legal ruling (ḥukm shar'ī) as a description is preferred over other types due to its harmonious nature. Regarding simple vs. compound descriptions, the Hanafis hold them equal, while the Shāfi'īs favor the simple. The author then defends the Hanafi position on correlation (dawrān) and probing (sabr), arguing that co-occurrence is not a condition for causality. He then lists the hierarchy of public interests (maṣāliḥ): necessary (ḍarūriyyāt), needs (ḥājiyyāt), and improvements (taḥsīniyyāt), with their complements. Within the necessary, the order is: religion, life, lineage, intellect, wealth, though some argue the last four take precedence because they are rights of humans (ḥuqūq al-'ibād) while religion is the right of Allah (ḥaqq Allāh). The author illustrates this with the example of a murderer who apostatizes: he is handed over for qiṣāṣ (retribution) rather than execution for apostasy, because the right of the victim's family is dominant. Similarly, one may abandon Friday and congregation to protect wealth from theft, showing that human rights can override divine obligations in some cases. However, the author clarifies that this is not the same as completely abandoning one for the other. The final section enumerates the eight tarjīḥ (preferences) in qiyas that the Hanafis discuss: four sound and four unsound. Two sound examples are given in detail: the marriage of a free man to a slave woman despite having a free woman, and the wiping over the head (masḥ) not requiring repetition three times. In both, the Hanafi analogy is argued to be stronger due to the effect of freedom and the nature of wiping.

Key Terms
قوة الأثر (quwwat al-athar): Strength of effect: a principle in tarjīḥ where the 'illah's effect on the ruling is considered strong and thus preferred.
الثبات على الحكم (al-thabāt 'alā al-ḥukm): Constancy on the ruling: preference based on the Lawgiver's frequent consideration of a description in a ruling.
إرقاق الماء (irqāq al-mā'): Enslaving the water (offspring): the Shafi'i cause for prohibiting marriage to a slave woman when one has a free wife.
التخويف (al-takhfīf): Lightening: the purpose behind wiping (masḥ) in wuḍū'.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do Hanafis consider their analogy for marriage of a free man to a slave woman stronger than the Shafi'i analogy?
A) Because the slave is allowed by explicit text.
B) Because the effect of freedom in broadening permissibility is stronger than the effect of enslaving offspring.
C) Because the free man has a greater need.
D) Because the Shafi'i analogy uses a weaker source of 'illah.

Key Takeaway
The strength of effect (quwwat al-athar) is a key Hanafi tarjīḥ principle, demonstrated by the permissibility of marriage to a slave woman and the non-repetition of wiping over the head.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
The Four Valid Types of Tarjih in Qiyas
Exposition of the four valid grounds for preferring one qiyas over another

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the four valid grounds for preferring one qiyas over another in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
The strength of a qiyas depends on the effect of its attribute, its constancy, its multiple principles, or weakest, the inverse.

In Hanafi usul, when two analogies conflict, we prefer the one that yields stronger probability (ghalabat al-zann). The scholars enumerated eight grounds of preference (tarjih), but only four are considered valid. This lesson unpacks each valid ground with examples, showing how they strengthen an analogy and how they are distinct from one another.

Guided Path
1
Identify the foundation of tarjih
Recognise that all preference is based on achieving predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann). The stronger analogy is the one that brings greater certainty to the mind of the mujtahid.
This principle underlies all eight tarjihs, valid and invalid.
2
Examine the first valid tarjih: quwwat al-athar
Study the effect of the descriptive attribute (wasf) in producing the ruling. If the attribute's effect is stronger in a particular analogy, that analogy is preferred. The marriage example illustrates this: freedom has a stronger effect in expanding the permissible than slavery has in restricting it.
The Hanafi position on marrying a slave woman while having a free woman is upheld by this tarjih.
3
Analyze the second and third tarjihs: thabat ala al-hukm and kathrat al-usul
Understand that constancy of the Lawgiver's consideration of a description in similar rulings strengthens an analogy. The wiping example shows that ease (takhfif) is repeatedly considered in non-rational purifications. Multiplicity of principles (kathrat al-usul) means the same description is effective in many original cases. The scholars debate whether these are distinct or overlapping.
The text resolves that the third is more general than the second, but both reduce to strength of effect.
4
Consider the fourth valid tarjih: al-aks
Learn that the inverse (using the absence of a ruling in a similar case to argue against the cause) is the weakest valid tarjih because rulings can have multiple causes. However, it is still considered a valid ground when no stronger evidence exists.
Example: wiping the head is not repeated because it is an incomprehensible purification, unlike rinsing the mouth which is repeated but is not a pillar.

الأمة مع طول الحرة لزم إرقاق مائه (على أن العزل ونكاح الصغيرة والعجوز والعقيم جائز اتفاقاً مع أنه إتلاف) له (حقيقة) فالإرقاق الذي هو اتلاف حكمي أولى أن يجوز (تدبر) واعلم أن جواز نكاح الحر مع الأمة وإن أي الشافعي مسح الرأس (ركن) للوضوء (فيثلث كالمغسول) من الأعضاء وإنما كان قياسنا أقوى (لأنه لا يظهر تأثير الركنية في التثليث، لكن على هذا يفسد القياس، والكلام كان في الترجيح، ولذا لم يذكره وقال : (لو سلم تأثير الركنية في التثليث فتشريع المسح سيما مع عدم الاستيعاب ليس إلا للتخفيف) فللمسح تأثير في التخفيف فلا يثلث، وأما الركنية فإنما تأثيره في التكميل، وقد سن بالاستيعاب (ومنها) أي من الترجيحات الصحيحة (الثبات على الحكم أي كثرة اعتبار الشارع للوصف فيه أي في الحكم، فله قوة تفيد غلبة الظن (كالمسح) أنه مؤثر في التخفيف في كل تطهير غير معقول كالتيمم ومسح الجبيرة والجورب والخف فلم يشرع فيها التكرر فله كثرة اعتبار في التخفيف بخلاف الاستنجاء من الحجر) وقد شرع فيه التكرار (فإنه) تطهير (معقول) قد قصد فيه إزالة الخبث (إذ التكرار في التنقية مؤثر، وأما الركنية فأثبت في الإكمال) فإن أركان الصلاة من إكمالها، وكذا أركان الحج ، وكذا أركان الغسل، لكن الإكمال مختلف ففي الغسل بالتكرار (وهو ههنا) أي مسح الرأس (بالاستيعاب ومنها أي من التراجيح الصحيحة كثرة الأصول على القول (المختار) فإنه أيضاً يفيد قوة في القياس ولا يلزم كثرة العلل أي الدلائل حتى يمنع الترجيح به على مذهبنا الاتحاد الوصف المعلل، وما دام هو واحداً فالقياس ،واحد فلا تعدد للدلائل فافهم (قيل) القائل الإمام فخر الإسلام وصدر الشريعة رحمهما الله تعالى. الترجيح (الثالث: قريب من الترجيح (الثاني) لأن كثرة اعتبار الشارع يوجبه كثرة الأصول (و) قال (في التلويح: والتحرير الحق بأن التفرقة بين الثلاثة بالاعتبار (فالأول أي قوة الأثر (بالنظر إلى عدم تخلف كثرة الاعتبار عن كثرة الأصول فظاهر، وهذا إنما يتم لو كان المراد بكثرة الاعتبار كثرة تأثيره في عين الحكم، ولو كان أعم منه، ومن كثرة تأثيره في جنسه لم يتم ، مع أن أتباع فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة يدعون انتفاء التأثير في الجنس بسيطاً (وأما التفرقة بالاعتبار بينها فغلط، ألا ترى المسح أقوى في التخفيف ولو عدم النظائر بل القوة عبارة عن قوة المناسبة بين الوصف والحكم بحيث يكاد يحكم بعليته العقل، ولولا الشرع كما قيل في الإسكار للحرمة) فحينئذ يتحقق قوة الأثر وإن لم تكن كثرة الاعتبار وكثرة الأصول فلا تغفل ومنها أي من التراجيح الصحيحة العكس كمسح أي كالقياس بأن مسح الرأس مسح (لا يعقل فلا يسن تكراره بخلاف القياس بأنه ركن فيسن تكراره لأنه منقوض بالمضمضة) فإنها تكررت وليست ركناً (وهذا) أي العكس أضعف الوجوه للترجيح لأن الحكم يثبت بعلل شتى) فلا يستلزم عدم مرجح لما بينا أن الأصل في العلة الاتحاد، فالأكثر فيها العكس فتذكر
Regarding the preservation of wealth like fear of theft etc., so the right of Allah is left for the right of the servant. It was responded that retaliation also involves the right of Allah because the killer destroys the building of the Lord and does what Allah forbade. Yes, the predominant right is that of the servant since Allah gave his guardian clear authority, so handing over to the guardian combines both rights. The payment is for this, not because fulfilling the right of the servant is advanced and leaving to the deputy as in Friday and congregation is not from the preference under discussion because it involves complete abandonment of the other. As for preferring one of the two conflicting analogies by giving precedence to its origin over the other, that preference is for the analogy by accident and for the text by essence. Its aspects have been presented in the Sunnah and in what we have mentioned is sufficient for the insightful person. The foundation of the chapter on preference is giving precedence to predominant probability. So what brings predominant probability is preferred over what brings lesser probability. Then the Hanafis (may Allah have mercy on them) mentioned in this chapter eight preferences: four valid and four invalid. As for the four valid ones: among them is the strength of effect because by it the analogy is strengthened and it brings predominant probability, like the marriage of a slave woman while having access to a free woman is permissible for a free man by analogy to a slave since it is permissible for him by consensus. Al-Shafi'i said: This marriage is not permissible by analogy to one who has a free wife due to the commonality of enslaving the child while being free of need. Our analogy is stronger because the effect of freedom in widening the permissible, which is a blessing, is stronger than the effect of slavery in the permissible as an honor to him over the slave. Hence, because freedom affects widening the permissible, four are permitted for a free man and two for a slave. So restricting freedom and widening slavery is a reversal of the Sharia and contrary to reason. What is said in al-Talwih that this restriction is from the category of honor is fine. How so? The widening of the permissible is for honor, so it is not established by a manner that would lose that honor when the honorable is prevented from marrying a lowly, just as it is permissible for a disbeliever to marry a Zoroastrian but not for a Muslim, though there is something in the chain because its permissibility is not from the category of expansion. It is rejected because there is no lowness like disbelief. And the marriage of a Muslim while having access to a Muslim woman to a Scriptural woman is permissible by consensus. If lowness were a preventer due to being under supervision, the Scriptural woman would not be permissible except out of necessity. As for the enslaving of the child which al-Shafi'i made the cause for prohibition, it is rebutted by the slave who is analogized upon, for his child is free since slavery is from the mother not the father. Since he is allowed to marry a slave woman while having a free woman, the enslaving of his child is required. Moreover, coitus interruptus and marriage to a minor, an old woman, and a barren woman are permissible by consensus even though it is actual destruction, so inferential destruction is more likely permissible. Reflect. Know that the permissibility of a free man marrying a slave woman even if he has opportunity is established by general texts, and this analogy supports it. What emboldened al-Imam al-Shafi'i is his adherence to the implied meaning as previously mentioned. Also from the strength of effect is the analogy of wiping the head like the khuff in that both are wiping, so it is not to be done thrice, stronger than the analogy of al-Shafi'i that wiping the head is a pillar of ablution so it is to be done thrice like the washed limbs. Our analogy is stronger because the effect of being a pillar on doing thrice is not apparent. But on this, the analogy fails, and the discussion was about preference. Therefore, he did not mention it and said: If the effect of pillar on thrice is conceded, then the legislation of wiping, especially without full coverage, is only for ease. So wiping has the effect of ease, so it is not done thrice. As for pillar, its effect is only on completion, and it has been prescribed with full coverage. Also among the valid preferences is constancy of the ruling, i.e., the multiplicity of the legislator's consideration of the description in the ruling, so it has strength that brings predominant probability, like wiping that it is effective in ease in every purification that is not rational, like tayammum, wiping over splint, sock, and khuff, so repetition was not prescribed in them, so it has multiplicity of consideration in ease unlike istinja' with stones, where repetition has been prescribed, for it is rational purification intended for removal of impurity, since repetition in cleaning is effective. As for pillar, it is established for completion. The pillars of prayer, hajj, and ghusl are for completion, but completion differs: in ghusl with repetition, and here in wiping the head with full coverage. Also among the valid preferences is multiplicity of principles over the chosen opinion, for it also gives strength to the analogy, and multiplicity of causes (i.e., proofs) does not necessarily occur to prevent preference by it according to our madhhab that the description to be a cause is single. As long as it is one, the analogy is one, so there is no multiplicity of proofs. Understand. It was said by the author: The third preference is close to the second because multiplicity of the legislator's consideration necessitates multiplicity of principles. And it was said in al-Talwih: The correct view is that the distinction between the three is by consideration: the first looks to the description, the second to the ruling, and the third to the origin. But all go back to strength of effect only. And upon this is Imam Shams al-A'immah. I say: The truth is that the third is more general than the second. For constancy of the ruling per se only occurs when the effect is for the genus of the description or its type on the type of the ruling, so constancy is only multiplicity of effect. But if the effect is only on its genus, then that is only multiplicity of principles and multiplicity of consideration does not apply. And the fact that multiplicity of consideration does not fail to occur from multiplicity of principles is obvious, but this only works if the meaning of multiplicity of consideration is multiplicity of its effect on the exact ruling. If it is more general than that and includes multiplicity of its effect on its genus, it does not work. Moreover, the followers of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'immah claim the denial of effect on the genus simplistically. And the distinction between them by consideration is mistaken. Do you not see that wiping is stronger in ease even if it lacks parallels? Rather, strength is expression of the strength of the suitability between the description and the ruling such that the intellect almost judges its causality barring the Law, as was said regarding intoxication for prohibition. At that point, strength of effect is realized even if there is no multiplicity of consideration or multiplicity of principles. So do not neglect. Also among the valid preferences is the inverse, like the analogy that wiping the head is an incomprehensible wiping, so its repetition is not prescribed, unlike the analogy that it is a pillar, so its repetition is prescribed because it is refuted by rinsing the mouth, which is repeated but is not a pillar. This inverse is the weakest of the means of preference because the ruling is established by various causes, so the lack of a preponderator does not necessitate the lack of one because we have shown that the principle regarding the cause is unity, so the most frequent in it is the inverse. So remember.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage discusses the valid types of tarjih (preference) in qiyas according to the Hanafi school. The author begins by noting that the foundation of all preference is predominant probability (ghalabat al-zann). The Hanafis enumerate eight types of tarjih in this chapter, but only four are valid (sahihah). The first valid type is quwwat al-athar (strength of effect). This means that an analogy is preferred when the attribute (wasf) used as a cause has a stronger effect in bringing about the ruling. Two examples are given: (1) The marriage of a free man to a slave woman when he has access to a free woman. The free man is analogous to a slave, but the attribute of freedom has a stronger effect in expanding the permissible (halal), so the analogy for permissibility is stronger. Al-Shafi'i analogizes differently, making the enslavement of the child as the cause for prohibition, but the Hanafis show this is inconsistent. (2) Wiping the head in wudu: the Hanafis compare it to wiping the khuff—both are wiping and should not be done thrice—while al-Shafi'i treats wiping the head as a pillar like washing the face, so it should be thrice. The Hanafis respond that wiping is for ease, so repetition is not intended. The second valid type is thabat ala al-hukm (constancy of ruling) or kathrat i'tibar al-shar'i (multiplicity of the Lawgiver's consideration of the description in the same ruling). If a description (like al-takhfif, ease) is consistently considered in similar rulings across various contexts, that makes the analogy stronger. Example: Masah (wiping) is for ease, and this effect is seen in many cases like tayammum, wiping over splints, socks, etc., so it has multiplicity of consideration, unlike istinja' with stones which is a rational purification where repetition is intended. The third valid type is kathrat al-usul (multiplicity of principles). If the description is established as effective in many original cases, that gives strength. The authors discuss whether this is distinct from the second type. The fourth valid type is al-aks (the inverse). This is a weak but nevertheless valid form of tarjih, where the absence of a ruling in a case with similar description is used to argue that the description is not the cause. However, since rulings can have multiple causes, this is the weakest. The passage ends with a reminder.

Key Terms
قوة الأثر (quwwat al-athar): Strength of the effect of the descriptive attribute in producing the ruling; one of the valid grounds for preferring one analogy over another.
الثبات على الحكم (al-thabat ala al-hukm): Constancy of the ruling: the repeated consideration by the Lawgiver of the same description in similar rulings, indicating its effectiveness.
كثرة الأصول (kathrat al-usul): Multiplicity of original cases (usul) in which the same description is effective, strengthening the analogy.
العكس (al-aks): Inverse: using the absence of a ruling in a similar case to argue that the description is not the cause, but it is weak because rulings can have multiple causes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi discussion of tarjih in this passage, which of the following is considered the weakest of the four valid grounds for preferring one qiyas over another?
A) Quwwat al-athar (strength of effect)
B) Thabat ala al-hukm (constancy of ruling)
C) Kathrat al-usul (multiplicity of principles)
D) Al-aks (the inverse)

Key Takeaway
The four valid tarjihs in Hanafi usul are quwwat al-athar, thabat ala al-hukm, kathrat al-usul, and al-aks, with al-aks being the weakest.
