Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
No Tarjih by Multiplicity of Qiyas Proofs
Branch ruling applying the principle of non-preference by multiplicity of proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule that multiplicity of analogies does not create preference (tarjih) and explain the example of preemption (shuf'ah).

Core Idea
Multiple analogies with different causes do not combine to outweigh a single conflicting analogy.

This lesson explores a key Hanafi usul principle: that the strength of a qiyas is not enhanced by the existence of multiple other analogies that support the same ruling but are based on different effective causes (ilal). This principle is demonstrated through the ruling on preemption rights (shuf'ah) among co-owners with unequal shares. By examining the dispute with the Shafi'i school, we understand how this principle prevents a form of preference by multiplicity of proofs (tarjih bi kathrat al-adillah).

Guided Path
1
Identify the usul rule
What is the principle being invoked? It states that a qiyas is not given preference by the presence of other analogies that agree in ruling but differ in cause. This is because each qiyas stands on its own illa, and counting them would amount to tarjih bi kathrat al-adillah.
This is an extension of the earlier rule against preference by multiplicity.
2
Understand the rationale
Why is tarjih by multiplicity invalid? Because the strength of a qiyas lies in its specific cause, not in the number of similar rulings. Combining separate illal is not permissible; each must be independently valid.
Otherwise, one could create preference by merely multiplying analogies.
3
Examine the shuf'ah example
How does the principle apply to preemption? Two co-owners with unequal shares both claim the right. The Hanafi view: equal rights, because the right arises from joint ownership itself. The Shafi'i view: proportional rights, analogizing to offspring and fruit of property.
These two views represent conflicting analogies with different illal.
4
Compare the positions
The Hanafi argument: if rights were proportional, it would imply that the Shafi'i analogy is preferred because it is supported by a similar analogy (fruits) — i.e., multiplicity. Since that is invalid, the Hanafi position (equal rights) prevails.
The conflict is resolved not by counting proofs but by assessing the illa.
5
Derive the pratical outcome
The final ruling: both co-owners share the preemption right equally, irrespective of ownership shares.
This is the mufta bihi in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلة عدم الحكم، لكن مع ذلك (فرع) على ما سلف في الأصل الثاني في بحث المعارضة من عدم الترجيح بكثرة الأدلة أن لا يرجح قياس بقياس) آخر موافق له في الحكم (مخالف) له (في العلة) على قياس آخر معارض إياه وكذا كل ما يصلح علة استقلالاً (لا يصلح مرجحاً) وإلا لزم الترجيح بكثرة الدلائل فلم يتفاوت بتفاوت الملك للشفيعين بأن يكون ملك أحدهما ثلث الدار المشفوع بها وللآخر الثلثان (ما يشفعان فيه حتى يكون أثلاثاً بل يستحقانه على التناصف (خلافاً للشافعي) الإمام (له أن الشفعة من مرافق الملك كالولد والثمرة من مرافقه فيقسم بقدر الملك) فيختلفان
The cause is the absence of the ruling, but despite that a branch: based on what preceded in the second chapter on conflict about non-preference by multiplicity of proofs: that an analogy is not preferred over another analogy that agrees with it in ruling but differs in cause over a conflicting analogy. Likewise, every attribute that qualifies as a cause independently does not serve as a preponderator, otherwise it would necessitate preference by multiplicity of proofs. Therefore, the two partners in preemption do not become unequal by the inequality of their ownership such that one owns one-third of the property subject to preemption and the other two-thirds, so that they would share it in thirds, but rather they both become entitled equally. This is contrary to al-Imam al-Shafi'i, who holds that preemption is a concomitant of ownership like offspring and fruit, so it is divided according to ownership, so they differ.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage presents a branch (far') that applies the earlier principle: when two conflicting analogies are presented, and one of them is supported by multiple other analogies that agree in the ruling (but have different causes), that multiplicity does not make it preponderant. The reason is that doing so would amount to preferring by multiplicity of proofs (kathrat al-dalail), which is not valid. The example given is from preemption (shuf'ah). Suppose two owners of a shared property both have the right of preemption if the other sells, but their shares are unequal (one has one-third, the other two-thirds). According to the Hanafis, the right of preemption is equal (half and half) regardless of ownership shares, based on the principle that the right of preemption is established by the joint ownership itself and is not proportionate. Al-Shafi'i, by contrast, analogizes preemption to the fruits of the property, which are divided according to ownership. The Hanafis reject this because that would cause the right to vary with the share, but the text reminds us that this is a consequence of the principle that multiplicity of proofs does not give preference.

Key Terms
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Preemption: a right of a co-owner to purchase the share of a selling co-owner.
الترجيح بكثرة الأدلة (al-tarjih bi kathrat al-adillah): Preference based on multiplicity of proofs; rejected in Hanafi usul when the proofs are separate analogies with different causes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi position stated in this branch, how is the right of preemption (shuf'ah) divided among co-owners of unequal shares?
A) According to their ownership shares
B) Equally among all co-owners
C) According to a judge's discretion
D) The right goes to the oldest owner

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: multiplicity of analogies with distinct causes does not confer preference; applied to preemption, this yields equal rights regardless of ownership shares.

The Third Tarjih: Kathrat al-Usul vs. Kathrat al-I'tibar
Discussion on the correct understanding of the third type of preponderance in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between kathrat al-usul and kathrat al-i'tibar and explain why qawwat al-athar is the ultimate basis for tarjih in Hanafi usul.

Core Idea
True tarjih is strength of suitability (qawwat al-athar), not mere multiplicity.

This passage delves into the nuanced discussion within Hanafi usul about the third type of preponderance (tarjih) in qiyas. While scholars distinguish between multiplicity of origins (kathrat al-usul) and multiplicity of the Lawgiver's consideration (kathrat al-i'tibar), the author argues that both ultimately reduce to the strength of the effect (qawwat al-athar) based on the suitability (munasaba) between the attribute and the ruling. Understanding this hierarchy is critical for correctly weighing analogical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three types of tarjih
The passage refers to three types: (1) qawwat al-athar (strength of effect), (2) kathrat i'tibar al-shari' (multiplicity of Lawgiver's consideration), and (3) kathrat al-usul (multiplicity of origins). Notice how each is defined relative to the attribute, the ruling, or the original case.
These are mentioned in the cited text from al-Talwih.
2
Understand the proposed distinction by consideration
Some scholars, like the followers of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma, distinguish these three by 'consideration': first is about the attribute, second about the ruling, third about the origin. Recognize that this distinction is being critiqued.
The author labels this distinction a mistake.
3
Recognize the author's unification thesis
The author asserts that all three types actually refer to qawwat al-athar. He argues that the third is more general than the second, and that true preponderance lies in the strength of the suitability (munasaba) between attribute and ruling, not in multiplicity alone.
This is the core claim of the passage.
4
Analyze the example of wiping (al-mash)
The author gives the example of wiping over socks as stronger in alleviation even without parallels. This shows that qawwat al-athar can exist without kathrat al-usul or kathrat al-i'tibar. Reflect on how this example illustrates the principle.
Alleviation (takhfif) is the ruling, and wiping is the attribute.
5
Conclude the practical impact on tarjih
The thamarah al-khilaf: when two qiyas conflict, you should prefer the one with stronger munasaba, not merely the one with more origins or considerations. This guides the mufti in selecting the preponderant analogy.
The author warns: 'so do not be heedless'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الترجيح (الثالث: قريب من الترجيح (الثاني) لأن كثرة اعتبار الشارع يوجبه كثرة الأصول (و) قال (في التلويح: والتحرير الحق بأن التفرقة بين الثلاثة بالاعتبار (فالأول أي قوة الأثر (بالنظر إلى الوصف والثاني) أي كثرة اعتبار الشارع بالنظر إلى الحكم والثالث بالنظر إلى الأصل) لكن الكل يرجع إلى قوة الأثر لا غير (وعليه) الإمام شمس الأئمة رحمه الله تعالى (أقول : الحق أن الثالث أعم من الثاني فإن الثبات على الحكم بعينه إنما هو إذا كان التأثير لجنس الوصف أو نوعه في نوع الحكم فإن الثبات إنما هو كثرة التأثير أما إذا كان التأثير في جنسه فقط فذلك كثرة الأصول فقط ولا يصدق فيه كثرة الاعتبار، وأما عدم تخلف كثرة الاعتبار عن كثرة الأصول فظاهر، وهذا إنما يتم لو كان المراد بكثرة الاعتبار كثرة تأثيره في عين الحكم، ولو كان أعم منه ومن كثرة تأثيره في جنسه لم يتم، مع أن أتباع فخر الإسلام وشمس الأئمة يدعون انتفاء التأثير في الجنس بسيطاً (وأما التفرقة بالاعتبار بينها فغلط، ألا ترى المسح أقوى في التخفيف ولو عدم النظائر بل القوة عبارة عن قوة المناسبة بين الوصف والحكم بحيث يكاد يحكم بعليته العقل، ولولا الشرع كما قيل في الإسكار للحرمة) فحينئذ يتحقق قوة الأثر وإن لم تكن كثرة الاعتبار وكثرة الأصول فلا تغفل
The third (tarjih) is close to the second, because the multiplicity of the Lawgiver's consideration is caused by the multiplicity of origins. And he said in al-Talwih: The correct view is that the distinction between the three is by consideration: the first is qawwat al-athar with respect to the attribute, the second is kathrat i'tibar al-shari' with respect to the ruling, and the third is with respect to the origin. But all return to qawwat al-athar and nothing else. And upon this is Imam Shams al-A'imma (may Allah have mercy on him). I say: The truth is that the third is more general than the second, because persistence upon the same ruling is only when the effect is of the genus of the attribute or its type upon the type of the ruling; then persistence is merely multiplicity of effect. But if the effect is only upon its genus, then that is merely multiplicity of origins, and multiplicity of consideration does not apply to it. And the non-absence of multiplicity of consideration from multiplicity of origins is apparent. But this is only complete if by multiplicity of consideration is meant multiplicity of its effect upon the very ruling. And if it is more general than that and includes multiplicity of its effect upon its genus, then it is not complete. Moreover, the followers of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma claim the negation of effect upon the genus simply. And the distinction between them by consideration is a mistake. Do you not see that wiping (on socks) is stronger in alleviation even if it lacks parallels? Rather, strength is the strength of suitability between the attribute and the ruling such that the intellect nearly judges it to be the effective cause, were it not for the Law, as is said about intoxication for prohibition. Then qawwat al-athar is realized even if there is no multiplicity of consideration or multiplicity of origins. So do not be heedless.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage explains the third type of preponderance (tarjih) in qiyas according to the Hanafi usul tradition. The author, likely commenting on the matn, first states that the third tarjih (kathrat al-usul) is close to the second (kathrat i'tibar al-shari'), because multiplicity of the Lawgiver's consideration is caused by multiplicity of origins. He then cites a view from al-Talwih that distinguishes the three types: first is strength of effect (qawwat al-athar) regarding the attribute; second is multiplicity of the Lawgiver's consideration regarding the ruling; third is multiplicity of origins regarding the original case. However, all return to qawwat al-athar. Shams al-A'imma agrees. The author then gives his own view: the third is more general than the second, because persistence on a ruling occurs when the effect is of the same genus or type, which is multiplicity of effect. If the effect is only on the genus, it is merely multiplicity of origins, not multiplicity of consideration. He argues that multiplicity of consideration always accompanies multiplicity of origins if by consideration is meant effect on the very ruling. The followers of Fakhr al-Islam and Shams al-A'imma claim no effect on the genus at all. The author concludes that distinguishing them by consideration is a mistake; true strength lies in the suitability (munasaba) between attribute and ruling, as in the example of wipes for alleviation or the effect of intoxication for prohibition. Thus qawwat al-athar can exist without multiplicity of consideration or origins.

Key Terms
كثرة الاعتبار (kathrat al-i'tibar): Multiplicity of the Lawgiver's consideration of an attribute in rulings, i.e., the attribute being considered in many rulings.
كثرة الأصول (kathrat al-usul): Multiplicity of original cases (usul) in which a particular attribute is considered, without necessarily implying multiplicity of consideration in the same ruling.
قوة الأثر (qawwat al-athar): Strength of the effect, i.e., the strong suitability (munasaba) between the attribute and the ruling such that the intellect nearly judges causation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the author's main argument regarding the relationship between the third type of tarjih (kathrat al-usul) and the second (kathrat al-i'tibar)?
A) They are completely independent and should be evaluated separately.
B) The second is more general than the third.
C) The third is more general than the second, and both return to qawwat al-athar.
D) They are identical in meaning and can be used interchangeably.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, the basis for tarjih in qiyas is the strength of the suitability (qawwat al-athar) between the attribute and the ruling, not merely the multiplicity of origins or considerations.

The Inverse ('Aks) and the Principle Against Tarjih by Multiplicity of 'Illas
Exposition of 'aks as a weak tarjih and a furu' illustrating that multiplicity of 'illas does not give preponderance

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 'aks (inverse) as a valid but weak tarjih method, explain why multiplicity of 'illas does not give preponderance, and apply the efficient cause ('illa fa'iliyya) reasoning to the case of shuf'ah.

Core Idea
The inverse ('aks) is the weakest preponderant; multiplicity of 'illas does not give weight to a qiyas.

This passage weaves together two essential usul principles: the validity of 'aks (inverse) as a preponderant (though weak) and the firm rule that multiplicity of 'illas cannot strengthen a proof. The discussion culminates in a fierce legal dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over pre-emption (shuf'ah) rights, which serves as a concrete illustration of these abstract principles.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two main claims
Recognize that the passage discusses 'aks as a valid tarjih and then states the rule that multiplicity of proofs does not give preponderance.
The branch (far') on shuf'ah ties these together.
2
Analyze the 'aks example
Examine the example of wiping: wiping the head is not rationally understood, so repetition is not sunnah; but wiping as a pillar is repeated. The inverse is weak because the opposite case does not necessarily negate the ruling.
The default for 'illa is unity; multiplicity is the inverse.
3
Understand why 'aks is weak
A ruling can be established by various 'illas, so the absence of a particular 'illa does not mean the ruling is absent. Thus 'aks does not provide strong preponderance.
This is why 'aks is considered the weakest face of tarjih.
4
Apply the principle to shuf'ah
In pre-emption, two co-owners with unequal shares (e.g., 1/3 and 2/3) both have equal rights to pre-empt the entire property, not proportional shares. This illustrates that multiplicity of 'illas (different ownership shares) does not create proportional pre-emption rights.
The efficient cause ('illa fa'iliyya) does not divide.
5
Compare the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions
The Shafi'i view treats pre-emption as an appurtenance that divides proportionally. The Hanafi response distinguishes material causes from efficient causes and emphasizes the purpose of repelling harm.
The dispute is not about tarjih by multiplicity but about the nature of the 'illa.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومنها أي من التراجيح الصحيحة العكس كمسح أي كالقياس بأن مسح الرأس مسح لا يعقل فلا يسن تكراره بخلاف القياس بأنه ركن فيسن تكراره لأنه منقوض بالمضمضة فإنها تكررت وليست ركناً وهذا أي العكس أضعف الوجوه للترجيح لأن الحكم يثبت بعلل شتى فلا يستلزم عدم مرجح لما بينا أن الأصل في العلة الاتحاد، فالأكثر فيها العكس فتذكر. العلة عدم الحكم، لكن مع ذلك فرع على ما سلف في الأصل الثاني في بحث المعارضة من عدم الترجيح بكثرة الأدلة أن لا يرجح قياس بقياس آخر موافق له في الحكم مخالف له في العلة على قياس آخر معارض إياه وكذا كل ما يصلح علة استقلالاً لا يصلح مرجحاً وإلا لزم الترجيح بكثرة الدلائل فلم يتفاوت بتفاوت الملك للشفيعين بأن يكون ملك أحدهما ثلث الدار المشفوع بها وللآخر الثلثان ما يشفعان فيه حتى يكون أثلاثاً بل يستحقانه على التناصف خلافاً للشافعي الإمام له أن الشفعة من مرافق الملك كالولد والثمرة من مرافقه فيقسم بقدر الملك فيختلفان في الاستحقاق وأجيب بأن ذلك الانقسام في العلل المادية فقط، بل المرافق في العرف إنما تقال لما تولد منه، ولذا قال في «الهداية»: إن تملك ملك الغير لا يجعل ثمرة من ثمرات ملكه وهذه كا لعلة الفاعلية فلا تنقسم بانقسامها وقد جعل الشارع الملك مطلقاً علة للشفعة دفعاً لضرر جوار السوء فجعل كل جزء من العلة علة لجزء المعلول نصب الشرع بالرأي فهو باطل، بل أي قدر من الجوار فرض علة مستقلة لاستحقاق كل المشفوعة أقول فيه ما فيه لأنه ليس مبني كلام الشافعي رضي الله عنه على أن الترجيح بكثرة العلل، ولا جوابنا متوقف عليه، بل مبني النزاع أن كل جزء من المشفوع بها علة لجزء من الشفعة أم لا فتأمل
And among the valid preponderances is the inverse ('aks), like wiping: wiping the head is a wiping that is not rationally understood, so its repetition is not sunnah, unlike wiping as a pillar (in wudu) so its repetition is sunnah because it is refuted by rinsing the mouth (madmada) which is repeated but is not a pillar. And this, i.e., the inverse, is the weakest basis for preponderance because the ruling is established by various 'illas, so it does not necessitate the absence of a preponderant according to what we stated that the principle regarding the 'illa is unity; thus the majority regarding it is the inverse, so remember. The 'illa is the absence of the ruling. But despite that, a branch on what preceded in the second principle in the discussion of opposition: that there is no preponderance by multiplicity of proofs, so one qiyas should not be given preponderance over another qiyas that agrees with it in ruling but differs in 'illa over another qiyas opposing it. And likewise everything that can serve as an 'illa independently does not serve as a preponderant, otherwise it would necessitate preponderance by multiplicity of proofs. So it does not vary with variation of ownership for the two pre-emptors (shafi'an) such that one owns a third of the property subject to pre-emption and the other two-thirds, so that they pre-empt it in thirds; rather they deserve it on a half-and-half basis, contrary to al-Shafi'i. He argues that pre-emption is one of the appurtenances of ownership like a child and fruit are its appurtenances, so it is divided according to ownership, so they differ in entitlement. And the answer is that such division is only in material 'illas; rather appurtenances in convention are only said for what is generated from it. Hence it was said in al-Hidayah: 'Owning the property of another does not make a fruit of his ownership.' And this is like the efficient cause (al-'illa al-fa'iliyya); it does not divide by its division. The Lawgiver has made ownership absolutely an 'illa for pre-emption to repel the harm of a bad neighbor, so He made every part of the 'illa an 'illa for a part of the effect. To set up the Law by opinion is false. Rather, whatever amount of neighborliness is assumed is an independent 'illa for entitlement to the entire property subject to pre-emption. I say: There is something in it, because the statement of al-Shafi'i is not based on preponderance by multiplicity of 'illas, nor does our answer depend on it. Rather the dispute is based on whether every part of the property subject to pre-emption is an 'illa for a part of the pre-emption or not. So ponder.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage first discusses the inverse ('aks) as a type of valid tarjih, illustrated by wiping: wiping the head in wudu is not rationally understood (ghayr ma'qul), so repetition is not recommended, but wiping as a pillar (for tayammum?) is repeated? Actually, the example is that wiping as a pillar is repeated, but it is refuted by rinsing the mouth which is repeated but not a pillar. The 'aks is considered the weakest because rulings can be established by various 'illas, so the absence of a preponderant is not necessitated; the default for 'illa is unity, so multiplicity is the inverse. Then a branch (far') is presented: based on the principle that multiplicity of proofs (kathrat al-adilla) does not give preponderance, one qiyas should not be preferred over another solely because it agrees in ruling but has a different 'illa. The example of shuf'ah (pre-emption) illustrates this: two co-owners with unequal shares (one third and two thirds) both have pre-emption rights, but the shares are not divided proportionally; they each have equal right to the entire property. Al-Shafi'i disagrees, treating pre-emption as an appurtenance of ownership that divides proportionally. The Hanafi response distinguishes material 'illas from efficient causes; property is the efficient cause for pre-emption and does not divide. The Lawgiver made ownership an absolute cause for pre-emption, so each part of ownership entitles to the whole. The author adds that the dispute with Shafi'i is not about tarjih by multiplicity of 'illas but about whether parts of the property are independent causes for parts of the pre-emption right.

Key Terms
العكس (al-'aks): The inverse: a type of tarjih where the opposite case indicates the weakness of a qiyas, considered the weakest form.
الشفعة (al-shuf'ah): Pre-emption: the right of a co-owner to purchase the share of a new owner to prevent harm from a bad neighbor.
العلة الفاعلية (al-'illa al-fa'iliyya): Efficient cause: a cause that does not divide by division of its subject; contrasts with material cause.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) In proportion to their ownership (1/3 and 2/3)
B) Equally, each having right to the entire property
C) The one with 2/3 has full right
D) They do not have any right

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, 'aks is the weakest preponderant, and multiplicity of 'illas does not give a qiyas preference over another; the efficient cause concept explains equal pre-emption rights.

Invalid Types of Tarjih in Qiyas
Explaining invalid types of preponderance in qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four invalid types of tarjih in qiyas according to Hanafi usul and explain why each is rejected.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of 'illas, counting resemblances, transitivity, and simplicity are all invalid grounds for preponderance.

In Hanafi usul, not every factor that seems to strengthen an 'illa is valid. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut enumerates four fallacious methods of tarjih that were mistakenly considered valid by some scholars. Understanding these invalid types protects the student from common errors in qiyas and clarifies the Hanafi criterion: only effective meaning (ta'thir) gives genuine strength.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first invalid type: multiplicity of 'illas
Recognize that tarjih by kathrat al-'ilal (having multiple 'illas) is invalid because each 'illa is independent and multiplicity does not add strength. The passage reminds that this was already known.
This is the most obvious invalid type; it is a baseline.
2
Analyze the second type: preponderance of shabah (resemblances)
Understand the Hanafi rejection of ghalabat al-ashbah. Each shabah is an 'illa, and multiplicity does not preponderate. Note the contrast with Shafi'i who accept this tarjih. Study the example: brother vs. paternal cousin.
The example shows that counting resemblances leads to incorrect rulings.
3
Examine the third type: increased transitivity (ziyadat al-ta'diya)
Tarjih based on an 'illa applying to more cases is invalid because strength lies in the effectiveness of the 'illa, not its scope. The example of taste (tu'm) in riba illustrates that a more transitory 'illa is not stronger.
Transitivity does not equal strength; a less transitory 'illa may be more effective.
4
Evaluate the fourth type: simplicity (basata)
Tarjih by simplicity of the 'illa (not composite) is invalid. The criterion for preponderance is the effective meaning, not how many parts the 'illa has. Both simple and composite 'illas can be equally strong or weak.
Simplicity offers no logical advantage in causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما التراجيح الفاسدة فمنها بكثرة العلل وقد عرفت ومنها الترجيح بغلبة الأشباه وإذا كان فاسداً فلا يقدم ذو شبهين على ذي شبه واحد خلافاً للشافعي رضي الله عنه فإنه يقبل هذا الترجيح ويقدم ذا شبهين وإنما قلنا بفساده لأن كل شبه علة ولا ترجيح بكثرة العلل كما لو قيل : الأخ كالأبوين في المحرمية فله بهما شبه واحد ومثل ابن العم في حل الحليلة أي حليلته له و حل أخذ الزكاة منه والشهادة له والقصاص إذا قتل أحد الأخوين الآخر، فله مع ابن العم أشباه فيلحق به فلا يعتق بالملك كابن العم وهذا فاسد. فإن هذه الأشباه علل بزعمكم فلا ترجيح لها وإلا فلا دخل لها في الحكم بل الموجب عندنا القرابة المحرمية المقتضية للصلة ومنها الترجيح بزيادة التعدية كالطعم في باب الربا يعم القليل في التفاح دون الكيل ولا أثر له أي لهذا في الترجيح بل إنما الترجيح للقوة في التأثير وبزيادة التعدية لا تثبت القوة ومنها الترجيح بالبساطة أي بساطة العلة وعدم تركبها من أجزاء كالطعم أي كترجيحه على الكيل والجنس في باب الربا مع أن المختصر والمطول سواء في البيان والعبرة للمعاني التي بها التأثير فلا ترجيح بها أصلاً كذا في البديع
As for the invalid preponderances: among them is by multiplicity of 'illas, and you have already known. And among them is preponderance by preponderance of shabah (resemblances). And since it is invalid, one with two shabahs should not be preferred over one with one shabah, contrary to al-Shafi'i (may Allah be pleased with him), for he accepts this preponderance and prefers the one with two shabahs. We only said it is invalid because every shabah is a 'illa, and there is no preponderance by multiplicity of 'illas. As if it is said: the brother is like the parents in mahramiyya (prohibited relationship), so he has one shabah with them. And like the paternal cousin (ibn 'amm) in the permissibility of his wife (hall al-halila), the permissibility of taking zakat from him, giving testimony for him, and retaliation if one brother kills the other. So he has multiple shabahs with the paternal cousin, so he is attached to him and thus he is not freed by ownership like the paternal cousin. And this is invalid. For these shabahs are 'illas according to your claim, so there is no preponderance for them; otherwise they have no role in the ruling; rather the effective factor for us is the prohibiting kinship that necessitates connection. And among them is preponderance by increased transitivity (ziyadat al-ta'diya), like the taste (tu'm) in the chapter of riba: it is general (ya'mm) in the little amount of the apple, but not in measurement (kayl). And it has no effect on preponderance. Rather, preponderance is only due to strength in effect, and increased transitivity does not establish strength. And among them is preponderance by simplicity (basata), i.e., simplicity of the 'illa and its not being composed of parts, like taste: i.e., preferring it over measurement and type in the chapter of riba, even though the abbreviated and detailed are equal in clarification. And the criterion is the meanings by which effect is produced, so there is no preponderance from these at all. Thus it is in al-Badi'.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage enumerates invalid types of tarjih (preponderance) in qiyas according to Hanafi usul. First, tarjih by multiplicity of 'illas (kathrat al-'ilal) is invalid because multiplicity of 'illas does not give preponderance, as previously established. Second, tarjih by preponderance of shabah (resemblances) is also invalid. The author contrasts the Hanafi position with al-Shafi'i, who does accept tarjih by similarity. The Hanafi argument: each shabah is an 'illa, and multiplicity of 'illas does not preponderate. An example is the brother: he is similar to parents in mahramiyya (one shabah). The paternal cousin (ibn 'amm) has multiple shabahs (like permissibility of his wife, taking zakat, testimony, retaliation). If we follow the invalid rule, the paternal cousin would be attached to him (like the cousin?) and would not be freed by ownership. But this is false; the real cause is prohibiting kinship. Third, tarjih by increased transitivity (ziyadat al-ta'diya) is invalid as in the example of 'taste' in riba: taste covers small amounts of apples but not measurement, so it is not a valid preponderant; preponderance is based on strength of effect, not transitivity. Fourth, tarjih by simplicity (basata) is invalid: preferring a simple 'illa (like taste) over a complex one (like measurement and type) does not give preponderance because the criterion is the effective meaning, not simplicity. The author cites al-Badi' as his source.

Key Terms
غلبة الأشباه (ghalabat al-ashbah): Preponderance of resemblances: an invalid tarjih that gives weight to an attribute based on the number of similarities it has with multiple rulings.
زيادة التعدية (ziyadat al-ta'diya): Increased transitivity: the idea that an 'illa that applies to more cases is stronger, considered invalid for tarjih.
البساطة (al-basata): Simplicity of the 'illa (not composite); an invalid basis for tarjih.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an invalid type of tarjih according to this passage?
A) Preponderance by strength of effect
B) Preponderance by simplicity of 'illa
C) Preponderance by consensus
D) Preponderance by explicit text

Key Takeaway
Hanafi usul rejects tarjih based on multiplicity of 'illas, resemblance counting, transitivity, and simplicity; only effective meaning (ta'thir) is the valid basis for preponderance in qiyas.

Refuting False Methods of Tarjih in Qiyas
Refutes false tarjih methods in qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify four false methods of tarjih in qiyas and explain why the Hanafi school rejects each, particularly the dispute with Imam al-Shafi'i over ghalabat al-ashbah.

Core Idea
Multiplicity of causes or similarities does not strengthen a legal analogy; only the effective cause matters.

In Hanafi usul, not every type of preference is valid. This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut refutes four common but false methods of tarjih in qiyas. The author argues that preference based on the mere number of causes or similarities is invalid, and only the strength of the effective cause matters. The main dispute is with Imam al-Shafi'i, who accepted preference by prevalence of similarities.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four false tarjih methods
List the four methods explicitly mentioned: kathrat al-'ilal (multiplicity of causes), ghalabat al-ashbah (prevalence of similarities), ziyadat al-ta'diyah (increased transitivity), and basatah (simplicity).
Each is presented as a separate false method in the text.
2
Understand the argument against multiplicity of causes
The author argues that each 'illah is independent; adding multiple causes does not increase the strength of the analogy. The example from shuf'ah is used: every part of neighborliness is an independent cause for pre-emption, but they do not accumulate.
This is a rational argument based on the nature of causality.
3
Analyze the Shafi'i dispute on ghalabat al-ashbah
Imam al-Shafi'i accepts preference by prevalence of similarities, e.g., a brother has multiple similarities with a cousin but only one with parents. Hanafis reject this because each similarity is a claim for an 'illah, and multiplicity does not strengthen. The effective cause is the prohibiting relationship (qarabah muharramah), not the number of similarities.
The brother/cousin example illustrates the practical difference.
4
Recognize why increased transitivity and simplicity are invalid
Increased transitivity (e.g., taste applying to many items) does not prove strength; strength is in the effect itself. Simplicity of the cause also does not indicate effectiveness. Only the meaning that produces an effect (ma'na mu'aththir) matters.
These are further examples of methodologically flawed reasoning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
في الاستحقاق (وأجيب بأن ذلك الانقسام في العلل المادية فقط، بل المرافق في العرف إنما تقال لما تولد منه، ولذا قال في «الهداية»: إن تملك ملك الغير لا يجعل ثمرة من ثمرات ملكه (وهذه كالعلة الفاعلية) فلا تنقسم بانقسامها وقد جعل الشارع الملك مطلقاً علة للشفعة) دفعاً لضرر جوار السوء فجعل كل جزء من العلة علة لجزء المعلول نصب الشرع بالرأي فهو باطل، بل أي قدر من الجوار فرض علة مستقلة لاستحقاق كل المشفوعة (أقول: فيه ما فيه لأنه ليس مبني كلام الشافعي رضي الله عنه على أن الترجيح بكثرة العلل، ولا جوابنا متوقف عليه، بل مبني النزاع أن كل جزء من المشفوع بها علة لجزء من الشفعة أم لا فتأمل، وأما التراجيح الفاسدة فمنها بكثرة العلل وقد عرفت (ومنها الترجيح بغلبة الأشباه) وإذا كان فاسداً فلا يقدم ذو شبهين على ذي شبه واحد خلافاً للشافعي رضي الله عنه فإنه يقبل هذا الترجيح ويقدم ذا شبهين وإنما قلنا بفساده لأن كل شبه علة ولا ترجيح بكثرة العلل كما لو قيل: الأخ كالأبوين في المحرمية فله بهما شبه واحد (ومثل ابن العم في حل الحليلة أي حليلته له و) حل أخذ الزكاة منه (والشهادة له (والقصاص إذا قتل أحد الأخوين الآخر، فله مع ابن العم أشباه فيلحق به فلا يعتق بالملك كابن العم) وهذا فاسد. فإن هذه الأشباه علل بزعمكم فلا ترجيح لها وإلا فلا دخل لها في الحكم بل الموجب عندنا القرابة المحرمية المقتضية للصلة (ومنها) الترجيح بزيادة التعدية كالطعم في باب الربا يعم القليل في التفاح دون الكيل ولا أثر له أي لهذا في الترجيح (بل) إنما الترجيح للقوة في التأثير وبزيادة التعدية لا تثبت القوة (ومنها) الترجيح بالبساطة أي بساطة العلة وعدم تركبها من أجزاء كالطعم أي كترجيحه على الكيل والجنس في باب الربا مع أن المختصر والمطول سواء في البيان والعبرة للمعاني التي بها التأثير فلا ترجيح بها أصلاً كذا في البديع.
Regarding entitlement (it was answered that that division is only in material causes, rather the companion in custom is only said of what is generated from it. Hence it was said in al-Hidayah: that owning another's property does not make it a fruit of his ownership. And these are like the efficient cause, so they do not divide by its division. And the Lawgiver made absolute ownership a cause for pre-emption, to repel the harm of bad neighborliness, so He made every part of the cause a cause for part of the effect. Setting up law by opinion is false. Instead, any amount of neighborliness is considered an independent cause for entitlement to the entire pre-empted property. I say: there is something in it because the statement of al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, is not based on preference by multiplicity of causes, nor is our answer dependent on it, rather the dispute is based on whether every part of the pre-empted property is a cause for part of the pre-emption or not. So contemplate. As for false preferences: among them is preference by multiplicity of causes, and you have known it. And among them is preference by prevalence of similarities. If it is false, then one with two similarities should not be preferred over one with one similarity, contrary to al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, for he accepts this preference and prefers the one with two similarities. We only say it is false because every similarity is a cause, and there is no preference by multiplicity of causes. For example, if it is said: the brother is like both parents in the prohibition of marriage, so he has one similarity with them. And like the cousin in the permissibility of his wife, and the permissibility of taking zakat from him, and giving testimony for him, and retaliation if one brother kills the other, so he has similarities with the cousin, so he is attached to him and is not freed by ownership like the cousin. And this is false, for these similarities are causes in your claim, so there is no preference for them, otherwise they have no bearing on the ruling, rather the effective factor according to us is the prohibiting relationship that necessitates connection. And among them is preference by increased transitivity, like the taste in the chapter of usury, it applies to the small quantity in the apple, not to the measuring weight, and it has no effect in preference, rather preference is for strength in effect, and increased transitivity does not establish strength. And among them is preference by simplicity, i.e., simplicity of the cause and its lack of composition from parts, like the taste, i.e., preferring it over the measuring weight and genus in the chapter of usury, even though the brief and the lengthy are equal in clarity, and consideration is of the meanings that have effect, so there is no preference by them at all. Thus in al-Badi'.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of several false methods of tarjih (preference) in qiyas. The author begins by addressing an earlier argument about the division of causes, then states that the false preferences include: (1) Preference by multiplicity of causes (kathrat al-'ilal). The author argues that multiplicity does not strengthen a cause; each cause is independent and cannot be added. (2) Preference by prevalence of similarities (ghalabat al-ashbah). Here the author disagrees with Imam al-Shafi'i, who accepts this type of tarjih. The Hanafi position is that each similarity is a separate cause, and since multiplicity of causes does not afford preference, having two similarities is no better than one. An example is given: a brother is like both parents in one similarity (prohibition of marriage) and also has multiple similarities with a cousin (permissibility of wife, zakat, testimony, retaliation), but this does not make him like the cousin in terms of not being freed by ownership. The effective factor according to Hanafis is the prohibiting relationship, not the number of similarities. (3) Preference by increased transitivity (ziyadat al-ta'diyah). For example, in usury, taste is considered a cause that applies to many items, but this does not make it stronger; strength is in the effect itself. (4) Preference by simplicity (basatah) of the cause. Simplicity does not make a cause stronger; what matters is the effective meaning. Thus, all these methods are invalid according to Hanafi usul.

Key Terms
ترجيح (tarjīḥ): Preferring one evidence or cause over another when they conflict.
علة (ʿillah): The effective cause or ratio legis in qiyas that brings about the ruling.
شبه (shabbah): A similarity that suggests a cause but is not the effective cause itself.
تعدية (taʿdiyah): Transitivity or application of a cause to multiple cases.
بساطة (basāṭah): Simplicity of the cause, i.e., not composed of multiple parts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school reject preference by prevalence of similarities (ghalabat al-ashbah)?
A) Because similarities cannot be counted reliably.
B) Because each similarity is an independent cause and multiplicity does not strengthen.
C) Because the Shafi'i school rejects it.
D) Because similarities are irrelevant to qiyas.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi usul, preference in qiyas is based on the strength of the effective cause, not on multiplicity of causes or similarities.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Fasad al-I'tibar: The First Objection to Qiyas in Hanafi Usul
Defines munazarah and introduces the first type of objection to qiyas: fasad al-i'tibar

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify fasad al-i'tibar as the first objection to qiyas, list the five responses to it, and apply them to the Shafi'i example on tasmiyah.

Core Idea
Fasad al-i'tibar invalidates a qiyas that contradicts a text or consensus, but the text must be immune to interpretation or preponderance.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut introduces the etiquette of debate (adab al-munazarah) and the various objections that can be raised against an argument. We will focus on the first type of objection to qiyas: fasad al-i'tibar, or 'invalidity of consideration.' Understanding this concept is crucial for evaluating when a qiyas can be set aside due to conflict with a clear text or consensus. The passage also provides a detailed example involving Imam al-Shafi'i regarding the permissibility of meat when the name of Allah is intentionally omitted.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish munazarah from jidal and mukabarah
Define munazarah as a scholarly dispute aimed at manifesting truth, not mere argumentation or stubbornness, as stated in the first lines of the passage.
This sets the ethical framework for the discussion of objections.
2
Identify the three types of objections
The passage states that every discussion involves either man' (rejecting a premise), naqd (refutation by showing an exception or impossibility), or mu'aradah (presenting conflicting evidence). Note that naqd and mu'aradah reverse the roles of proponent and objector.
These are the general objection categories before focusing on qiyas.
3
Define fasad al-i'tibar
Fasad al-i'tibar is the first of five specific objections to qiyas. It occurs when the qiyas contradicts a text (nass) or consensus (ijma) that prevents the effective cause (illat) from being established. The passage describes it as 'invalidity of consideration.'
Refer to the legal definition dissector for the breakdown.
4
List the five responses to fasad al-i'tibar
The passage provides five ways to counter a claim of fasad al-i'tibar: (1) challenge the chain (sanad) if the text is khabar ahad; (2) deny that the text indicates the conflicting ruling; (3) state that the text is subject to interpretation (ta'wil); (4) state that it is specified (takhsis) by another evidence; (5) seek preponderance (tarjih) so that the qiyas takes precedence, or counter with a similar text (mu'aradah) to cancel both.
These responses show that fasad al-i'tibar is not automatic.
5
Analyze the Shafi'i example
The passage presents Imam al-Shafi'i's objection: the Hanafi qiyas that permits meat when a Muslim intentionally omits tasmiyah is invalid because it contradicts the Quranic verse (6:119). The Hanafi response is that the verse is interpreted (ta'wil) to refer to idolatrous slaughter, not to Muslims, thus the qiyas is not actually contradictory.
This example illustrates the practical application of the five responses.

فصل: في آداب المناظرة (وهي المخاصمة لإظهار الصواب احترز به عن المجادلة التي المقصود منها إلزام الخصم، والمكابرة التي بها يعجز عن إظهار الصواب. اعلم أن المستدل إذا بين دعواه بدليل فإن خفي على الخصم على قانون الاستعمال حقيقة كان أو مجازاً، والحق ما قال المصنف، فإن الفهم بعد البيان غير متوقف عليه وكفى للمقصود. فإذا اتضح مراده فإن كانت مقدماته مسلمة ولا خلل فيها بوجه لا تفصيلاً ولا إجمالاً لزم المقدمة الممنوعة، أو كان الخلل فيها إجمالاً من غير تعيين لمقدمة أصلاً، وذلك الخلل إما بتخلف الحكم عنه في صورة فيكون الدليل حينئذ أعم من المدعي أو لزوم محال آخر فينقض حينئذ ويدعي فساد الدليل، فلا بد من إقامة دليل، وإما بوجود دليل مقابل لدليل المستدل وحاكم بمنافي ما يحكم هو به فيعارض. وفي هذين أي النقض والمعارضة تنقلب المناصب فيصير المعترض مستدلاً، والمستدل معترضاً. وكل مقدمة من الدليل استدل عليها، فالكلام فيه كالكلام في أصل المدعي المدلل. فكل بحث إما منع أو نقض أو معارضة، لا كما زعم ناقلا أنه منحصر في الطرفين، وأما التوجيه بأن النقض يرجع إلى المنع مع السند فلا يخفى فساده. ثم الأسئلة الواردة على القياس أنواع خمسة: النوع الأول: ما يمنع التمكن من القياس لعدم كون المحل صالحاً له ويسمى فساد الاعتبار، وهو مخالفة القياس للنص أو الاجماع اللذين يمنعان التعليل، وأدرجه في «الكشف» عن بعض الكتب الأصولية في فساد الوضع وجعله أحد نوعيه، وحاصله مخالفته لما هو مقدم شرعاً. وجوابه بالطعن في السند إن كانا مرويين بالآحاد أو بمنع دلالته على المنافي لحكم القياس أو بأنه مؤوّل أو مخصص بدليله أو بترجيحه بسبيله أي بسبيل الترجيح بحيث يتقدم على الخبر أو بالمعارضة بمثله فبالمعارضة تساقطا ويبقى القياس معمولاً. ولا يجب بيان المساواة الواقعية في إبداء المعارض لأنه متعسر لا يكاد يوجد فيسد باب المعارضة، إلا أن للمعترض أن يرجح خبره فتفوت المعارضة. نعم: يجب أن لا يكون هذا الخبر مرجوحاً اتفاقاً كآحاد مع مشهور فإن مراعاة هذا تتيسر. واعلم أن الصحابة كانوا يرجعون عند تعارض النصوص مطلقاً إلى القياس وإن كان في جانب واحد وفي آخر أكثر، فعلم أن لا ترجيح بالكثرة للأدلة. فلو عارض المعترض بنص آخر لم يسمع فإن الواحد يعارض الاثنين من غير ترجيح. وعلم أيضاً أن لا معارضة بين النص والقياس، وإلا كان الأخذ بالقياس تحكماً، فلو قال المستدل: عارض نصك قياسي لم يجز، والسر فيه أن الضعيف كالقياس وإن اضمحل في مقابلة القوي، لكن ربما يرجح المساوي عند التعارض كالعدالة الإسلام، فإنه مرجح. وهذا بظاهره يدل على أن الدليل بالذات هو النص، والقياس مرجح إياه، وهذا ناظر إلى ما ذهب إليه البعض أن الدليل الضعيف يرجح القوي، وهذا غير سديد، فإن كان دليل يصلح بنفسه حجة لا يرجح غيره، بل الحق أن الدليل بالذات القياس، والخبران المتعارضان تساقطا، وقد مر ما يكفي للمسترشد. وعلم أيضاً أن المعتبر في فساد الاعتبار مخالفة نص سالم عن المعارض، كيف وبالمعارض يسقط هذا وهو فلم يبق مخالف حتى يفسد اعتباره، تدبر. مثاله ما يورد من قبل الشافعي الإمام رضي الله عنه على صحة حل متروك التسمية عامداً والذابح مسلم: ذبح التارك للتسمية وقع من أهله وهو من له ملة في محله وهو المذبوح الحلال ذبحه فيحل به المذبوح كالناسي في تركها تحل ذبيحته، فيقال: هذا القياس فاسد الاعتبار لقوله تعالى: {وَلَا تَأْكُلُوا مِمَّا لَمْ يُذْكَرِ اسْمُ اللَّهِ عَلَيْهِ} [الأنعام: ١١٩] الآية. فيجاب من قبلهم بأنه مؤوّل بذبح الوثني بقوله صلى الله عليه وآله وأصحابه وسلم.
Chapter: On the Etiquette of Debate (It is the dispute for the purpose of manifesting correctness, distinguished from argumentation whose aim is to refute the opponent, and from stubbornness by which one is unable to manifest correctness). Know that when the proponent presents his claim with evidence, if the opponent fails to understand the meaning of his speech due to brevity or oddity in usage, he should ask for clarification, and it is upon the proponent to explain his intent when asked; otherwise it remains unknown, and debate cannot proceed, even if it is without transmission from language or the people of custom or without mentioning a contextual indicator. Some have stipulated the use of a word according to the rules of usage, whether literal or figurative, but the correct view is what the author said, for understanding after explanation is not dependent on that, and it suffices for the purpose. When his intent becomes clear, if his premises are conceded and free from any defect, whether detailed or general, then the discussion must end and correctness becomes manifest. If not all of his premises are sound and conceded, but some are defective, then if the defect is in a specific premise, the opponent may object to that premise, whether without evidence or with evidence, and demand proof for it, so the proponent responds by proving the objected premise. Or if the defect is general, without specifying any premise, that defect is either that the ruling is absent from a case, making the evidence more general than the claim, or it leads to an impossibility, in which case it is refuted (naqd) and the evidence is claimed to be invalid, so another proof must be provided. Or else there may be a counter-evidence opposing the proponent's evidence and ruling contrary to what he rules, so it is contradictory (mu'aradah). In these two cases, i.e., naqd and mu'aradah, the positions are reversed, so the objector becomes the proponent and the proponent becomes the objector. Every premise of the evidence that is argued about is like the original claim. Thus every discussion is either objection (man'), refutation (naqd), or contradiction (mu'aradah), not as a transmitter claimed that it is confined to two sides. As for the notion that naqd reduces to man' along with evidence, its falsity is clear. Then the questions directed at qiyas are of five types: The first type: what prevents the possibility of qiyas because the subject is not suitable for it, and it is called fasad al-i'tibar (invalidity of consideration), which is the contradiction of qiyas with a text or consensus that prevent the effective cause. It is included in al-Kashf from some usul books under fasad al-wad' (invalidity of postulation), making it one of its two types. Its essence is its contradiction with what is legally established. Its response is by challenging the chain of transmission if the texts are transmitted by ahad reports, or by denying its indication of what contradicts the ruling of qiyas, or by stating that it is subject to interpretation (ta'wil) or specification (takhsis) by its evidence, or by preponderance (tarjih) in such a way that it takes precedence over the hadith, or by countering with a similar text, and by countering they cancel each other out, and the qiyas remains operative. It is not necessary to demonstrate actual equivalence in presenting the counter-evidence, because it is difficult and rarely exists, which would close the door of mu'aradah, except that the objector may preponderate his hadith, and then mu'aradah is lost. Yes, it is necessary that this hadith not be outweighed by consensus, like a solitary hadith against a well-known one, for observing this is easy. Know that the Companions, may Allah be pleased with them, when texts conflicted, they would absolutely resort to qiyas, even if it was on one side and there were more on the other. So it is known that there is no preference by multiplicity of evidences. If the objector tries to contradict with another text, it is not accepted, for one contradicts two without preference. It is also known that there is no contradiction between text and qiyas, for if there were, acting on qiyas would be arbitrary. If the proponent says: 'My qiyas contradicts your text,' it is not permissible. The secret is that the weak (like qiyas) even if it disappears in the face of the strong, but sometimes the equal is preferred when they conflict, like the justice of Islam, for it is a preponderator. This apparently indicates that the evidence per se is the text, and qiyas preponderates it. This looks to what some have held that weak evidence preponderates strong evidence, but this is not sound, for if there is evidence that is valid by itself, it does not preponderate another. Rather the truth is that the evidence per se is qiyas, and the two conflicting texts cancel each other out. And what suffices for the seeker has already been mentioned. It is also known that what is considered in fasad al-i'tibar is contradicting a text that is free from contradiction. How? With a contradictory text, this text falls, so there remains no contradiction to invalidate consideration. Reflect. Its example is what is raised by Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, regarding the validity of permissibility of that over which the name of Allah was intentionally omitted and the slaughterer is Muslim: The slaughter of the one who omitted the name occurred from its people (i.e., one who has a religion) in its place (i.e., the lawful slaughtered animal), so it makes the animal lawful, like the one who forgets to mention the name, his slaughtered animal is lawful. So it is said: This qiyas is invalid due to contradiction with the saying of Allah: {And eat not of that over which the name of Allah has not been mentioned} [al-An'am 6:119]. They respond by stating that it is interpreted to refer to the slaughter of the idolater, based on his statement, peace be upon him and his family and Companions.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a chapter on the etiquette of debate (adab al-munazarah). It first defines munazarah as a dispute aimed at manifesting the truth, distinguishing it from mere argumentation (jidal) and stubbornness (mukabarah). The proponent must present his claim with evidence. If the opponent does not understand due to brevity or oddity, he may ask for clarification, and the proponent must explain. This is essential for the debate to proceed. The author then discusses the types of objections to a qiyas argument: if all premises are conceded and sound, the debate ends. If there is a defect, the opponent may object by man' (rejecting a specific premise), naqd (refuting by showing the ruling is absent in a case or leads to impossibility), or mu'aradah (presenting a contradictory evidence). In naqd and mu'aradah, the roles reverse. The passage then focuses on the first type of objection to qiyas: fasad al-i'tibar, which is when the qiyas contradicts a text or consensus that prevents the effective cause. This is known as 'invalidity of consideration.' The response to such an objection is by challenging the authenticity (sanad) of the text, or showing it is subject to ta'wil or takhsis, or by seeking tarjih, or by countering with a similar text. The author notes that the Companions would resort to qiyas when texts conflicted, even if the qiyas was weaker in number. There is no preference by multiplicity of evidences. Interestingly, the author states that there is no contradiction between text and qiyas because the weak (qiyas) disappears in the face of the strong (text), but when two texts cancel each other out, qiyas becomes the operative evidence. The example given involves Imam al-Shafi'i: he used qiyas on the permissibility of meat over which the name of Allah was intentionally omitted by a Muslim, comparing it to forgetfulness. This qiyas is challenged by the Quranic verse forbidding eating of that over which Allah's name is not mentioned. The Hanafi response is that the verse is interpreted (ta'wil) to refer to idolatrous slaughter, as indicated by the Prophet's hadith.

Key Terms
مناظرة (munāẓarah): A scholarly dispute aimed at manifesting the truth, distinguished from mere argumentation.
فساد الاعتبار (fasād al-iʿtibār): The invalidity of a qiyas due to its contradiction with a text or consensus that prevents the effective cause.
نقض (naqḍ): Refutation of a claim by showing that the ruling is absent in a case or leads to impossibility, thereby invalidating the evidence.
منع (manʿ): Objection to a specific premise in an argument, either with or without counter-evidence.
معارضة (muʿāraḍah): Presenting a contradictory evidence that opposes the proponent's evidence and ruling.
تأويل (taʾwīl): Interpretation of a text to a meaning other than its apparent sense, based on evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the first type of objection that can be raised against a qiyas, according to this passage?
A) Mu'aradah (contradiction)
B) Naqd (refutation)
C) Fasad al-i'tibar (invalidity of consideration)
D) Man' (objection to a premise)

Key Takeaway
Fasad al-i'tibar is a crucial objection that requires careful analysis of whether the text is truly clear and uncontradicted; if the text can be interpreted or specified, the qiyas may stand.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
12 min
Fasad al-I'tibar: The First Objection to Qiyas
Explains the first type of objection to qiyas with an example of a Hanafi-Shafi'i dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define fasad al-i'tibar, distinguish it from other objections (man', naqd, mu'arada), and apply the Hanafi response to a Shafi'i objection using the example of tasmiyah in slaughter.

Core Idea
Fasad al-i'tibar invalidates qiyas if it contradicts a text or ijma', but Hanafis respond by interpreting or prioritizing the qiyas as a specifier.

In Hanafi usul, objections to qiyas are systematically classified. The first type, fasad al-i'tibar, challenges the validity of the analogy itself by showing it contradicts a clear text or consensus. This lesson unpacks that classification and works through a classic dispute between Hanafis and Shafi'is over the permissibility of meat when the tasmiyah is deliberately omitted. Understanding this objection sharpens your ability to evaluate qiyas arguments and defend them using Hanafi principles.

Guided Path
1
1. Recognize the three types of objections
The passage states that every investigation into a qiyas argument is either prevention (man'), invalidation (naqd), or opposition (mu'arada). Identify what each means and how they differ.
Man' challenges the premise, naqd shows an inconsistency, and mu'arada brings counter-evidence.
2
2. Define fasad al-i'tibar
Fasad al-i'tibar occurs when the qiyas contradicts a text (nass) or consensus (ijma') that prevents the legal cause (ta'lil). This makes the qiyas invalid from the start.
It is the first of five types of questions that can be raised against qiyas.
3
3. Review the possible responses
The passage lists ways to counter a fasad al-i'tibar objection: attacking the chain (sanad), denying the text's indication, interpreting the text (ta'wil), specifying it, preferring the qiyas, or opposing it with a similar text.
These responses allow the Hanafi to preserve the qiyas.
4
4. Walk through the Hanafi-Shafi'i example
Shafi'i: The Quran (6:119) forbids eating without tasmiyah, so qiyas on the forgetful person is invalid. Hanafi: The verse is interpreted to refer to polytheists, and a hadith specifies that the believer's slaughter is valid. Also, qiyas on a specifier (forgetful) takes precedence over the general text.
This demonstrates fasad al-i'tibar and the Hanafi defense.
5
5. Analyze the final nuance on distinction between deliberate and forgetful
The objector may distinguish between deliberate (negligent) and forgetful (excused). The author notes this moves the discussion but is permissible. The Hanafi response is that the cause exists with or without negligence, but the qiyas is still valid.
The passage ends with a reflection that such a distinction may undermine the preponderance of the qiyas.

The response to it is by attacking the chain of transmission, if they are transmitted by ahad, or by denying its indication of the contrary, or by claiming it is subject to interpretation or specified by its evidence, or by preferring it by its method of preference such that it takes precedence over the report, or by opposing it with a similar report, then by opposition they cancel out and qiyas remains operative. Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage from Fawatih al-Rahmut defines the types of objections (i'tirad) against qiyas. It begins with a general classification: every investigation in a qiyas argument is either prevention (man'), invalidation (naqd), or opposition (mu'arada). Then it introduces the five types of questions (as'ila) that can be raised against qiyas. The first type is fasad al-i'tibar (invalidity of consideration), which occurs when the qiyas contradicts a text or ijma' that prevents the legal cause (ta'lil) from being established. The author then details the responses to such an objection: attacking the chain of transmission, denying the text's indication, or interpreting it as being subject to specification or preference. Importantly, the author emphasizes that there is no need to demonstrate actual equivalence (musawat waqi'iyya) when presenting an opposing text, as that would close the door to opposition. The passage includes a classic dispute between the Hanafis and Shafi'is regarding the meat of an animal slaughtered by a Muslim who deliberately omits the tasmiyah. The Shafi'i argues that qiyas on the forgetful person is invalid because the Quranic verse (6:119) forbids eating without mentioning Allah's name. The Hanafi response is that the verse is specific to polytheist slaughter, and there is a hadith that the believer's slaughter is valid whether or not he mentions the name, so long as not deliberate. The Hanafi then argues that the qiyas is on a specifier (the forgetful person) and thus preempts the general text. The discussion concludes with a counter-argument about the distinction between deliberate and forgetful, which the author analyzes, ultimately leaving the matter with a note of caution.

Key Terms
فساد الاعتبار (fasad al-i'tibar): A type of objection where the qiyas contradicts a text or ijma' that prohibits the legal cause (ta'lil), rendering the analogy invalid.
مقدمه (muqaddimah): A premise of the qiyas argument; each premise can be subject to objection (man', naqd, or mu'arada).
نقض (naqd): Invalidation of a premise by showing that the ruling is absent in a case where the cause exists, or by showing an impossibility.
معارضة (mu'arada): Opposition by bringing a conflicting evidence that outweighs the proponent's evidence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi discussion, what is the response if an objector claims that a qiyas is fasad al-i'tibar due to a Quranic verse?
A) The qiyas is automatically invalid
B) The verse must be interpreted (mu'awwal) or specified by a hadith, and the qiyas may still be valid
C) The objector must bring a hadith that is more authentic
D) The qiyas is abandoned in favor of the verse

Key Takeaway
Fasad al-i'tibar is a powerful objection, but Hanafi usul provides multiple tools—ta'wil, takhsis, and tarjih—to defend a qiyas against a conflicting text.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Objection to the Ruling of the Original Case: Is it Permissible?
Expanding on the second type of qiyas objection and critiquing its prohibition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the second type of objection (i'tirad) in qiyas, state the Hanafi and Shafi'i positions on its permissibility, and evaluate the author's critique of the traditional prohibition.

Core Idea
An objection that proves the opposite of the original's ruling directly invalidates the qiyas, making it more decisive than simple prevention.

In qiyas, an objector may attack the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl). But is this permissible? The traditional Hanafi view says no, because it usurps the proponent's role. However, the commentator questions this: if the objector proves the opposite, the evidence collapses. This lesson unpacks the dispute and the author's argument for accepting such objections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of objection
Recognize that this is the second type of i'tirad, which targets the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl) rather than the cause (illa).
2
Understand the traditional prohibition
The objector is not allowed to object without bringing independent proof, because that would make him a proponent (mustadill). This is the standard Hanafi position.
3
Analyze the author's critique
The author argues that proving the opposite of the original's ruling is more effective than mere prevention (man'). Prevention leaves the premise in doubt, while proof of the opposite definitively invalidates the qiyas.
The author uses the phrase 'أولى من منعه' to show preference.
4
Compare with Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi's position
Al-Shirazi (Shafi'i) says the objection should not be heard because it would prolong investigation and require completing evidence with a doubtful premise. Consider how the author responds.
5
Examine the example
The example involves wiping the head: Shafi'i says it is a rukn so it should be repeated like ghusl; the Hanafi objects that the sunna is to complete the washing, which is achieved by repetition only because the place is encompassed. Trace how this illustrates objection to hukm al-asl.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع (الثاني) من الاعتراض: (ما يرد على حكم الأصل، ولا يستدل على خلافه ابتداء، لأنه غصب لمنصب المستدل فيصير هو مستدلاً، ولم يجوزوه) ولم يظهر لي الآن وجه امتناعه، كيف وليس هذا إلا المنع عن إظهار الصواب، فإنه لا شك أن تمام الدليل يتوقف على ثبوت حكم الأصل وقد أبطله المعترض بإثبات منافيه فقد بطل الدليل قطعاً. ثم هذا أولى من منعه، فإن المنع لا تبطل به المقدمة، بل تبقى في دائرة الاحتمال وبهذا النحو من الإبطال يظهر كذبه بحيث لا يرجى الإتمام بوجه ويظهر الحق، فافهم فهو الأحرى بالقبول بل منع حكم الأصل إن قبل المنع وقول أبي إسحق الشيرازي (لا يسمع) المنع على حكم الأصل لأنه حكم شرعي كالمدعي فلا بد له ما لا بد له فيطول البحث (لا يسمع) كيف وعلى هذا يلزم أن يتم الدليل مع الشك في مقدمة منه، وأيضاً يلزم التزام تسليم ما هو كاذب عنده أو مشكوك الصدق، قيل في وجهه أن مدعي المستدل ثبوت حكم الفرع لو ثبت حكم الأصل، فلا يضر حينئذ منع حكم الأصل ونقض المصنف بالعلة فإن مثله يجري فيها أيضاً فلا يصلح منه العلة أيضاً، والحل أن هذا مكابرة فإنه من البين أن المقصود إثبات حكم الفرع في الواقع ولو ظناً لتحصيل حكم شرعي موجب للعمل فافهم إلا أن شرط إجماعنا فيه ولا يجوز القياس على أصل يخالف فيه الخصم، فحينئذ لا يصح المنع قطعاً، لكن هذا الشرط تحكم محض، ومنع عن بعض أنواع الاستدلالات (مثاله) قول الشافعي رضي الله عنه : مسح الرأس (رکن فیسن تكرره كالغسل فيمنع الحنفي سنية تكرر الغسل، بل) إنما السنة (إكماله) أي الغسل في محله (إلا) أنه أي الغسل (لما استوعب المحل فهو أي إكماله إنما يكون (بتكرره)
The second type of objection: (That which is directed against the ruling of the original case, without the objector initially proving its opposite, because that would usurp the position of the proponent, making the objector a proponent, and they did not permit it.) And I do not see now the reason for its prohibition. How is this anything but preventing the manifestation of truth? For there is no doubt that the completeness of the evidence depends on the establishment of the ruling of the original case, and the objector has nullified it by establishing its opposite, so the evidence has been definitively invalidated. Moreover, this is more appropriate than prevention (man'), for prevention does not invalidate the premise, but rather it remains within the realm of probability. By this type of invalidation, its falsity becomes apparent such that there is no hope of completion at all, and the truth appears. So understand, it is more deserving of acceptance. Rather, preventing the ruling of the original case, if prevention is accepted—and the saying of Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi: 'Prevention of the ruling of the original case is not heard because it is a legal ruling like the claim, so it must have what it must have, prolonging the investigation'—(is not heard). How? And on this, the evidence would be completed with doubt in one of its premises, and also it necessitates accepting what is false in his view or dubious in truth. It was said in its reasoning: the proponent claims the establishment of the ruling of the branch, if the ruling of the original is established, so preventing the ruling of the original does not harm. And the author's (al-Muharrar?) invalidation by cause (naqd bil-illa) is similar, for it also applies to it, so it is not valid as a cause either. The solution is that this is obstinacy, for it is clear that the goal is to establish the ruling of the branch in reality, even if hypothetically, to obtain a legal ruling that necessitates action. So understand, except that there is a condition in our consensus: qiyas is not permissible on an original that the opponent disputes. Then at that time, prevention is definitely not valid. But this condition is pure arbitrariness, and it prevents some types of inferences. Its example: the saying of al-Shafi'i: Wiping the head is a pillar (rukn), so it is sunna to repeat it like washing (ghusl). The Hanafi prevents the sunna of repeating washing, rather the sunna is completing it, i.e., washing in its place, except that since washing encompasses the place, its completion is achieved by repetition.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage discusses the second type of objection in qiyas: objecting to the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl). The objector does not bring a counter-evidence initially but simply denies the ruling of the original. The text notes that this is generally disallowed because it usurps the role of the proponent, making the objector a proponent. However, the author (likely the commentator) questions this prohibition, arguing that such an objection is more effective than simple prevention (man') because it definitively nullifies the evidence. The author cites Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi's view that it should not be heard, but the author criticizes this, saying it could lead to incomplete proofs. The passage includes a condition from the Hanafis themselves: qiyas is not allowed on an original that the opponent disputes, which would then make prevention invalid. The author calls this condition arbitrary. An example is given from the dispute between Shafi'i and Hanafi regarding wiping the head: Shafi'i argues that since wiping is a pillar, it should be repeated like washing; the Hanafi rejects the sunna of repeating washing, saying the sunna is to complete the washing, which is achieved by repetition when the place is encompassed.

Key Terms
حكم الأصل (hukm al-asl): The legal ruling that applies to the original case (asl) in a qiyas argument.
منع حكم الأصل (man' hukm al-asl): Prevention of the ruling of the original case, i.e., objecting to it without bringing a counter-evidence.
نقض بالعلة (naqd bil-illa): Invalidation of the cause (illa) by showing it is not present or produces contradiction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the author's main argument against the traditional prohibition of objecting to the ruling of the original case?
A) It is permissible because the proponent cannot object back.
B) It is more effective than prevention because it definitively invalidates the evidence.
C) It is allowed only if the objector is a Hanafi.
D) It is prohibited because it usurps the proponent's role.

Key Takeaway
An objection that proves the opposite of the original's ruling is more decisive than simple prevention, because it directly invalidates the entire qiyas evidence.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Objecting to the Original Ruling in Qiyas: Hanafi Position
Discussion of the permissibility of objecting to the original ruling in qiyas debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that objecting to the original ruling (hukm al-asl) is permissible in qiyas debate and explain the rational justifications for this position.

Core Idea
Objecting to the original ruling is a legitimate and necessary step to ensure the proof's soundness.

In usul al-fiqh debates, a key point of contention is whether an objector may challenge the ruling of the original case in a qiyas argument. Some scholars, like Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi, disallow it, claiming it distracts from the main issue and turns the objector into a proponent. The Hanafi school, as defended by our author, holds that such objections are not only permissible but essential for exposing false premises and reaching the truth. This lesson explores the rational arguments for the Hanafi position and the practical implications in scholarly debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Dispute
The central issue is whether an objector may challenge the hukm al-asl (ruling of the original case) in a qiyas argument. The Hanafi school says yes; some Shafi‘i scholars say no.
This is the second type of objection in the classification of i‘tirad.
2
Understand the Opposing View
Opponents argue that objecting to hukm al-asl distracts from the main issue and forces the objector to become a proponent, leading to lengthy debates. They also claim it is a matter of convention, not reason.
Key figures: Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi and al-Ghazali.
3
Analyze the Hanafi Response
The Hanafi response emphasizes that the proof depends entirely on the soundness of the original ruling. If the original ruling is false, the proof collapses. Objecting with evidence is more effective than mere refusal (man‘) because it definitively exposes falsehood. The objector does not become a proponent; he merely challenges a premise.
The author calls the opposing view 'obstinacy' and 'pure arbitrariness'.
4
Evaluate the Practical Impact
The difference between the schools has real consequences in debate: a Hanafi may object to the original ruling, while a Shafi‘i following al-Shirazi would refuse to hear such an objection. This affects how arguments are constructed and resolved.
Examples from wiping the head, tayammum, and fasting illustrate the method.
