The second type of objection: (what is directed at the ruling of the original case, and one does not initially argue against it because that would usurp the position of the proponent and make the objector a proponent, and they did not permit it). And it is not apparent to me now the reason for its prohibition. How is this anything other than preventing the manifestation of correctness? For there is no doubt that the completion of the proof depends on the establishment of the ruling of the original case, and the objector has nullified it by proving its opposite, so the proof has been definitively nullified. Moreover, this is more appropriate than mere refusal (man'), for refusal does not nullify the premise; rather, it remains within the realm of possibility. And by this type of nullification, its falsehood appears such that there is no hope of completing it in any way, and truth becomes manifest, so understand. It is more deserving of acceptance. Rather, refusal of the ruling of the original case, if it is accepted, and the statement of Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi: 'Objection to the ruling of the original case is not heard because it is a legal ruling like the claim of the claimant, so it must have what it must have, and thus the discussion becomes lengthy.' 'Not heard' — how? And on this, it would follow that the proof is completed while there is doubt about one of its premises, and also it would require acceptance of something that is false in one's view or of doubtful truth. It was said as an explanation: The proponent claims the establishment of the ruling of the branch case on the condition that the ruling of the original case is established, so objecting to the original ruling does not harm. And the author's refutation [of this] using the cause (illa) is similar — the same thing applies to it, so the cause also would not be valid. The solution is that this is obstinacy, for it is clear that the purpose is to establish the ruling of the branch case in reality, even if it is speculative, to obtain a legal ruling that necessitates action, so understand. Except that there is a condition in our consensus: qiyas is not allowed on an original case that the opponent disagrees with. In that case, objection (man') is definitively not valid. But this condition is pure arbitrariness and prevents some types of reasoning. Example: The statement of al-Shafi‘i, may God be pleased with him: Wiping the head is a pillar (rukn), so its repetition is sunna like washing. The Hanafi would prevent the sunna of repetition of washing. Rather, the sunna is its completion, i.e., washing in its place, except that since washing covers the place, its completion is through repetition. So repetition became sunna only because it is a type of completion in the washed limbs, not because it is repetition. Unlike wiping, since it does not cover the head, especially in the proponent's view, its completion is by covering it. Just as the completion of recitation is by completing the sura, not by repeating a verse. And the proponent is not cut off from the research he was engaged in by transitioning to proving the ruling of the original case, according to the correct madhhab, because it is proving a premise of the proof in proving the claim, just as before, and he does not exit from it, like the cause, i.e., just as he does not exit from proving the claim by engaging in proving the cause by agreement. And if they were to stipulate that this constitutes a break (inqita‘), this stipulation would be invalid because it prevents manifesting the truth, which depends on establishing the original case. And the statement of Imam Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali: 'That is a matter of convention, reason has no entry into it, so whoever wishes may set it as he wishes' — this is rejected. Rather, the rules of debate are rational; how can it be? These rules were only set to enable the manifestation of correctness and to inform the seeker of guidance. So they must be set in a way that leads to the objective, so understand. And the objector is not cut off from objecting to the preferred view (al-mukhtar), even if a small group thinks otherwise, merely by his adopting the proof for the ruling of the original case, because it must be sound, so he has the right to object to the proof by refusal (man‘). The strict [scholars] said: 'In this there is remoteness from the objective by engaging in other than it, so it is not allowed.' We say: We do not concede the remoteness such that it falls into something other than the objective. Rather, since the objective is not achieved except through it, it is necessarily the objective, so it is indispensable. And know: This issue can perhaps also be answered by transmission from the observer of the debate (nazir al-manazir) agreeing with the proponent on the ruling of the original case and its correction, so he cannot object. But this is only valid if the proponent is preserving the convention and his aim is to force [his opponent]. If the aim is to establish the actual ruling, then he must prove the original with evidence, so understand. Then sometimes after division (tardid) he may object, and it is called taqsim; so he objects to one of the two branches resulting from the tardid, while that is what is intended, and accepts the other, which is not beneficial, or both. That is, objecting to both branches is valid if each of the two objections has a different angle. But if the angles of objection are identical, then there is no benefit in prolonging by splitting. Example: In the case of a healthy person who lacks water: he found the cause for tayammum, so tayammum is permissible for him, like a traveler who lacks water. The objector said: 'The cause is lack of water absolutely, whether in residence or travel, or lack of water with absence of residence — which do you claim?' The first is rejected, and the second does not benefit you. I say: Its summary, i.e., the summary of the objection, is refusal (man‘) with the presentation of evidence, so the proponent must prove the rejected premise. Thus what was said — that its summary is the objector's claim of a preventing factor (mani‘) present in the branch — is refuted. And its clarification is upon him, because a claim without evidence is not heard, and it suffices for the proponent that the original [lack of preventing factor] is the default. The reason for the refutation is clear, for it is not a claim of a preventing factor, but rather a refusal of causality (illiyya). Then there remains something in the example: the discussion was about objecting to the ruling of the original case, but here the objection goes back to the cause of the original case. Another example: The Shafi‘is say: Fasting the month of Ramadan is a prescribed fast, so specification (ta‘yin) at the intention is obligatory, like making up missed fasts (qada‘), where specification is obligatory. It is said by the Hanafi: If what is meant by the obligation of specification is the obligation after specification by the Lawgiver, then it is non-existent in the original case, for qada‘ is not specified by the Lawgiver. If the obligation is before specification, then it is non-existent in the branch case, and it cannot be proven there, so the condition of qiyas is lost. And sometimes they object to the original case, like the cause and the branch, i.e., just as the cause is objected to, the branch is objected to.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage discusses the second type of objection in qiyas debate: objecting to the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl). The author (Mulla Jiwan or the commentator) argues that such an objection is permissible and even necessary to reach the truth, contrary to scholars like Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi who disallow it. The author provides rational arguments: the proof depends on the original ruling, so if it is false, the proof collapses. Objecting is better than mere refusal (man‘) because it exposes falsehood definitively. He refutes the argument that it prolongs debate by stating that the objective is truth, and if establishing the original ruling is necessary, it becomes the objective itself. He also criticizes al-Ghazali's view that debate rules are purely conventional, asserting they are rational. The passage then gives examples: the Shafi‘i argument that wiping the head requires repetition due to being a pillar, like washing; the Hanafi response distinguishes between washing (which covers the place) and wiping (which does not). Another example concerns tayammum: the cause is lack of water, but whether it is absolute or qualified by travel leads to different rulings. Finally, an example about fasting: the Shafi‘i view that specification of intention is obligatory for Ramadan by analogy with qada‘; the Hanafi responds by analyzing whether the obligation is before or after specification by the Lawgiver, showing the analogy fails. The passage ends by noting that objecting to the original case, cause, and branch are all possible.

Key Terms
حكم الأصل (hukm al-asl): The ruling of the original case in a qiyas argument, which is the basis for the analogy.
الاعتراض (al-i‘tirad): An objection raised against one of the components of a qiyas (original, branch, cause, ruling).
المنع (al-man‘): Refusal or rejection of a premise without providing evidence; a weaker form of objection.
التقسيم (al-taqsim): A method of objection where the objector divides a claim into two or more possibilities and then objects to each separately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is objecting to the ruling of the original case (hukm al-asl) preferable to mere refusal (man‘)?
A) Because it is quicker and avoids lengthy debate.
B) Because it definitively exposes the falsehood of the premise, while refusal leaves it in doubt.
C) Because it is the only way to object according to Abu Ishaq al-Shirazi.
D) Because it forces the proponent to concede defeat immediately.

Key Takeaway
Objecting to the original ruling is a valid and effective method of rebuttal in qiyas debate, as it definitively exposes flawed premises without turning the objector into a proponent.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
The Objector's Right to Challenge the Original Ruling's Proof (Man' al-Asl)
Discussing types of objections against the original ruling in analogical reasoning

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of objections to the original ruling in qiyas and apply the Hanafi position that the objector may deny the evidence of the original without being cut off from the debate.

Core Idea
Objecting to the original ruling's proof is not a departure but a necessary step toward truth.

In Hanafi usul, the process of qiyas involves a proponent (mustadill) who seeks to extend a ruling from an original case (asl) to a new case (far'). The opponent (mu'tarid) has the right to challenge every premise. A key point of contention is whether the objector can deny the evidence for the original ruling itself, or whether that would be an illegitimate departure. The Hanafi school firmly holds that the objector may deny the evidence (man' al-asl), and that this right is rationally necessary for the pursuit of truth. This lesson explores that right, its justification, and its application through examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's basic right
The passage states that the objector is not cut off from objecting even after the deducer brings evidence for the original; he may deny the evidence. Understand that this right stems from the need for the original's proof to be sound.
The deducer remains in the same discussion; proving the original is part of proving the claim.
2
Analyze the rational vs. conventional debate
Distinguish between al-Ghazali's view that debate rules are purely conventional and the Hanafi view that they are rational. Note that Hanafi argues rules must be set to achieve the goal of truth.
The passage rejects al-Ghazali's view as 'mamnu' (rejected).
3
Examine examples of man' al-asl
Work through the example of the healthy person lacking water: the objector splits the cause into absolute or conditional, then denies one part and declares the other useless. See how this challenges the original's ruling without raising a separate preventer.
The objector does not claim a preventer; he denies the causality itself.
4
Apply the principle to the fasting intention dispute
The Shafi'i claim that fasting Ramadan requires specification at intention like make-up fast is countered by the Hanafi objector by showing that the condition of specification is absent either in the original (make-up) or branch (Ramadan), thus breaking the qiyas.
This is man' al-asl by pointing out a missing condition.
5
Handle disputed conditions in man' al-asl
Note the difference between Qadi Abu Zaid (allows denial of disputed conditions) and Fakhr al-Islam (disallows) and the relied-upon view that denial for compulsion (ilzam) is permissible when the deducer does not hold the condition.
The example of wudu vs. tayammum illustrates an original deviated from qiyas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الأصل الرابع/ القياس
فالتكرر إنما صار سنة لأنه نوع من الإكمال في الأعضاء المغسولة، لا لأنه تكرر، (بخلاف المسح فإنه لما لم يستوعب الرأس خصوصاً عند المستدل (فتكميله استيعابه) كما أن تكميل الاشتغال فلا يتم القراءة بإتمام السورة لا بتكرار الآية (ولا ينقطع المستدل عما كان فيه من البحث (للانتقال) إثبات المدعي إلى إثبات حكم الأصل (على) المذهب الصحيح لأنه إثبات مقدمة من الدليل في إثبات المدعي كما كان ولا يخرج عنه ( كالعلة) بحيث توصل إلى المقصود فافهم. ولا ينقطع المعترض) عن الاعتراض على المختار) وإن ظن شرذمة قليلة خلاف ذلك بمجرد اعتبار إقامته الدليل على حكم الأصل (لأنه لا بد من صحته فله (الاعتراض على الدليل (بالمنع) الجواب أيضاً بالنقل عن ناظر المناظر) موافقة للمستدل في حكم الأصل (وتصحيحه) فلا يتمكن من المنع لكن هذا إنما يصح إذا كان المستدل حافظاً للوضع مقصوداً منه الإلزام وإما إذا كان المقصود إثبات الحكم الواقعي إلا إذا أثبت الأصل بالدليل فافهم ثم قد يمنع بعد ترديد ويسمى تقسيماً فيمنع أحدهما) أي أحد الشقين الحاصلين بالترديد (و) الحال أنه هو المراد) ويسلم الآخر الغير النافع (أو كلاهما وذلك أي منع الشقين إذا الممنوعة (فاندفع ما قيل أن حاصله ادعاء المعترض (مانعاً موجوداً في الفرع (وإنما بيانه عليه) لأن الدعوى بلا بينة لا تسمع ويكفي للمستدل أن الأصل (عدمه وجه الدفع ظاهر، فإنه ليس ادعاء المانع، بل منع العلية الحنفي (إن كان المراد بوجوب التعيين (الوجوب بعد تعيين الشرع فمنتف في الأصل) فإن القضاء ليس متعيناً من قبل الشرع ( وإن كان الوجوب (قبله) أي قبل تعيين الشرع فمنتف في الفرع) ولا يمكن إثباته فيه فيفوت شرط القياس (وقد يمنع) الأصل (كالعلة والفرع) أي كما أن العلة تمنع والفرع يمنع (باعتبار إنتفاء شرط مجمع عليه أو مختلف فيه إلا إلزاماً أي من جهة الإلزام فإن المعتبر هنا تسليم المناظر دون الصحة الواقعية هذا عند القاضي الإمام أبي زيد، وشمس الأئمة، وقال الإمام فخر الإسلام لا يجوز منع الشرط المختلف فيه، وصحح صاحب «الكشف» الأول كما : هو الظاهر، ثم الإلزام إنما يتحقق إذا كان المستدل لا يرى الشرط دون الخصم، وأما في العكس فيجوز منع الشرط ولو كان المستدل في صدد الإلزام كما لا يخفى (مثاله) قول الشافعية: الوضوء عبادة، فتجب النية) فيه ( كالتيمم) يجب فيه النية (فيقال: الأصل) فيه (معدول به عن القياس، لأن التراب ملوث) فلا يصلح مطهراً، إلا أن الشارع جعله مطهراً عند إرادة الصلاة فلا يقاس عليه .
The Fourth Principle: Qiyas. Repetition only becomes sunnah because it is a type of completion in the washed limbs, not because it is repetition. (Unlike wiping, since it does not encompass the head, especially according to the deducer, its completion is encompassing it) just as the completion of the occupation; one does not complete the recitation by finishing the surah, not by repeating the verse. (And the deducer is not cut off from the discussion he was in) for (moving) from proving the claim to proving the ruling of the original (according to) the correct madhhab, because it is proving a premise of the evidence in proving the claim as he was, and he does not leave it (like the cause) i.e., as he does not leave proving the claim by occupying himself with proving the cause, by agreement. If they were to agree on cutting off, i.e., that this is a cut from the discussion, (this) agreement would be invalid because it prevents manifesting the truth which depends on proving the original. (And the saying of) Imam Hujjat al-Islam al-Ghazali: 'That is a conventional matter, reason has no entry into it, so whoever wishes may set it as he wishes' (is rejected, rather the laws of debate are rational; how so, these laws were only set to enable the manifestation of correctness and inform the seeker of guidance, so they must be set in a way that leads to the goal, so understand. And the objector is not cut off) from objecting to the chosen (view) even if a small group thinks otherwise, merely by considering his establishing the evidence for the ruling of the original (because it must be sound, so he has the right to object to the evidence (by denial). Those who cut off: (said: 'In it there is departure from the goal by occupying with something else, so it is not permissible.' (We said: 'We do not admit the departure to the extent of falling into something other than the goal; rather, since the goal is not achieved except by it, it is a necessary goal.') So it is necessary. (And know) perhaps this issue can also be answered by transmission from the supervisor of the debate) agreeing with the deducer on the ruling of the original (and its correction) so he cannot deny, but this is only valid if the deducer is preserving the convention and intends to compel; if the intention is to prove the real ruling, then only if he proves the original with evidence, so understand. Then he may deny after hesitating, called 'taqsim', so he denies one of them i.e., one of the two branches resulting from the hesitation (while) it is the intended, and concedes the other which is useless, (or both of them). That is, denying both branches if each of the two denials has a different basis; if the bases of denial are the same, there is no benefit in lengthening by splitting. Example: regarding the healthy person lacking water (he found the cause of tayammum) which is (so tayammum is permissible for him) like the traveller lacking water, it is permissible for him. (So the objector said: 'The cause is loss absolutely, whether in residence or travel, or loss with non-residence; what do you claim? The first is denied, the second does not benefit you.' I say its summary i.e., the summary of the objection is denial with showing a basis. So the deducer must prove the denied premise. (Thus what was said falls away) that its summary is the objector's claim of a preventer existing in the branch; (rather its explanation is upon him) because a claim without evidence is not heard, and it suffices for the deducer that the origin (its non-existence. The manner of rebuttal is apparent, for it is not a claim of preventer, but rather denial of causality. Then something remains in the example, because the discussion was about denial of the original's ruling, and the objection here refers to the original's cause. (Another example): The Shafi'is' saying: Fasting Ramadan is a prescribed fast, so it requires specification at the intention (like make-up fast) requires specification. (So it is said by the Hanafi): 'If by requirement of specification is meant the requirement after specification by the Law, then it is absent in the original, for make-up is not specified by the Law; if the requirement is before specification, then it is absent in the branch and cannot be proven in it, so the condition of qiyas is lost.' (And the original may be denied) like the cause and the branch, i.e., just as the cause can be denied and the branch can be denied with regard to the absence of a condition, whether agreed upon or disputed, except for purposes of compulsion; for what is considered here is the opponent's admission, not actual correctness. This is according to Qadi Imam Abu Zaid and Shams al-A'immah. Imam Fakhr al-Islam said: Denial of a disputed condition is not permissible. The author of 'al-Kashf' corrected the first as is apparent. Then compulsion is only realized when the deducer does not see the condition unlike the opponent; in the opposite case, denial of the condition is permissible even if the deducer is in the process of compulsion, as is not hidden. (Its example): The Shafi'is' saying: Wudu is an act of worship, so intention is required in it (like tayammum) requires intention. (So it is said: The original) in it (is deviated from qiyas, because earth is impure) so it is not suitable as a purifier, except that the Lawgiver made it a purifier when intending prayer, so it is not analogized upon it.
Source pages p. 383

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the rules of debate in qiyas, specifically addressing objections to the original ruling (hukm al-asl). The commentator explains that repetition in washing limbs becomes sunnah because it completes the wash, not because it is repetition, contrasting with wiping where completion is by covering the whole head. The deducer (mustadill) does not abandon his claim when moving to prove the original ruling; he is still engaged in establishing the proof. Al-Ghazali's view that debate rules are purely conventional is rejected; they are rational and must lead to truth. The objector (mu'tarid) is not cut off from objecting even after the deducer brings evidence for the original; he may deny the evidence. The example of the healthy person lacking water shows how an objector can deny the cause (fear of being without water) by splitting it into absolute or with non-residence. Another example from Shafi'i-Hanafi dispute on fasting requires specification of intention; the Hanafi objector shows the condition (specification) fails either in the original or branch, invalidating the qiyas. Finally, the passage discusses denial of the original due to absence of a condition, with different Hanafi opinions on whether disputed conditions can be denied; the relied-upon view is that they can be denied for compulsion purposes. The example of wudu and tayammum illustrates that the original (tayammum) is an exception from qiyas because earth is inherently impure, so analogy to wudu fails.

Key Terms
المستدل (al-mustadill): The one who brings the proof (the proponent) in a qiyas argument.
المعترض (al-mu'tarid): The objector who challenges the proof or reasoning in the debate.
منع الأصل (man' al-asl): Objection by denying the validity or proof of the original ruling in qiyas.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the example of the healthy person lacking water, what is the objector's basis for denying the analogy between the resident and the traveller?
A) The resident does not need tayammum at all.
B) The cause (loss of water) is absolute, so the effect applies to both, but the objector splits it into two possibilities.
C) The resident has water available nearby.
D) The traveller is always permitted tayammum regardless of conditions.

Key Takeaway
The objector in qiyas may deny the proof of the original ruling, and this right is rationally necessary to reach the truth.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

Denial of the Cause's Existence (Man' al-Wujud)
Explains first subtype of objection against the cause: denial of its existence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the method of objecting to qiyas by denying the existence of the claimed cause in the original, and state how the proponent can respond.

Core Idea
Denying the cause's existence refutes qiyas; the proponent must prove existence via sense, reason, or law.

In usul al-fiqh, when a qiyas is presented, one valid objection is to deny that the claimed cause actually exists in the original case. This lesson explains the concept with the example of istinja' and wiping, and the proper response.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
Recognize that the objector claims the cause does not exist in the original. In the example, the Hanafi denies that istinja' is wiping; it is removal of filth.
2
Classify the means of proof
The proponent must prove existence. The passage lists three means: sense, reason, or law. Determine which applies.
3
Apply to the example
Istinja' as 'removal of filth' is proven by law (shar') and reason (the action). Wiping is not its essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
النوع الثالث) من الاعتراض (ما يرد على علة الأصل وذلك وجوه أولها منع وجودها) في الأصل (مثاله قول الشافعي : مسح الرأس (مسح فيسن تثليثه كالاستنجاء فمنع كون الاستنجاء مسحاً بل الاستنجاء إزالة النجاسة ولذا لا يشترط عندنا ،عدد، بل المعتبر فيه التنقية على أكمل الوجوه بأي عدد حصل (وجوابه) أي جواب هذا المنع (بإثبات وجودها فيه بحس) إن كان من الحسيات (أو عقل) إن كان من العقليات (أو شرع) إن كان من الشرعيات (وثانيها) أي ثاني وجوه اعتراضات علة الأصل منع العلية للوصف المذكور وإن وجد في الأصل (مثاله أن يقال في القياس المتقدم : لا نسلم أن تثليث الاستنجاء معلل بأنه مسح) كيف ولا مناسبة بينه وبين ،التكرار بل معلل بكونه إزالة للخبث وهو يناسب التكرار، واختلف في هذا الاعتراض، فقيل : لا يقبل والمختار قبوله، وإلا أي أن لم يقبل بل يكتفي بوجودها مع حكم الأصل (يصح) التعليل بكل طرد (وهو) أي الطرد (لا يفيد الظن ظن العلية، فالتالي باطل. المنكرون للقبول (قالوا : الاقتصار أي اقتصار المعترض على المنع دليل عجزه عن الإبطال (وهو) أي العجز (دليل) (صحته أي الدليل وإذا صح فلا يسمع الاعتراض عليه، بل لا يقبل (قلنا) كون العجز دليل الصحة (ممنوع) فإن رب دليل يكون باطلاً، ولا يقدر على إبطاله (ولو تم هذا الكلام لزم) صحة دليل النقيضين كالحدوث للعالم (والقدم) له (إذا تعارضا وعجز كل من المستدلين عن الإبطال لدليل الآخر (قيل) في تقوية قول القابل (السبر) : ههنا دليل ظاهر على العلية (للناظر والمناظر فيدفع به منعه فلا بد أن يعدل بعد هذا إلى الإبطال فليفعل ابتداء قصراً للمسافة بخلاف سائر (الأدلة إذ ليس فيها دليل ظاهر لإثبات المقدمة (أقول فيه غصب للمنصب من غير ضرورة وقد منعتم من قبل فلا يقدر على الإبطال (إذ لا دليل ههنا على العلية حتى ينقض أو يعارض فلا بد أن يمنع حتى يأتي بمسلكه فيفعل مع ما يفعل) به من وجوه الاعتراض على أن السبر قد لا يسلمه أحدهما كالحنفية) أي كما إذا كان أحد الخصمين حنفياً فلا يتمكن من الاستدلال له به ولا عليه ،به ولك أن تقول : وأيضاً لا على المناظر تعيين الطريق، فله أن يسلك أي طريق شاء قصيراً كان أو طويلاً فافهم (وجوابه) أي جواب هذا النحو من الاعتراض (بإثباتها ) أي بإثبات العلة بمسلك من مسالكها التي مرت فيرد عليه ما يليق به فعلى النص يرد إذا استدل به (الإجمال) أي أنه مجمل لا يصلح حجة من
The third type of objection is what is directed against the cause of the original, and there are several aspects. The first: denial of its existence in the original. Example: The Shafi'i's saying: Wiping the head is wiping, so its thrice repetition is recommendable like Istinja'. So it is denied that Istinja' is wiping; rather Istinja' is removal of impurity, and therefore number is not required according to us, rather what is considered is cleaning in the best manner, by any number achieved. (Its response) i.e., the response to this denial is by proving its existence in it by sense, if from the sensory, or by reason if from the rational, or by law if from the legal. The second: i.e., the second aspect of objections against the cause of the original: denial of causality for the mentioned attribute even if it exists in the original. Example: It is said in the preceding qiyas: We do not accept that the thrice repetition of Istinja' is caused by it being wiping; how so? There is no appropriateness between it and repetition; rather it is caused by it being removal of filth, which is appropriate for repetition. There is disagreement about this objection. Some said: It is not accepted. The chosen view is its acceptance. Otherwise, i.e., if not accepted, but rather content with its existence with the original ruling, then the attribution would be valid by every co-extensive attribute, and co-extensiveness does not produce probability of causality, so the consequent is false. Those who deny acceptance said: Limiting oneself to denial is evidence of inability to invalidate, and inability is evidence of validity, and if it is valid, then objection to it is not heard; rather it is not accepted. (We said: That inability is evidence of validity is denied. For many proofs are invalid, yet one cannot invalidate them. If this statement were complete, it would entail the validity of the proof of two contradictory things, like the world's origination and its eternity, if they conflict and each of the two deducers is unable to invalidate the other's proof. It was said to strengthen the view of those who accept: At-taghsir (division) here is a clear evidence for causality for the observer and the debater, so it repels his denial; so he must after this turn to invalidation; let him do it initially to shorten the distance, unlike other proofs, for there is no clear evidence for proving the premise. (I say: In it there is usurpation of position without necessity, and you have already prevented; so he cannot invalidate, for there is no evidence here for causality so that it can be refuted or contradicted; so he must deny until he brings his path, then he does whatever he does by way of objections. Moreover, at-taghsir may not be accepted by one of them, like the Hanafis, i.e., if one of the two opponents is Hanafi, he cannot argue for it or against it. And you may say: Also, the debater is not required to specify a path; he may take any path he wishes, short or long, so understand. (Its response) i.e., the response to this kind of objection is by proving it, i.e., proving the cause by one of the paths that have passed, then what befits it is replied to; so based on the text, it is replied if he argues by it (the summary) i.e., it is ambiguous, not valid as evidence.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the third type of objection in qiyas: objections against the cause (illa) of the original. The commentator explains two subtypes: (1) Denial of the cause's existence in the original. Example: The Shafi'i analogizes thrice repetition in wiping the head from Istinja', claiming wiping is the cause. The Hanafi objector denies that Istinja' is wiping; it is removal of filth, so number is not required. The response is to prove the cause exists through sense, reason, or law. (2) Denial of the causality of the attribute, even if it exists. Example: The Hanafi objector says the thrice repetition in Istinja' is not caused by wiping but by removal of filth. There is ikhtilaf: some say such denial is not accepted; the chosen view is acceptance. Otherwise, any co-extensive attribute would be considered a cause, but co-extensiveness does not give certainty. Those who reject argue that the objector's mere denial shows inability to invalidate, which implies the proof's validity. The Hanafi response refutes: inability does not prove validity, as false proofs can also be unassailable. The commentator further discusses the argument of 'sabr' (division) as a clear indicator of causality, but notes that it may not be accepted by both parties (e.g., Hanafis). Ultimately, the objector must either prove the cause via established paths or continue denying until a proper counter-argument is made.

Key Terms
منع الوجود (man' al-wujud): Objection by denying that the claimed cause exists in the original case.
منع العلية (man' al-illiyya): Objection by denying that the attribute is the cause, even if it exists in the original.
السبر (al-sabr): A method of dividing attributes to identify the cause, used in debate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi objection to the Shafi'i analogy of thrice repetition, what is the cause that is denied existence?
A) Thriss repetition
B) Wiping
C) Istinja'
D) Removal of filth

Key Takeaway
Denial of the cause's existence (man' al-wujud) is a valid objection in qiyas, requiring the proponent to prove existence via sense, reason, or law.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
10 min
Denial of Causality (Man' al-Illiyya) of the Cause
Explains second subtype of objection against the cause: denial of causality, including the scholarly dispute.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi position on accepting denial of causality as an objection in qiyas and evaluate the counter-arguments.

Core Idea
Denying causality is a legitimate objection because co-extensiveness does not prove causation; otherwise, any constant attribute would be a cause.

Even if the claimed cause exists in the original, the objector may deny that it is the actual cause. This lesson explains the scholarly disagreement over the acceptance of this objection, with the Hanafi school favoring its acceptance.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the objection
The objector accepts the cause exists but denies it is the effective cause. In the example, the Hanafi says thrice repetition in istinja' is not due to wiping but due to removal of filth.
2
Identify the dispute
Some scholars reject this objection, arguing that mere denial shows inability and implies the proof is correct. The chosen view is acceptance.
3
Analyze the Hanafi response
Inability does not prove validity; false proofs can also be irrefutable. Accepting denial preserves the integrity of causal reasoning.
4
Understand the role of sabr
Sabr (division) is proposed as evidence for causality, but it may not be accepted by all (e.g., Hanafis).

النوع الثالث) من الاعتراض (ما يرد على علة الأصل وذلك وجوه أولها منع وجودها) في الأصل (مثاله قول الشافعي : مسح الرأس (مسح فيسن تثليثه كالاستنجاء فمنع كون الاستنجاء مسحاً بل الاستنجاء إزالة النجاسة ولذا لا يشترط عندنا ،عدد، بل المعتبر فيه التنقية على أكمل الوجوه بأي عدد حصل (وجوابه) أي جواب هذا المنع (بإثبات وجودها فيه بحس) إن كان من الحسيات (أو عقل) إن كان من العقليات (أو شرع) إن كان من الشرعيات (وثانيها) أي ثاني وجوه اعتراضات علة الأصل منع العلية للوصف المذكور وإن وجد في الأصل (مثاله أن يقال في القياس المتقدم : لا نسلم أن تثليث الاستنجاء معلل بأنه مسح) كيف ولا مناسبة بينه وبين ،التكرار بل معلل بكونه إزالة للخبث وهو يناسب التكرار، واختلف في هذا الاعتراض، فقيل : لا يقبل والمختار قبوله، وإلا أي أن لم يقبل بل يكتفي بوجودها مع حكم الأصل (يصح) التعليل بكل طرد (وهو) أي الطرد (لا يفيد الظن ظن العلية، فالتالي باطل. المنكرون للقبول (قالوا : الاقتصار أي اقتصار المعترض على المنع دليل عجزه عن الإبطال (وهو) أي العجز (دليل) (صحته أي الدليل وإذا صح فلا يسمع الاعتراض عليه، بل لا يقبل (قلنا) كون العجز دليل الصحة (ممنوع) فإن رب دليل يكون باطلاً، ولا يقدر على إبطاله (ولو تم هذا الكلام لزم) صحة دليل النقيضين كالحدوث للعالم (والقدم) له (إذا تعارضا وعجز كل من المستدلين عن الإبطال لدليل الآخر (قيل) في تقوية قول القابل (السبر) : ههنا دليل ظاهر على العلية (للناظر والمناظر فيدفع به منعه فلا بد أن يعدل بعد هذا إلى الإبطال فليفعل ابتداء قصراً للمسافة بخلاف سائر (الأدلة إذ ليس فيها دليل ظاهر لإثبات المقدمة (أقول فيه غصب للمنصب من غير ضرورة وقد منعتم من قبل فلا يقدر على الإبطال (إذ لا دليل ههنا على العلية حتى ينقض أو يعارض فلا بد أن يمنع حتى يأتي بمسلكه فيفعل مع ما يفعل) به من وجوه الاعتراض على أن السبر قد لا يسلمه أحدهما كالحنفية) أي كما إذا كان أحد الخصمين حنفياً فلا يتمكن من الاستدلال له به ولا عليه ،به ولك أن تقول : وأيضاً لا على المناظر تعيين الطريق، فله أن يسلك أي طريق شاء قصيراً كان أو طويلاً فافهم (وجوابه) أي جواب هذا النحو من الاعتراض (بإثباتها ) أي بإثبات العلة بمسلك من مسالكها التي مرت فيرد عليه ما يليق به فعلى النص يرد إذا استدل به (الإجمال) أي أنه مجمل لا يصلح حجة من
The third type of objection is what is directed against the cause of the original, and there are several aspects. The first: denial of its existence in the original. Example: The Shafi'i's saying: Wiping the head is wiping, so its thrice repetition is recommendable like Istinja'. So it is denied that Istinja' is wiping; rather Istinja' is removal of impurity, and therefore number is not required according to us, rather what is considered is cleaning in the best manner, by any number achieved. (Its response) i.e., the response to this denial is by proving its existence in it by sense, if from the sensory, or by reason if from the rational, or by law if from the legal. The second: i.e., the second aspect of objections against the cause of the original: denial of causality for the mentioned attribute even if it exists in the original. Example: It is said in the preceding qiyas: We do not accept that the thrice repetition of Istinja' is caused by it being wiping; how so? There is no appropriateness between it and repetition; rather it is caused by it being removal of filth, which is appropriate for repetition. There is disagreement about this objection. Some said: It is not accepted. The chosen view is its acceptance. Otherwise, i.e., if not accepted, but rather content with its existence with the original ruling, then the attribution would be valid by every co-extensive attribute, and co-extensiveness does not produce probability of causality, so the consequent is false. Those who deny acceptance said: Limiting oneself to denial is evidence of inability to invalidate, and inability is evidence of validity, and if it is valid, then objection to it is not heard; rather it is not accepted. (We said: That inability is evidence of validity is denied. For many proofs are invalid, yet one cannot invalidate them. If this statement were complete, it would entail the validity of the proof of two contradictory things, like the world's origination and its eternity, if they conflict and each of the two deducers is unable to invalidate the other's proof. It was said to strengthen the view of those who accept: At-taghsir (division) here is a clear evidence for causality for the observer and the debater, so it repels his denial; so he must after this turn to invalidation; let him do it initially to shorten the distance, unlike other proofs, for there is no clear evidence for proving the premise. (I say: In it there is usurpation of position without necessity, and you have already prevented; so he cannot invalidate, for there is no evidence here for causality so that it can be refuted or contradicted; so he must deny until he brings his path, then he does whatever he does by way of objections. Moreover, at-taghsir may not be accepted by one of them, like the Hanafis, i.e., if one of the two opponents is Hanafi, he cannot argue for it or against it. And you may say: Also, the debater is not required to specify a path; he may take any path he wishes, short or long, so understand. (Its response) i.e., the response to this kind of objection is by proving it, i.e., proving the cause by one of the paths that have passed, then what befits it is replied to; so based on the text, it is replied if he argues by it (the summary) i.e., it is ambiguous, not valid as evidence.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on the third type of objection in qiyas: objections against the cause (illa) of the original. The commentator explains two subtypes: (1) Denial of the cause's existence in the original. Example: The Shafi'i analogizes thrice repetition in wiping the head from Istinja', claiming wiping is the cause. The Hanafi objector denies that Istinja' is wiping; it is removal of filth, so number is not required. The response is to prove the cause exists through sense, reason, or law. (2) Denial of the causality of the attribute, even if it exists. Example: The Hanafi objector says the thrice repetition in Istinja' is not caused by wiping but by removal of filth. There is ikhtilaf: some say such denial is not accepted; the chosen view is acceptance. Otherwise, any co-extensive attribute would be considered a cause, but co-extensiveness does not give certainty. Those who reject argue that the objector's mere denial shows inability to invalidate, which implies the proof's validity. The Hanafi response refutes: inability does not prove validity, as false proofs can also be unassailable. The commentator further discusses the argument of 'sabr' (division) as a clear indicator of causality, but notes that it may not be accepted by both parties (e.g., Hanafis). Ultimately, the objector must either prove the cause via established paths or continue denying until a proper counter-argument is made.

Key Terms
منع الوجود (man' al-wujud): Objection by denying that the claimed cause exists in the original case.
منع العلية (man' al-illiyya): Objection by denying that the attribute is the cause, even if it exists in the original.
السبر (al-sabr): A method of dividing attributes to identify the cause, used in debate.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the Hanafi school accept denial of causality (man' al-illiyya) as an objection?
A) Because the objector must always be allowed to deny.
B) Because co-extensiveness does not prove causation.
C) Because the proponent cannot respond.
D) Because it is a textual command.

Key Takeaway
Denial of causality (man' al-illiyya) is accepted in Hanafi usul to ensure that only well-established causes are used in qiyas.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

The Objection of Denying Causality (Man' al-'Illiyya) in Qiyas
Explains the third type of objection regarding the 'illa in qiyas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the two subtypes of objections against the 'illa in qiyas and evaluate the Hanafi position on accepting the denial of causality.

Core Idea
Co-extensiveness (tard) does not prove causality; the objector may deny the 'illa even if it exists in the asl.

This lesson focuses on a key rule in Hanafi usul: the objector in qiyas can challenge the 'illa itself, not just its existence. We examine the internal debate about whether such objections should be accepted and how to respond. Understanding this refines your ability to construct and critique analogical arguments in Islamic legal reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection type
The passage distinguishes two subtypes: denial of existence (man' al-wujud) and denial of causality (man' al-'illiyya). Learn to recognize each in a qiyas debate.
Example: In the istinja' analogy, Hanafis first deny that istinja' is a wipe (existence), then deny that triple repetition is caused by it being a wipe (causality).
2
Understand the internal dispute
Some scholars reject accepting the denial of causality, arguing that if the objector cannot refute the proof, the objection should not be heard. The chosen Hanafi view accepts it.
The key evidence: tard does not yield probability (zann) of causality, so the objector has the right to demand a proper proof.
3
Evaluate the arguments
Analyze the opponent's claim that inability to refute proves validity. The Hanafi response: many false proofs cannot be refuted, so inability is not proof. This is illustrated by the reductio ad absurdum of two contradictory proofs.
Follow the logical steps: if inability proved validity, then two contradictory arguments would both be valid when their proponents cannot refute each other.
4
Recognize the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
In a debate, if the opponent denies causality, the proponent must prove the 'illa via a recognized method (e.g., texts, ijma', dawaran), not rely on bare existence.
This forces the proponent to bring stronger evidence, ensuring the robustness of qiyas.
5
Apply the method of response
The proponent can respond to an objection by establishing the 'illa through sensory, rational, or scriptural proof, or by using the recognized masalik al-illa. If unable, he may transition to another universally accepted proof.
The example of Prophet Ibrahim's debate with Nimrod illustrates a transition to a clearer proof when the initial one was contested.

دليل آخر (وذلك) أي جواز الانتقال لأن العمدة في المناظرة (محافظة المقصود بالذات) وههنا المقصود إثبات الحكم بالعلة المدعاة فما دام في سعي العلة ليس خارجاً عن المقصود بلا بأس به، وفيه دغدغة، فإن ههنا مناظرتين إحداهما : أصل المناظرة لإثبات الحكم بالعلة، وثانيتهما لإثبات العلة وإن كان هذا بدليل فبالانتقال من دليل العلة إلى آخر لحقه الدبرة في هذه المناظرة، وهذه مناظرة أخرى فهذا الانتقال كانتقال من حجة إلى حجة أخرى لإثبات أصل المقصود فافهم.
Third type of objection: that which is leveled against the 'illa of the asl. It has aspects: first, denial of its existence in the asl. Example: Al-Shafi'i says: wiping the head is a wipe, so its triple repetition is recommended like istinja'. [Response]: Deny that istinja' is a wipe; rather istinja' is removal of impurity, hence number is not required according to us. Its response: proving its existence by sensory evidence if it is sensory, or by reason if it is rational, or by revelation if it is scriptural. Second aspect: denial of causality for the mentioned attribute even if it exists in the asl. Example: in the aforementioned qiyas: we do not accept that triple repetition in istinja' is caused by it being a wipe, for there is no relation between that and repetition; rather it is caused by removal of impurity which suits repetition. There is disagreement about this objection: it is said it is not accepted; the chosen view is its acceptance. Otherwise, if not accepted and one is satisfied with its existence with the ruling of the asl, then the attribution by any co-extensive property would be valid, and co-extensiveness does not produce probability of causality, so the conclusion is false. Those who deny acceptance say: the objector's limitation to denial is evidence of his inability to refute, and inability is evidence of the proof's validity. If so, the objection is not heard. We say: that inability is evidence of validity is denied, for many proofs are false and one cannot refute them. If this argument were valid, it would entail the validity of two contradictory proofs, like the world being both originated and eternal, when two arguers are unable to refute each other's proofs. It is said in support of the accepter: sabr (elimination) provides apparent evidence of causality for the debater, so the denial is repelled, and he must then resort to refutation; he should do so from the beginning to shorten the process, unlike other proofs where there is no apparent evidence to establish the premise. I say: this is overstepping without necessity, and you have already denied before and cannot refute. So he must deny until he brings his own method. The response to this type of objection is to establish the 'illa via one of its recognized methods. So when the proof is a text, the objection is that it is mujmal (ambiguous) without elaboration, or mu'awwal (interpreted) not literal, or mu'arada (contradictory) by another text, etc. When the proof is ijma', the objection is denial of its existence if it is ahad, or that it is sukti and so not proof. When the proof is dawaran (co-extensiveness and co-exclusiveness) or similar, the objection is denial of its validity. The proponent must prove it if possible; if not, he may transition to a universally accepted method, as in the debate of al-Khalil (Abraham) with Nimrod: 'My Lord is He who gives life and causes death.' Nimrod said: 'I give life and cause death' ... etc. This shows the permissibility of transition, because the objective is to maintain the intended point. There is a nuance: this involves two debates, but in the end, the transition is from one proof to another to establish the main objective. Understand.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage discusses the third category of objections in qiyas: those directed against the 'illa (cause) of the asl (original case). The author, expanding on the matn of Musallam al-Thubut, explains two subtypes: first, denying the existence of the 'illa in the asl; second, denying that the attribute cited is truly the cause (even if it exists). The example from the Shafi'i argument about triple wiping in istinja' illustrates both: the Hanafi respondent first denies that istinja' is a wipe (denying existence), then denies that triple repetition is caused by it being a wipe (denying causality). The author notes an internal Hanafi dispute about whether such objections should be accepted; the chosen view is acceptance, supported by reasoning that mere co-extensiveness does not prove causality. The passage then outlines how to respond to such objections: by proving the 'illa through sensory, rational, or textual means, or by using the established methods (masalik al-illa) like texts, ijma', dawaran, etc. Finally, the author points out that if the proponent cannot prove the 'illa, he may transition to another universally accepted proof, citing the example of Prophet Ibrahim debating Nimrod.

Key Terms
علة ('illa): The effective cause in qiyas that links the original case to the new case.
أصل (asl): The original case whose ruling is known and used as a basis for analogy.
منع (man'): An objection denying the existence or causality of the 'illa.
طرد (tard): Co-extensiveness of the attribute with the ruling, not sufficient to prove causality.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the chosen Hanafi view regarding accepting the objection of denying causality (man' al-'illiyya) in qiyas?
A) It is not accepted; the objector must refute the proof directly.
B) It is accepted because co-extensiveness does not prove causality.
C) It is accepted only if the objector provides an alternative 'illa.
D) It is not accepted; the objector's inability to refute proves the proof's validity.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school accepts the objection of denying causality in qiyas, forcing the proponent to prove the 'illa through a recognized method, thereby ensuring rigorous analogical reasoning.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate

Transition Between Arguments in Qiyas
Discusses the permissibility and forms of transition in argumentation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four forms of intiqal in qiyas argumentation and explain why transitions are legitimate, not blameworthy.

Core Idea
Transition in qiyas is a legitimate method to complete a proof, not a sign of weakness.

In usul al-fiqh, the process of qiyas often involves a back-and-forth between proponents and opponents. The Hanafi tradition recognizes that a debater may need to shift from one line of proof to another—a transition (intiqal). This lesson explores the four forms of intiqal as presented in Fawatih al-Rahmut, emphasizing that such transitions are not only permissible but sometimes necessary to strengthen a proof.

Guided Path
1
1. Recognize the context of intiqal
Intiqal occurs when a proponent needs to shift from one 'illa (effective cause) to another, or from one ruling to another, in order to complete the proof. The author distinguishes between blameworthy and permissible transitions.
The text focuses on permissible transitions.
2
2. Learn the first form: transition between 'illas
When the opponent denies (mumana'a) the attribute of the 'illa, the proponent moves to another 'illa to prove the first. This is an indirect way of establishing the original 'illa.
Al-Kashshaf is cited as the source for this form.
3
3. Analyze the second form: transition to a different 'illa for a needed ruling
Here, the proponent uses a different 'illa to prove a ruling that is a premise of the original argument. The kitaba example illustrates this: the opponent claims that deficiency in servitude prevents kaffara, so the proponent proves that kitaba does not cause deficiency by comparing it to a sale.
This is essentially proving a premise.
4
4. Understand the third form: transition to another ruling with the same 'illa
The proponent uses the original 'illa to prove a different ruling that the first ruling depends on. The text states this is also not blameworthy.
The fourth form is not present in the available text.
5
5. Summarize the principle
All three forms are permissible because they serve to complete the proof. The proponent is not changing the main argument but rather providing necessary supporting premises.
Key: intiqal is not a sign of defeat but a strategic move.

ثم للانتقال صور أربع : الأولى : الانتقال من علة إلى علة أخرى لإثبات العلة الأولى. قال في الكشف : هذا الانتقال إنما يكون في الممانعة فإن الخصم إذا منع عليه وصف المجيب لم يجد بدأ من إثباته بدليل آخر . الثانية : الانتقال من العلة الأولى إلى علة أخرى لإثبات حكم آخر يحتاج إليه الحكم الأوّل، وهذا أيضاً ليس انتقالاً مذموماً، لأنه إثبات لما يتوقف عليه، فهو بالحقيقة إثبات لمقدمة الدليل مثاله قولنا : الكتابة عقد يحتمل الفسخ بالإقالة فلا يمنع التكفير كالبيع بشرط الخيار فاعترض الخصم أن غاية ما لزم عدم مانعية الكتابة وإني أقول به، بل المانع النقصان في الرق فنقول : الرق لم ينقص به، لأن الكتابة عقد معاوضة، فلا نقصاناً، يوجب كالبيع ، فههنا وإن انتقل إلى إثبات عدم النقصان بعلة أخرى لكن : لكونه مقدمة من أصل الدليل فيتمم الدليل الأول، هكذا الكتابة عقد لا يوجب نقصاً في الرق ويقبل الفسخ، فلم يتحقق المنع فيجزي . الثالثة : الانتقال إلى حكم آخر يحتاج إليه الأوّل بالعلة الأولى وهذا أيضاً ليس انتقالاً
Then there are four forms of transition. First: transition from one 'illa to another 'illa to establish the first 'illa. Al-Kashshaf says: this transition occurs in the case of opposition; when the opponent denies the attribute, the respondent must prove it by another proof. Second: transition from the first 'illa to another 'illa to establish another ruling needed for the first ruling. This is also not blameworthy transition, because it is establishing what it depends on; essentially it is establishing a premise of the proof. Example: we say: kitaba (contract of manumission) is a contract that can be rescinded by iqala, so it does not prevent kaffara, like a sale with the option of repudiation. The opponent objects that at most it negates the preventive nature of kitaba, but the real preventer is deficiency in servitude. We say: servitude is not diminished by it, because kitaba is a contract of exchange, so it does not cause deficiency, like a sale. So here, although we transitioned to proving no deficiency by another 'illa, it is because it is a premise of the original proof, thus completing the first proof. So kitaba is a contract that does not cause deficiency in servitude and accepts rescission, so the objection is not established, and it suffices. Third: transition to another ruling needed for the first ruling using the same 'illa. This is also not transition...
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage presents the topic of transition (intiqal) in argumentation within qiyas. It acknowledges that a debater may need to shift from one line of proof to another, and this is not necessarily blameworthy. The author lists four forms (only three are fully described in the available text). The first form is when the opponent denies the attribute of the 'illa, so the proponent moves to another 'illa to establish the first one. The second form is moving to a different 'illa to prove a ruling that is needed for the original ruling; this is essentially proving a premise. The example given is about kitaba (contract of manumission) where the opponent claims deficiency in servitude as a preventer, and the proponent moves to show that kitaba does not cause deficiency. The third form is moving to a different ruling needed for the first ruling while using the same 'illa. The author emphasizes that these transitions are not blameworthy because they serve to complete the proof. The fourth form is not present in the text.

Key Terms
انتقال (intiqal): Transition from one argument or 'illa to another in debate to strengthen the proof.
ممانعة (mumana'a): Opposition; when the opponent denies the attribute or 'illa.
كتابة (kitaba): A contract between a master and a slave for manumission in exchange for payment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following best describes the second form of intiqal?
A) Moving from one 'illa to another to prove the same ruling directly.
B) Moving from the first 'illa to another 'illa to prove a ruling that is a premise of the first ruling.
C) Moving to a different ruling using the same 'illa.
D) Abandoning the original argument and starting a new one.

Key Takeaway
Intiqal is a permissible and often necessary tool in qiyas argumentation, provided it serves to complete the proof.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

The Fourth Type of Intiqal: Transition from One 'Illa to Another
Explains the fourth type of intiqal and its permissibility debate

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi position on intiqal (transition from one 'illa to another) in munazara and identify the key proofs and rejoinders in the debate.

Core Idea
Transitioning to a new proof in debate shows defeat; the Hanafi school forbids it.

In Islamic legal theory, the science of munazara (debate) has strict rules to ensure intellectual rigor. One debated issue is whether a debater may abandon one 'illa (effective cause) and adopt another to prove the same claim. The Hanafi school, following the majority, holds that this is impermissible because it indicates failure to uphold the initial commitment. This lesson unpacks the reasoning and examines the famous counterexample from the story of Prophet Ibrahim (AS) and Nimrod.

Guided Path
1
Identify the contested ruling
Determine what exactly is being disputed: the permissibility of transitioning from one 'illa to another to prove the original claim in the same debate.
2
State the majority/Hanafi position
The majority, including the Hanafi Imam Fakhr al-Islam, rule that such transition is impermissible. The debater's inability to fulfill his commitment results in defeat (dabara) and the debate ends.
This is the mufta bihi position.
3
Examine the opposing proof
A small group allows transition, citing the story of Prophet Ibrahim (AS) who moved from the argument of giving life and death to the argument of bringing the sun from the east when Nimrod obstinately rejected the first.
This is a Quranic narrative (Surah al-Baqarah).
4
Analyze the Hanafi rejoinders
The commentator provides multiple answers: (1) the first response was sufficient; (2) the transition was not from an incomplete proof; (3) Ibrahim only committed to proving Lordship by any proof; (4) the story concerns signs of Lordship, not impelling 'illa.
The fourth rejoinder is criticized as weak.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah al-khilaf)
If transition occurs, the debate is rendered invalid according to the majority; the opposing view would consider it valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة: الانتقال من علة إلى علة أخرى لإثبات أصل المدعي ومنه ما ذكر المصنف من مسلك إلى آخر فإنه علة لإثبات المقصود من المناظرة الثانية، فالانتقال منه إلى آخر من علة إلى علة أخرى، فذهب الجمهور إلى عدم جوازه، ومنهم الإمام فخر الإسلام من قدس سره وخالف شرذمة قليلة وذهبوا إلى الجواز لنا : إنه عجز عن الوفاء بما التزم في المناظرة من إثبات المقصود بالعلة المدعاة فلحق الدبرة وتمت المناظرة، ولو تم ما انتقل إليه صار مناظرة أخرى كيف ولو جوز هذا لم تتم مناظرة أبداً. واستدلوا بقصة الخليل التي مرت، وجوه من الأجوبة الأوّل ما مر وهو جواب تام الثاني: أن الممنوع من الانتقال ما كان عند عدم ظهور تمامية الأوّل إلى لم يجب عما قال استخفافاً لكلامه وتنبيهاً على سخافته، ثم أقام حجة أخرى فبهت، وهذا لا يخلو من نوع قلق، بل الأولى أن يقال : إن مقصود الخليل من الاستدلال بالإحياء والإماتة الاستدلال بالقدرة الكاملة العامة، لكن ادّعاها في مثال الإحياء والإماتة، ولما كابر قرر ذلك الدليل في مثال آخر فليس فيه انتقال أصلاً فافهم الثالث: أن الخليل لم يكن التزم إلا إثبات ربوبية الله تعالى بدليل ما أياً كان وقد وفى والممنوع من الانتقال مما التزم إثباته به، وفيه نوع الخفاء فإن إيقاع المناظرة بنحو التزام بإتمامه إثبات ما ادّعى بما استدل، فالانتقال منه انتقال من مما التزم. الرابع: أنه من معرفات ربوبية الله تعالى ولا بأس بالانتقال فيه، وإنما الكلام في العلة الباعثة وهذا ليس بشيء، فإن الخروج عما ناظر فيه قبيح على كل حال، ثم ههنا انتقالات أخر كالانتقال من علة إلى أخرى لإثبات حكم آخر غير محتاج إليه أو من ذلك إلى حكم آخر كذلك غير محتاج إليه، ولعل هذا الانتقال إن كان بعد ظهور فساد الدليل الأصل فقد لحقه الدبرة في أصل المناظرة، وهذه مناظرة لمطلوب آخر إن كان بعد تمام أصل الدليل فقد لحق الدبرة المعترض وهذا شروع في مناظرة أخرى فليس هذه الأقسام مما نحن فيه وإن عد هذا الكلام من تتمة المناظرة الأولى فهو حشو هكذا ينبغي أن يفهم المقام
The fourth: Transition from one 'illa to another in order to prove the original claim. Included in this is what the author mentioned from one path to another, for it is an 'illa to prove the objective of the second debate. So the transition from it to another is from one 'illa to another. The majority, including Imam Fakhr al-Islam (may his secret be sanctified), held it impermissible. A small band opposed and allowed it. For us: He is unable to fulfill what he undertook in the debate of proving the objective by the claimed 'illa, so defeat overtakes him and the debate ends. If what he transitions to were completed, it would become another debate. How so? If this were allowed, a debate would never be completed. They adduced the story of Khalil (Abraham) mentioned earlier. Several answers: first, it has passed, and it is a complete answer. Second: The prohibited transition is when the first [proof] was not fully apparent, but here it is not so. For the inference by giving life and taking life was complete in reality. What Nimrod the accursed mentioned of giving life to himself and taking life were meant as release from prison and avoiding killing, and killing—this is far from what Khalil intended. He could have said: I meant real giving of life and real taking of life. If so, then revive this dead one and kill this released one without direct causation as Allah does, and the argument would be complete. But he did not respond to what he said out of disregard for his speech and to point out its absurdity. Then he presented another argument and silenced him. This is not without some unease. Rather, it is better to say: The purpose of Khalil in inferring by giving life and taking life was to infer the complete universal power, but he claimed it in the example of giving life and taking life. When he obstinately persisted, he established that proof in another example, so there is no transition at all, so understand. Third: Khalil had only undertaken to prove the Lordship of Allah by any proof, and he fulfilled it. The prohibited transition is from what he undertook to prove it by. There is some obscurity here, for conducting the debate in a way that undertakes to complete the proof of what he claimed by the proof he used—so transitioning from it is a transition from what he undertook. Fourth: It is among the indicators of Allah's Lordship, and there is no harm in transitioning therein. The discussion is about the impelling cause, and this is nothing, for departing from what he debated is ugly in any case. There are other transitions here, such as transition from one 'illa to another to prove another judgment not needed, or from that to another judgment also not needed. Perhaps this transition, if after the corruption of the original proof becomes apparent, then defeat has overtaken him in the original debate, and this is a debate for another objective. If after the completion of the original proof, then the objector is overtaken by defeat, and this is starting another debate. So these types are not of our concern, and if this speech is counted as a completion of the first debate, it is redundant. Thus the station should be understood.
Source pages p. 386

Commentary
This passage concerns the fourth type of transition (intiqal) in argumentation: moving from one effective cause ('illa) to another to prove the original claim. The author, in commenting on Musallam al-Thubut, explains that this is what happens when a debater switches from one line of reasoning to a different one for the same conclusion. The majority of usul scholars (including the Hanafi Imam Fakhr al-Islam) forbid this because it indicates the debater's inability to fulfill his commitment, thus concluding the debate in his defeat. A small group allowed it, citing the famous debate between Prophet Ibrahim (AS) and Nimrod. The commentator then offers several rejoinders to this objection: first, the story is interpreted differently; second, the transition was not from an incomplete proof; third, Ibrahim had only committed to proving Lordship by any proof, not a specific one; and fourth, the story is about signs (ma'rifat) not legal 'illa, but this is dismissed. The passage concludes by distinguishing this type from other irrelevant transitions.

Key Terms
انتقال (intiqal): الانتقال من علة إلى علة أخرى لإثبات أصل المدعي
علة (illa): الوصف الذي يكون باعثاً على الحكم ومؤثراً فيه
مناظرة (munazara): الجدل العلمي لإثبات حكم أو دليل
دبرة (dabara): انكسار المحتج وعجزه عن إتمام الحجة

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) المنع
C) التفصيل بين ما إذا كان الدليل الأول تاماً أم لا

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning

The Four Objections of 'Adam al-Ta'thir and Their Reduction
Classifies and reduces four types of 'adam al-ta'thir objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the four types of 'adam al-ta'thir objections and analyze how each reduces to either man' al-'illiyya or mu'arada.

Core Idea
The four objections collapse into two: denial of causality or counterargument.

In qiyas (analogical deduction), the disputant might attack the effective cause (al-'illa) of the original case. The jadal scholars (mujādilūn) classified such attacks into four types of 'adam al-ta'thir (non-effect). The Hanafis, however, did not treat them as separate categories because, as this passage shows, they ultimately reduce to either man' al-'illiyya (denial of causality) or mu'arada (counterargument). Understanding this classification sharpens your ability to structure objections in usul debate.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Types
List and describe each type: (1) lack of effect absolutely in the description, (2) lack of effect specifically in the original case, (3) lack of effect of the restricted description and removal of its restriction, (4) lack of effect of the restricted in the disputed branch.
Memorize the sequence: waṣf, aṣl, ḥukm, far'.
2
Study the Fiqh Examples
Work through each example provided. Type 1: a Hanafi example about dawn prayer adhan. Type 2: a Shafi'i example about sale of absent item. Type 3: a Hanafi example about apostates destroying property. Type 4: a Shafi'i example about a woman marrying without a suitable match.
The examples illustrate how the objection is raised in real legal debates.
3
Analyze the Reduction Scheme
The text states: types 1 and 3 reduce to man' al-'illiyya (the opponent denies the causality of the claimed description). Types 2 and 4 reduce to mu'arada (the opponent presents a different description as the real 'illa).
Note: type 4's reduction is disputed—some say it reduces to type 3.
4
Evaluate the Controversy on Type 4
The passage notes a view from al-Tahrīr that type 4 reduces to type 3 because removing the restriction is the same as the third objection. However, the author suggests it could also be mu'arada if the absolute description is proposed as an alternative 'illa.
Understand why this nuance matters: it changes how you frame your objection in debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

الخ
The fourth original: Qiyas. The aspects of objection to the 'illa of the original. The Hanafis did not mention them because they are not independent, as they reduce to denial or counterargument: the lack of effect and consideration of the description in it. The disputational scholars divided them into four categories. First: that its lack of effect appears in the ruling absolutely. Second: lack of effect in that particular original. Third: lack of effect of the restricted and removal of a restriction from it and claiming the absolute upon it absolutely. Fourth: lack of effect of this restricted in the disputed matter and removal of the restriction in the place of dispute. Example of the first (called lack of effect in the description): as if it were said to the Hanafis: 'The dawn prayer is not shortened, so its adhan is not brought forward to the time like Maghrib.' It is responded that the lack of shortening is incidental, as there is no suitability for bringing forward the adhan, so it is not considered at all unanimously. The second (lack of effect in the original): as if it were said to the Shafi'is: 'In the sale of an absent item, the commodity is not visible, so its sale is invalid like the bird in the air whose sale is invalid.' It is responded that the inability to deliver is independent in its effect in the original, which is the bird in the air, so being invisible has no role. Third (lack of effect of the restriction, called lack of effect in the ruling): as if the Hanafis said concerning apostates: 'They are polytheists who destroyed property in the abode of war, so they are not liable if they become Muslims, like other polytheists who are not liable for what they destroyed after Islam.' It is responded that the abode of war has no effect in negating liability, because the negation of liability according to you (Hanafis) is absolute regarding apostates who destroyed and then became Muslims. Fourth (lack of consideration by removing the restriction in the place of dispute, called lack of effect in the branch): as if it were said to the Shafi'is: 'She married herself without a suitable match, so her marriage is rejected like the marriage of the guardian of a minor girl without a suitable match, which is not executed.' It is responded that not having a suitable match has no effect according to you (Shafi'is) because the dispute between us and you is absolute concerning the validity of marriage by women's expressions, whether with a suitable match or not. According to you it is invalid, according to us it is valid. They said: The first and the third reduce to denial of causality regarding what the arguer claimed as 'illa, for he claimed causality of this restricted and his causality is denied—either denial of causality absolutely or of the restricted by removing the restriction. The second and the fourth reduce to counterargument in the original, for in them is presenting another 'illa, which is the absolute. In al-Tahrir: No, rather the fourth reduces to the third—the reduction is apparent, for when the restriction is removed, the causality of the restricted is denied as in the third. But it can possibly be a counterargument: if the restriction is removed in this ruling, the absolute remains as 'illa, so there is presenting another 'illa as well. However, this is not the objector's aim, for apparently his purpose is to object to the opponent's 'illa, not to prove another 'illa. Hence the author said: 'and it is more similar.' And an objection was raised to what they said: 'There is a difference between denying causality to indicate it and establishing evidence of its non-existence. Here, claiming lack of effect is nullifying it, not denial.' Similarly, there is a difference between presenting what could be the 'illa and presenting what is definitely the 'illa. The counterargument is the first; in the second, the nullification of the 'illa is necessary. I say: Perhaps the mentioned reduction is to avoid usurpation of the arguer's position in the very form of the debate. Otherwise, if not for this reason, every premise can be denied—even metaphorically—and it is possible to indicate its invalidity from the start. So here too it is possible, and we do not deny it, but we return to avoid necessary usurpation. So understand. And in it is apparent consideration, because his saying: every premise can be denied, etc.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage discusses the objections (i'tiradat) leveled against the 'illa (effective cause) in the original case of qiyas. The Hanafis did not include these as separate categories because they ultimately reduce to either denial of causality (man') or counterargument (mu'arada). The disputational scholars (jadaliyyun) divided them into four: (1) lack of effect absolutely (in the description), (2) lack of effect specifically in the original, (3) lack of effect of the restricted and removal of the restriction claiming the absolute, (4) lack of effect of the restricted in the disputed branch and removal of the restriction in the place of dispute. Each is illustrated with a fiqh example involving Hanafi or Shafi'i positions. The text then analyzes whether these four reduce to denial of causality or counterargument, noting a view (from al-Tahrir) that the fourth reduces to the third, but it could also be a counterargument. The author (Ibn Abd al-Shakur?) adds a final remark about the purpose of this reduction being to avoid usurping the arguer's role, and that every premise can be denied even metaphorically, but we avoid that to maintain proper debate etiquette.

Key Terms
عدم التأثير ('adam al-ta'thir): عدم ظهور أثر الوصف المدعى كعلة في الحكم
قيد (qayd): وصف يضاف إلى المطلق يخصصه
مطلق (mutlaq): الوصف غير المقيد
منع العلية (man' al-'illiyya): إنكار كون الوصف علة للحكم
معارضة (mu'arada): إبداء علة أخرى تنافس العلة المدعاة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which two of the four types of 'adam al-ta'thir reduce to man' al-'illiyya?
A) The first and the second
B) The first and the third
C) The second and the fourth
D) The third and the fourth

Key Takeaway
The four 'adam al-ta'thir objections to the 'illa reduce to two fundamental categories: denial of causality (man') and counterargument (mu'arada).

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
advanced
12 min
The Four Types of 'Adam al-Ta'thir in Qiyas Objections
Classification and analysis of four types of 'adam al-ta'thir objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the four types of 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect) objections, and classify each as reverting to man' (rejection) or mu'arada (opposition).

Core Idea
Four objections based on lack of effect: absolute, specific, qualified, and branch-specific. They revert to rejection or opposition.

In this passage, the author presents a classification of objections to the effective cause ('illa) based on the claim that the attribute has no effect ('adam al-ta'thir). This is a crucial concept in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, as it refines the methodology of qiyas. The four types are defined with examples, and their relationship to the two main categories of objection—rejection (man') and opposition (mu'arada)—is analyzed. Understanding this classification sharpens your ability to both construct and critique analogical arguments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Four Types and Their Names
Read the passage and list the four types: 1) Absolute lack of effect in the ruling (العدم التأثير مطلقاً) called lack of effect in the attribute, 2) Lack of effect in that original case (عدم التأثير في ذلك الأصل), 3) Lack of effect of the qualification (عدم تأثير القيد) called lack of effect in the ruling, 4) Lack of effect by eliminating the qualification in the branch (عدم الاعتبار بإلغاء القيد في محل النزاع) called lack of effect in the branch.
2
Analyze Each Example
For each type, the passage gives an example from Hanafi or Shafi'i argumentation. Examine how the attribute is claimed to have no effect. For example, the first type: the attribute 'not shortened' (Fajr) is said to have no effect on advancing the adhan. Understand why it is considered incidental (tardi).
3
Determine the Reversion: Man' or Mu'arada
The passage states that types 1 and 3 revert to rejection (man') of the 'illiyyah, while types 2 and 4 revert to opposition (mu'arada). Note the reasoning: the first and third involve denying the cause's effectiveness; the second and fourth involve presenting an alternative cause (the absolute attribute).
4
Evaluate the Dispute on the Fourth Type
The author notes a disagreement: some (like the author of al-Tahrir) argue that the fourth type actually reverts to the third (man'), not mu'arada. Read the objection and the author's response about the objector's intention. Weigh the arguments.
5
Apply the Classification
Given a new qiyas argument, try to identify if an objection based on 'adam al-ta'thir could be raised, and if so, which type it falls into and whether it is man' or mu'arada. This builds skill in critiquing analogies.
