The fourth principle: Qiyas: Types of objection to the 'illa of the original case. The Hanafis did not mention it due to its lack of independence because it reverts to rejection or opposition: lack of effect and consideration of the attribute in it. The dialecticians divided it into four categories: First: that its lack of effect appears in the ruling absolutely. Second: lack of effect in that original specifically. Third: lack of effect of the qualified and eliminating a qualification from it, claiming the absolute absolutely. Fourth: lack of effect of this qualified in the disputed case and eliminating the qualification in the locus of dispute. Example of the first, called lack of effect in the attribute: as if it is said to the Hanafis: Fajr is not shortened, so its call to prayer is not advanced before time like Maghrib. It is replied that the lack of shortening is incidental, having no suitability for advancing the call, so it is not considered at all by agreement. Second: lack of appearance of effect in that original, called lack of effect in the original: as if it is said to the Shafi'is: In selling an absent object, it is a commodity not seen, so its sale is invalid like a bird in the air, its sale is invalid. It is replied that inability to deliver is independent in effect in the original, which is the bird in the air, so being unseen has no bearing. Third: lack of effect of the qualification, called lack of effect in the ruling: as if the Hanafis say about apostates: they are polytheists who destroyed property in dar al-harb, so they are not liable if they become Muslims like other polytheists who are not liable for what they destroyed after Islam. It is replied that dar al-harb has no effect on the negation of liability because according to you Hanafis there is absolute negation for apostates who destroyed then became Muslims. Fourth: lack of consideration by eliminating the qualification in the locus of dispute, called lack of effect in the branch: as if it is said to the Shafi'is: she married herself to a non-suitable match, so his marriage is rejected like the guardian marrying a minor to a non-suitable match, it is not valid. It is replied that non-suitable match has no effect according to you Shafi'is because the dispute between us is absolute regarding the validity of marriage with women's expressions whether suitable or not; according to you it is invalid, according to us it is valid. They said: the first and third revert to rejection of 'illiyyah concerning what the proponent claimed as 'illa, for he claimed 'illiyyah of this qualified and it has been rejected either absolutely or by eliminating the qualification. The second and fourth revert to opposition in the original, for they involve presenting another 'illa which is the absolute. In al-Tahrir: rather the fourth reverts to the third, the return is apparent because when he eliminates the qualification he has rejected the 'illiyyah of the qualified as in the third, but it can be considered opposition because if he eliminates in this ruling, the absolute remains as 'illa, so it involves presenting another 'illa. But that is not the aim of the objector; his aim is to object to the opponent's 'illa, not to prove another 'illa. Hence the author said: it is more appropriate. It was objected to what they said: there is a difference between rejecting the 'illiyyah by indicating it and establishing evidence for its absence. Here there is a claim of lack of effect, so it is an invalidation, not a rejection. Likewise, there is a difference between presenting what possibly is the 'illa and presenting what is definitely the 'illa. Opposition is the former; in the latter it necessitates absolute invalidation of the 'illa. I say: perhaps the above reversion is to avoid usurping the position of the proponent in the debate itself; otherwise every premise that admits rejection, even figuratively, can be indicated to be false from the start, so here too it can, and we do not reject it, but we revert to avoid usurpation. Understand. There is an apparent problem: his statement 'every premise admits rejection etc.' is rejected: rejection suffices with possibility, while invalidation requires completing the premises, rarely possible in the chain. Also, you know that prohibiting usurpation of the position for invalidating a premise of the proof is arbitrary; rather it prevents a type of manifesting truth. Rather, this type is better than rejection, because with rejection the proponent still hopes to complete his speech by proving the rejected premise, but after establishing evidence of its falsity, his hope is cut off from the root. Then there is disagreement about the incidental qualification (al-qayd al-tardi). The chosen view is that it is rejected if the proponent acknowledges its being incidental, then it is inappropriate to include it in the evidence because he is lying by including it due to his acknowledgement. It was said: it is not rejected because the purpose of the 'illa is to entail the ruling, and if a part entails the ruling (i.e., the absolute upon qualification), then the whole (qualified by the incidental) entails necessarily, so it does not harm the objective. I say: the other part may be specified by specification, so the qualification disrupts the entailment apparently, because it may be that the qualification is something excluded, leading eventually to increased clarification. So ponder. Otherwise, i.e., if he did not acknowledge its being incidental, it is not rejected, and it is permissible to qualify with it because it may have a valid purpose: repelling a break (naqd) from the 'illa to the 'broken break' (al-naqd al-maksur), which is harder for the objector, perhaps his mind does not turn to it. I say: its being a valid purpose in debate is questionable because it is disguise contrary to manifesting truth. The fourth type of objections to the 'illa of the original (specific to the Shafi'is) concerns suitability (munasaba), as said in Sharh al-Mukhtasar: it is not clear why it is specific to Shafi'is, for suitability of the 'illa is a condition for all, but Shafi'is are satisfied with it alone, while our scholars also require effect (athar). So these objections should be accepted by all, except that the Hanafis did not mention them for what the author will mention: they are four: first, impugning suitability by presenting a predominant or equal harm based on the disruption of suitability, so it is only effective against those who accept disruption. Its answer: preferring the benefit to the harm in general, because were it not predominant, adherence to the invalid would follow, which is the ruling of the original. One might say: the ruling of the original is agreed upon even after evidence, so it must contain a benefit, but it does not entail the predominance of the proponent's benefit; that benefit could be other than it, so general indication is insufficient. Rather the proponent's benefit must be specifically predominant over the objector's harm. Likewise, its answer also: preferring the benefit over the harm in detail due to specific preponderances, such as the Shafi'is' argument for proving the option of the session: 'A cause of rescission is found in the session, namely repelling harm from the seller, so rescission is established for him.' It is countered by an equal harm: the other party, the buyer, suffers harm by losing ownership of the sold item. It is answered that the buyer acquires a benefit by owning the commodity, while the seller repels harm by losing ownership for an unsatisfactory price, and repelling harm is more important than acquiring benefit. But do not overlook that the sold item entered the buyer's ownership by the contract, so the buyer's harm in losing what he owned without his consent is more severe, while the seller only wants to return ownership after loss; he wants to acquire benefit by harming another. Understand. And similar to the issue of devoting oneself to supererogatory worship is better.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is an advanced discussion of qiyas (analogy) in Hanafi usul al-fiqh, specifically about the types of objections (i'tiradat) that can be raised against the 'illa (effective cause) of the original case (asl). The author, from the commentary tradition on Musallam al-Thubut, explains that the Hanafis did not treat these objections as independent because they ultimately revert to either man' (rejection) or mu'arada (opposition). The text then presents a four-fold classification by the dialecticians (jadalists) regarding the notion of 'adam al-ta'thir (lack of effect) of the descriptive attribute (wasf). The first type is absolute lack of effect in the ruling (e.g., Fajr prayer not being shortened has no bearing on advancing the adhan). The second is lack of effect in that particular original case (e.g., an unseen commodity is not analogous to a bird in the air because inability to deliver is the real cause). The third is lack of effect of a qualification (qayd) by eliminating it and claiming the absolute (e.g., the condition of dar al-harb is irrelevant for indemnity after Islam). The fourth is lack of effect of the qualification specifically in the disputed branch (e.g., the spouse's suitability is irrelevant when women contract marriage). After examples, the text analyzes which types revert to man' and which to mu'arada. The author then clarifies a subtle point: the fourth reverts to the third, not to mu'arada, because eliminating the qualification is like rejecting 'illiyyah. A further objection is raised about the difference between rejecting 'illiyyah and proving its invalidity. The discussion then turns to the 'incidental qualification' (al-qayd al-tardi): if the proponent admits it is incidental, it is rejected; otherwise it may be allowed but could be a form of disguise (talbis). Finally, the text introduces a fourth type of objection specific to the Shafi'is regarding munasaba (suitability). Here, the Hanafi position is that suitability alone is insufficient; effect (athar) is also required. The author gives an example from the option of the session (khiyar al-majlis), weighing harms and benefits, and then mentions the preference for supererogatory worship.

Key Terms
عدم التأثير ('adam al-ta'thir): Lack of effect of the attribute in the ruling; a type of objection to the 'illa.
العلة (al-'illa): The effective cause in qiyas that is used to extend the ruling from the original case to the branch.
المنع (al-man'): Rejection of the 'illiyyah of the attribute claimed by the proponent.
المعارضة (al-mu'arada): Opposition by presenting an alternative 'illa that contradicts the proponent's claim.
القيد الطردي (al-qayd al-tardi): An incidental qualification that has no legal effect; its inclusion is questioned.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which types of 'adam al-ta'thir revert to man' (rejection) of the 'illiyyah?
A) The first and second
B) The first and third
C) The second and fourth
D) The third and fourth

Key Takeaway
The four types of 'adam al-ta'thir objections are classified by whether the lack of effect is absolute, original-specific, qualification-specific, or branch-specific, and they revert to either man' or mu'arada based on the objector's intent.

Al-Qiyas — Analogical Reasoning
intermediate
8 min
Ruling on Tardi Qualifications in 'Illah
Discussing whether a tardi qualification is rejected or accepted, and comparing objection vs invalidation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on tardi qualifications in qiyas and apply the distinction between objection (man') and invalidation (ibtal).

Core Idea
A tardi qualification is rejected only if the arguer admits its irrelevance; otherwise it may serve a clarifying purpose.

This passage from Fawatih al-Rahamut tackles a key logical tool in qiyas: the tardi qualification (qayd tardi). A tardi qualification is an extra condition in the 'illah that does not affect the ruling but is added to avoid a counterexample. There is disagreement among usulis on whether such a qualification invalidates the 'illah. The passage also clarifies the difference between a simple objection (man') and full invalidation (ibtal), which affects how a mujtahid defends his argument.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish between man' and ibtal
Understand that man' only alleges the premise is unproven (sufficient probability), whereas ibtal requires disproving the premise fully.
2
Identify the tardi qualification
Recognize it as a non-effective condition in the 'illah that does not contribute to the ruling but prevents a counterexample.
3
State the chosen (mukhtar) position
The chosen view is that if the arguer admits the qualification is tardi, it is rejected because he would be lying by including an irrelevant element.
4
Outline the opposing view
Some scholars hold it is not rejected because the 'illah still entails the ruling; the unqualified part already entails, so the whole does as well.
5
Analyze the author's caution
The author notes that the other part of the 'illah might be specified by an exception, which could disrupt entailment, but the tardi qualification can also serve to clarify and explain.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ممنوع : فإن المنع يكفي فيه الاحتمال والإبطال لا بد فيه من إتمام المقدمات وقلما يتيسر في السند، وأيضاً : أنت قد عرفت إن امتناع غصب المنصب لإبطال مقدمة الدليل تحكم بل منع له عن نوع من أنواع إظهار الصواب، بل هذا النوع أولى من المنع، فإن في المنع يبقى للمستدل طمع إتمام كلامه بإثبات المقدمة الممنوعة، وبعد قيام الدليل على بطلانها انقطع طمعه من الأصل (ثم) اختلف في القيد الطردي، والمختار أن القيد الطردي مردود إن العلة اعترف المستدل بطرديته فلا يليق إيراده في الاستدلال لأنه كاذب حينئذ في جعله من بإقراره بالطردية وقيل : (لا يردّ لأن الغرض من العلة (استلزام الحكم والجزء إذا استلزم) الحكم وهو المطلق عند القيد (فالكل) وهو المقيد بالقيد الطردي (مستلزم قطعاً) فلا يضر بالمقصود أقول) قد يكون الجزء الآخر مخصصاً) بتخصيص (فيخل) التقييد (بالاستلزام ظاهراً) لجواز أن يكون المقيد بهذا القيد مما خص منه فيؤدي بالآخرة إلى زيادة بيان وإيضاح فتأمل فيه
Objection: For objection, probability suffices, but invalidation requires completion of premises and is rarely feasible regarding the chain. Also, you have known that preventing the usurpation of the position to invalidate a premise of the proof is arbitrary; rather, it is an objection of a type that manifests correctness. Indeed, this type is more appropriate than objection, because in objection the arguer retains hope of completing his speech by proving the objected premise, but after establishing proof of its falsehood, his hope is cut off from the root. Then, there is disagreement about the tardi qualification. The chosen view is that the tardi qualification is rejected. If the arguer admits its tardiness, then it is not befitting to present it in inference because he would then be lying in making it part of the cause due to his admission of its tardiness. And it is said: It is not rejected because the purpose of the cause is entailment of the ruling, and the part if it entails the ruling—which is the absolute at the time of the qualification—then the whole, which is qualified by the tardi qualification, necessarily entails, so it does not harm the objective. I say: It may be that the other part is specified by specification, so the qualification disrupts the entailment apparently, because it is possible that what is qualified by this qualification is among what has been specified, and ultimately it leads to increased clarification and explanation, so ponder upon it.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage discusses two related issues. First, it distinguishes between a simple objection (man‘) and invalidation (ibtāl). In objection, the challenger merely claims the premise is unproven, which is easier; invalidation requires disproving the premise, which is harder. The text argues that preventing the arguer from using a premise to invalidate the proof is arbitrary; instead, the opponent should object (man‘) or allow the arguer to proceed. Then, it addresses the tardi qualification (qayd ṭardī), i.e., a qualification in the ‘illah that has no effect on the ruling but is added to avoid a counterexample. There is disagreement: the chosen view (al-mukhtār) is that if the arguer admits the qualification is tardi, then it is rejected because he would be lying by including something irrelevant. Others argue it is not rejected because the essential purpose of the ‘illah is to entail the ruling; since the unqualified part already entails the ruling, the whole also entails it, so the tardi addition does not harm. The author then notes that the other part might be specified by an exception, which could disrupt the entailment, but ultimately the tardi qualification may serve to clarify and explain. This is a typical Hanafi usul discussion about the conditions of the ‘illah in qiyās.

Key Terms
القيد الطردي (al-qayd al-ṭardī): A qualification in the ‘illah that has no effect on the ruling but is added to avoid a counterexample.
المنع (al-man‘): An objection to a premise by claiming it is unproven, without disproving it.
الإبطال (al-ibṭāl): Invalidation of a premise by disproving it, requiring completion of premises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the chosen view in this passage, when is a tardi qualification (qayd ṭardī) rejected?
A) Whenever it appears in the ‘illah.
B) Only if the arguer admits that it is tardi.
C) Only if it contradicts the ruling.
D) It is never rejected.

Key Takeaway
A tardi qualification is rejected only when the arguer admits its irrelevance; otherwise it may be retained for clarification.
